Denial of Creator and Prophethood: The Proper Starting Point
Al-Nasafi explains that denying the Creator entails denying prophethood and outlines necessary preliminary arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proper order of argument in kalam when discussing prophethood, and why one must first establish the Creator and the epistemic value of reports before addressing prophethood.

Core Idea
One cannot argue for prophethood without first proving the Creator and the validity of reports as knowledge.

In this passage, Al-Nasafi identifies two groups who deny prophethood and explains the correct starting point for debate with each. He shows that theological argument must proceed in a logical order: foundational issues before derivative ones. This lesson will help you appreciate the methodical approach of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through.
Notice the concise style of Al-Nasafi.
2
Identify the two categories of opponents
Find where Al-Nasafi distinguishes between those who deny the Creator and those who deny prophethood while affirming the Creator.
The second group includes the Samaniyya and Barahima.
3
Understand why each requires a different starting point
For deniers of the Creator, the discussion must first establish the Creator. For deniers of prophethood based on rejection of reports, the discussion must first establish reports as valid knowledge.
This shows logical priority.
4
See the logical necessity: no sender, no messenger
Al-Nasafi states that affirming a messenger without a sender is impossible. This is the reason for the order.
This is a key conceptual point.
5
Reflect on the methodological approach
Consider how this methodical order strengthens theological argument by addressing foundations first.
This is a hallmark of Sunni kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أنكر الصانع فهو منكر للرسالة فأما من أنكر الصانع فلا شك أنه منكر للرسالة إذ القول بالرسول ولا مرسل محالاً. إلا أن الكلام معهم يقع في إثبات الصانع. وكذا من أنكر الرسالة من السمنية والبراهمة لما أن الخبر ليس من أسباب المعارف فلا يفيد خبر الرسول معنى فلا فائدة في إرساله. لا نتكلم معه في هذه المسألة ابتداء بل نتكلم في إثبات كون الخبر سببًا من أسباب المعارف، وقد مر في أول الكتاب ما يقع به [الفنية؟] بحمد الله تعالى.
Whoever denies the Creator is a denier of prophethood. As for the one who denies the Creator, there is no doubt that he is a denier of prophethood, because affirming a messenger without a sender is impossible. However, the discussion with them falls under establishing the Creator. And likewise, whoever denies prophethood among the Samaniyya and Barahima because the report (al-khabar) is not among the causes of knowledge, so the report of the messenger conveys no meaning, thus there is no benefit in sending him. We do not speak with him about this issue initially, but we speak about establishing that the report is a cause of knowledge, and it has already passed in the beginning of the book what suffices [or: is needed] by the praise of God.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage identifies two categories of opponents in the discussion of prophethood: (1) those who deny the Creator, and (2) those who affirm the Creator but deny prophethood, such as the Samaniyya and Barahima, because they reject that reports (al-khabar) can convey knowledge. Al-Nasafi explains the proper approach for each. For the first category, the discussion must first focus on proving the existence of the Creator, since logically a messenger cannot exist without a sender. For the second category, the initial discussion is not about prophethood itself but about establishing the epistemic value of reports as a valid source of knowledge, which was already addressed earlier in the book. This shows the methodical order of argument in kalam: foundational beliefs must be established before derivative ones.

Key Terms
الصانع
الرسالة
الرسول
السمنية
البراهمة
الخبر
أسباب المعارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can directly argue for prophethood without first establishing the existence of the Creator or the validity of reports.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that without the foundation of the Creator, prophethood is meaningless, and without reports as knowledge, prophethood's message is invalid. Thus, proper argumentation must address these foundations first.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, why is it impossible to speak about prophethood with someone who denies the Creator?
A) Because they reject reports.
B) Because affirming a messenger without a sender is impossible.
C) Because they deny the intellect.
D) Because they are not rational.

Key Takeaway
The proper method of kalam requires establishing foundational beliefs (Creator, validity of reports) before addressing derivative issues like prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever tried to convince someone about Islam without first ensuring they believe in God? How did that go, and what does this passage teach you about the proper order of discussion?
?
When you learn about Islam, do you sometimes skip foundational beliefs like tawheed and jump to secondary issues? What would it look like to start with the Creator every time?
?
The Samaniyya and Barahima rejected prophethood because they doubted the reliability of reports. In your own life, do you trust transmitted knowledge (e.g., from scholars, parents) or are you skeptical? How does your attitude affect your relationship with the Quran and Sunnah?

Reason's Obstacles and the Necessity of Revelation
Describes obstacles to rational inquiry and argues revelation is a necessary facilitator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine revelation is necessary to assist reason in overcoming its inherent obstacles.

Core Idea
Revelation is not a substitute for reason but its indispensable helper.

Imam Nasafi begins by acknowledging the difficulties that face the rational seeker of truth. Doubts, desires, and life's preoccupations can hinder clear thought. He then argues that divine revelation is the necessary assistant that guides and lightens the burden of reason. This passage establishes the complementary relationship between reason and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first obstacles, then the need for a helper, then the role of revelation.
Pay attention to the terms used for obstacles (shubahāt, amānī, shahawāt).
2
Identify the obstacles listed
List the obstacles that the author mentions as hindrances to rational inquiry. How do these obstacles affect the seeker?
The passage mentions doubts, wishes/desires, worries, pains, and the subtlety of theoretical matters.
3
Understand revelation's role
The author says revelation is an assistant (muʿāwin). How does revelation help? Does it replace reason or support it?
Revelation alerts the intellect and guides its method, making the task easier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صاحبه، وربما تعارضه الشبهات، وتصده عن حقيقة النظر الأماني والشهوات، وتمنعه عن الإحاطة بكل دقيق من الأمور وجليل، اعتراء الهموم وأسباب شغل القلب على صاحبه، وترادف الآلام والأوجاع عليه، وتشغله عن الاستقصاء في البحث والتأمل مع ما في المطالب النظرية من الغموض والدقة فلابد من معاون له إياه ليصل باتصاله به إلى ما يجب الوصول إليه. وذلك هو الوحي السماوي، والله الموفق. على أن العقول لو كان في جبلتها إمكان الوصول إلى جميع مصالح الدارين إلا أن ذلك لن يكون إلا بآداب الفكر، ونص مطية الخاطر، والانكباب على النظر الدائم والبحث الكامل، ويلحقه بذلك مشقة عظيمة، وتلزمه مؤنة كثيرة. ثم إنه إذا نبه على ذلك بالسمع وأرشد إلى كيفية استخراج ما يرومه بعقله تنبه لذلك، وسهل عليه الأمر وخفت له المؤنة.
…its companion, and perhaps doubts oppose him, and wishes and desires turn him away from the reality of reflection, and prevent him from encompassing every fine and great matter, the occurrence of worries and causes of the heart's preoccupation upon its companion, and the succession of pains and aches upon him, and they occupy him from thorough investigation and contemplation along with what is in theoretical inquiries of obscurity and subtlety. So he must have an assistant for him to reach, by his connection with it, what he must reach. That is divine revelation, and Allah is the Grantor of success. Moreover, even if the intellects had in their innate disposition the ability to reach all the benefits of the two abodes, that would not be except through the disciplines of thought, the exertion of the steed of the mind, and devotion to constant reflection and complete investigation, and great hardship attaches to him thereby, and much burden is necessary for him. Then, if he is alerted to that by revelation (al-samʿ) and guided to the method of extracting what he seeks with his intellect, he becomes alert to it, and the matter becomes easy for him, and the burden becomes light for him.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage describes the difficulties faced by a rational inquirer and argues that divine revelation is necessary to overcome them. It first lists obstacles: doubts (shubahāt), desires (amānī), preoccupations, pains, and the inherent subtlety of theoretical matters. These make it impossible to reach truth without assistance. The required assistant is divine revelation (al-waḥy al-samāwī). The author then acknowledges that even if reason could in principle attain all benefits, it would require immense effort and hardship. Revelation eases this burden by alerting the mind and guiding its method. Thus, revelation is presented as both a necessary helper and a facilitator that lightens the task of rational inquiry.

Key Terms
شبهات
شهوات
وحي سماوي
عقل
نظر
مؤنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that revelation is only for those without intellect, or that it contradicts reason.
Correction: The author shows that revelation assists reason and lightens its burden, implying a complementary relationship.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the main role of divine revelation in relation to reason?
A) It replaces reason entirely.
B) It assists reason and eases its hardships.
C) It contradicts reason.
D) It is only for those without intellect.

Key Takeaway
Revelation assists reason by alerting it and easing its burden, enabling it to reach truths it could not attain alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a doubt or a strong desire that pulls you away from seeking the truth, do you instinctively turn to revelation as your helper, or do you rely on your own reasoning? What would change if you made a habit of seeking divine guidance in such moments?
?
The passage says that even if your intellect could reach all truths, it would require immense hardship without revelation. Think of a time when you struggled to understand a theological issue. Did you seek Allah's help through the Qur'an and Sunnah to ease that struggle? What is one specific way you can invite revelation to 'alert' and 'guide' your thinking this week?
?
Consider the obstacles mentioned: doubts, desires, worries, pains. Which one do you find most often blocks your path to truth? How can you use the teachings of revelation to overcome that specific obstacle, starting today?

Understanding Lenient vs. Decisive Proofs
Al-Nasafi notes that the preceding argument was lenient; the true proofs are earlier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author presents a lenient argument before the decisive proofs and how this reveals theological methodology.

Core Idea
Methodology matters: lenient arguments prepare the mind; decisive proofs close the case.

In this brief methodological note, Imam al-Nasafi clarifies the purpose of the preceding discussion. He tells us outright that he has been using a lenient approach — not the strongest argument. The real, decisive proofs against the sufficiency of reason alone have already been presented earlier. This is a deliberate teaching strategy that we must recognize to avoid misunderstanding his argumentative structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its English translation. Notice the key term 'مساهلة' and the contrast with 'دلائل'.
The passage is short but packed with methodological significance.
2
Understand 'مساهلة' (leniency)
Consider what it means for an argument to be 'lenient'. It suggests concessive or gentle reasoning, not the most rigorous proof.
The author deliberately uses this approach before revealing the stronger proofs.
3
Recognize the author's main argument
The passage points out that the real proofs against rational sufficiency were given earlier. This tells us where to look for the author's definitive position.
Don't mistake the lenient argument for the final word.
4
Reflect on pedagogical strategy
Ask yourself: Why would a scholar present a lenient argument first? Consider the benefit of easing the reader into a complex topic before delivering the stronger case.
This reflects a common teaching method in Islamic theological works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هذا كله منا على طريق المساهلة، فأما دلائل بطلان القول بوقوع الغنية بمجرد العقل فقد سبق ذكرها بعون الله وتوفيقه.
Then all this from us is by way of leniency. As for the proofs of the invalidity of the statement that sufficiency occurs by reason alone, they have already been mentioned with the help of Allah and His success.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This short passage serves as a methodological note. The author clarifies that the preceding arguments were a lenient approach. The decisive proofs that reason alone is insufficient were already presented earlier in the work. This signals that the real refutation is not this concessive argument but the earlier evidences.

Key Terms
مساهلة
دلائل
غنية
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The preceding argument is the author’s main proof.
Correction: The author explicitly says it was a lenient approach; the decisive proofs were given earlier.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'على طريق المساهلة'?
A) That the argument is the strongest.
B) That it is a lenient approach.
C) That it is a philosophical approach.
D) That it is a new argument.

Key Takeaway
The author uses a lenient argument as a pedagogical stepping stone, but the real, decisive proofs against rational sufficiency are the earlier ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you share your faith with others, do you ever present softer arguments to make it easier for them, like the author's lenient approach? How does knowing that stronger proofs exist (as the author mentions) affect your honesty in presenting the truth? Will you commit to always pointing others toward the decisive arguments, even if they are harder?
?
The author admits his preceding argument is lenient. In your own studies, do you sometimes accept a weak argument because it feels comfortable? Are you willing to set aside easy explanations and search for the decisive proofs that the author says are already there? What specific step will you take today to dig deeper into your understanding of this issue?
?
You are reading a text where the author humbly says 'this is lenient.' How does that challenge your tendency to trust your own reasoning as sufficient? In what area of your life are you relying on 'reason alone' instead of seeking divine guidance? What will you change today to depend more on Allah's revealed truth than on your own intellect?

The Foolishness of Command Without Benefit
Uses qiyas shahid-ghaib to prove divine command without benefit is foolish.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidite kalam uses qiyas shahid-ghaib to argue that divine commands without benefit are impossible, and why this leads to a dilemma.

Core Idea
Qiyas shahid-ghaib forces consistency: what is foolish in this world cannot be attributed to God.

This passage presents a classic dialectical proof that uses analogy from the witnessed world to the unseen. The author poses a series of pointed questions that corner the opponent, showing that denying the principle leads to absurd or blasphemous conclusions. Follow the chain of reasoning carefully to see how kalam theologians establish that God's commands must have wisdom and benefit.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial question
Read the first sentence carefully. The author asks whether commanding without benefit and prohibiting without harm is foolish in the witnessed world. This sets up the analogy.
The term 'safah' (foolishness) is key.
2
Trace the 'No' branch
If the opponent says such action is not foolish in this world, then they contradict themselves when they call it foolish in the unseen. The argument shows inconsistency.
This forces the opponent to accept the initial premise.
3
Trace the 'Yes' branch: Good or Evil?
If the opponent admits it is foolish, the next question is whether it is good or evil. Claiming it is good undermines their intellect; claiming it is evil leads to further questions.
Note how the argument narrows down the options.
4
See the trap: Permissible, Obligatory, or Forbidden?
If evil, then classify it. If obligatory or permissible, ask who made it so. If they say God, then God commands evil—a blasphemous conclusion. The argument cuts off here, but the intended conclusion is clear.
The passage is incomplete but the logical endpoint is that such attribution is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: هل الأمر بما لا نفع للآمر بفعله والنهي عما لا ضرر على الناهي بفعله سفه في الشاهد أم لا؟ فإن قال: لا، أبطل كلامه لأن جعل ذلك في الغائب سفة، لدلالة كون مثله سفها في الشاهد. وإن قال نعم هو سفه . قيل: أحسن هو أم قبيح؟ فإن قال: هو حسن عرف العقلاء مبلغ عقله حيث زعم أن السفه حسن وإن قال: هو قبيح. قيل: أمباح أم محظور أم واجب؟ فإن قال بالوجوب أو الإباحة. فقيل: من الذي أوجبه عليه أو أباحه له؟ فإن قال: هو الله تعالى، فقد أعظم القول، حيث زعم أن الله تعالى يبيح القبيح أو يوجبه، على أنه مع بطلانه ترك لمذهبه لأن الإيجاب أو الإباحة هو
Then we say: Is commanding what has no benefit for the commander by doing it and prohibiting what has no harm for the prohibitor by doing it foolish in the witnessed world or not? If he says: No, he contradicts his own statement because making that in the unseen foolishness, due to the indication that its like is foolishness in the witnessed. And if he says: Yes, it is foolish. It is said: Is it good or evil? If he says: It is good, the intelligent recognize the measure of his intellect, since he claims that foolishness is good. And if he says: It is evil. It is said: Is it permissible, forbidden, or obligatory? If he says obligatory or permissible. Then it is said: Who made it obligatory upon him or permissible for him? If he says: It is Allah Almighty, then he has uttered a grave statement, since he claims that Allah Almighty permits evil or makes it obligatory, although besides its invalidity, he has abandoned his doctrine because the obligation or permissibility is...
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument using the principle of qiyas (analogy) from the witnessed world (shahid) to the unseen (ghaib). The author asks whether commanding without benefit and prohibiting without harm is foolish in this world. If the opponent says no, he refutes himself because consistency requires applying the same judgment to God. If he says yes, then the argument progresses: if he says such an act is good, his intellect is questioned; if evil, then he must classify it as permissible, forbidden, or obligatory. If he says obligatory or permissible, he is asked who made it so. If he answers God, that implies God commands evil, which is a serious blasphemy. The argument cuts off but the intended conclusion is clear.

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
سفه
شاهد
غائب
حسن
قبيح
إباحة
وجوب
محظور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Analogy from the visible world to God is invalid in theological reasoning.
Correction: This passage shows that qiyas shahid-ghaib can be used to expose contradictions in the opponent's position, even if not directly proving God's attributes. It is a valid dialectical tool.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the dialectical argument starting with 'ثم نقول'?
A) To prove that God cannot act foolishly by analogy with human agents.
B) To show that prophets are exempt from moral evaluation.
C) To argue that divine commands are arbitrary.
D) To demonstrate that foolishness is subjective.

Key Takeaway
The kalam argument uses a forced-choice structure to show that denying benefit in divine commands leads to absurd or blasphemous consequences, thereby proving that God's actions are purposeful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every command from God has a specific benefit for you, would you still hesitate to obey? What does your hesitation reveal about what you actually believe?
?
The argument shows that denying benefit leads to absurdity. In your daily life, where do you act as if a divine command is purposeless or outdated? Will you confront that area today?
?
Suppose you had to defend the wisdom of a divine command you personally struggle with. Could you do so without falling into the foolishness described here? What would your answer be?

The Preferred View and Two Prophethood Topics
Imam Nasafi states the preferred view and outlines the subsequent discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verified Maturidi scholars prefer the first view regarding miracles and how the discussion of prophethood is divided into two levels: general and specific.

Core Idea
Prophethood is proven first in general, then in specific messengers.

This short passage serves as a bridge. After settling the debate on miracles, Imam Nasafi identifies the verified position and then outlines the next phase of inquiry: first proving prophethood historically, then verifying individual messengers. This structure shows the logical progression from general evidence to specific claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise, transitional style.
2
Identify the preferred view
The passage states that the verified scholars (al-muḥaqqiqūn) are upon the first view. Recall from previous lessons that this is the Maturidi argument based on wisdom.
This anchors the discussion as authoritative.
3
List the two topics
The author then lists two topics: (1) establishing that prophethood (risāla) in general has certainly existed in past times, and (2) establishing the prophethood of specific messengers (ba‘ḍ al-rusul) by specification (ta‘yīn).
This is a preview of the upcoming two chapters.
4
Reflect on the logical sequence
Why this order? First the general proof that prophethood is a real phenomenon, then the identification of which individuals are true prophets. This is a classic theological method.
It mirrors the pattern of proving a genus then a species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمحققون منهم على الوجه الأول، والله الموفق. وإذا علم هذا فبعد ذلك يحتاج إلى التكلم في موضعين: أحدهما في أن الرسالة قد ثبت في الجملة لا محالة فيما مضى من الأزمنة. والثاني : في إثبات رسالة بعض الرسل على طريق التعيين. ونتكلم في الفصلين جميعًا بما يوجب صحة ما ندعي في كل فصل – إن شاء الله تعالى.
And the verified scholars among them are upon the first view, and God is the grantor of success. And when this is known, then after that there is need to speak on two topics: the first, that prophethood has been established in general without doubt in past times; and the second, establishing the prophethood of some messengers by way of specification. And we will speak on both chapters with what necessitates the correctness of what we claim in each chapter – God willing.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion on the impossibility of miracles for false prophets by noting that the verified scholars (al-muḥaqqiqūn) among the mutakallimun adopt the first view (i.e., the Maturidi argument based on wisdom). The author then transitions to the next part of the work, listing two topics to be addressed: first, establishing that prophethood in general (al-risālah fī al-jumlah) has certainly existed in past times; second, establishing the prophethood of specific messengers (ba‘ḍ al-rusul) through explicit designation. The author expresses intention to discuss both chapters with arguments that prove the correctness of the claims made.

Key Terms

الوجه الأول
رسالة
جملة
تعيين
فصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the author rejects the other views as false.
Correction: He merely states the preferred view among the verified scholars, implying that other views are less strong but not necessarily invalid.

Checkpoint
What are the two topics that the author announces will be discussed next?
A) The types of miracles and the nature of God
B) Establishing prophethood in general and establishing the prophethood of specific messengers
C) The definition of prophethood and the proof of revelation
D) The refutation of the opponents and the proofs of the Ash'arites

Key Takeaway
This passage concludes the debate on miracles and outlines the next two steps: first proving that prophethood in general has occurred, then identifying specific true prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you personally settled in your heart that prophethood is a historical fact, or do you still harbor doubts that keep you from fully trusting the messengers? What would it take for you to be certain like the 'verified scholars'?
?
The author humbly says 'God willing' before discussing proofs. When you study matters of faith, do you approach them with a sense of reliance on God's guidance, or do you rely only on your own intellect? How does that affect your conviction?
?
If someone asked you to name the specific messengers you believe in and why, could you give a reasoned answer, or would you rely on blind acceptance? What step will you take today to move from general belief to specific conviction?

Knowledge from Divine Revelation
Argument that various arts come from divine revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that certain specialized knowledge (e.g., reproduction, magic, warfare) is not derivable from sensory experience or reason, and therefore must come from divine revelation.

Core Idea
Some knowledge is only possible through direct instruction from God.

How did humans learn the secrets of magic, warfare, and child-rearing? The author of our passage argues that such knowledge cannot be explained by human senses or intellect alone. Instead, it points directly to a divine source: revelation to chosen prophets. Let's explore this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the original Arabic and the literal translation. Notice the list of examples and the logical structure: if not senses/reason, then revelation.
The term 'tawqīf' is key.
2
Identify the types of knowledge listed
The passage enumerates several areas: reproduction causes, child-rearing, animal nutrition, magic, alchemy, warfare. Write them down and consider: can these be learned by observation alone?
3
Understand the argumentative structure
The author asserts that these cannot be attained by senses or reason. He then concludes they must be 'tawqīf' from God, transmitted via revelation to prophets. Trace the logical flow.
This is a classic kalam argument from impossibility.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Why does this matter? It establishes the necessity of revelation and prophethood. Without divine instruction, humans would lack these crucial arts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا العلم بوجوه أسباب التناسل، والعلم بكيفية تربية الصغار والعلم بتدابير أغذية ما ليس له نطق. وكذا العلم بالسحر وتغيير جواهر الأشياء بأنواع المعالجات. وعلوم محاربة الأعداء. فكان ثبوت العلم بهذه الأشياء مع استنساخ حصول ذلك بشيء من الحواس أو العقل، دليلاً أن العلم بها كلها توقيف من عند العليم الخبير الذي لا يعزب عنه شيء ولا يخفى عليه خافية، وذلك يكون بالوحي إلى من اصطفاه لرسالته والله الموفق.
And likewise knowledge of the aspects of the causes of reproduction, and knowledge of the method of raising children, and knowledge of the management of nutrition for those without speech. And likewise knowledge of magic and changing the essences of things through various kinds of treatments. And the sciences of warfare against enemies. Then the establishment of knowledge of these things, while it is impossible that this occurs through any of the senses or the intellect, is evidence that knowledge of all of them is a tawqīf (divine instruction) from the All-Knowing, the All-Aware, from whom nothing escapes and no hidden thing is concealed, and that occurs through revelation to whom He has chosen for His message. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents an argument that certain kinds of knowledge — such as the causes of reproduction, child-rearing, animal nutrition, magic, alchemy, and military science — cannot be acquired through the senses or reason alone. Therefore, their existence among humans is evidence that they must have been taught by God, the All-Knowing, through revelation to His chosen prophets. The author uses the term 'tawqīf' to indicate that this knowledge is directly imparted by God, not derived through human effort. The conclusion is that such knowledge comes from divine revelation, specifically to the prophets.

Correction: The author argues such knowledge could not be attained by senses or reason, so it must have been taught directly by God via revelation.

Key Takeaway
The existence of certain specialized knowledge that humans could not have deduced on their own points to divine revelation as its source.

How Tawatur Transmits Miracles with Certainty
The author explains how miracles are transmitted through tawatur to later generations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how mutawātir reports make the miracles of prophets certain for later generations, because collusion on a lie is impossible among diverse people.

Core Idea
Tawatur turns miracles into certain knowledge, as if you saw them yourself.

How can we be sure that miracles really happened centuries ago? The author explains that direct witnesses see them, but later generations rely on mutawātir reports—reports passed by so many independent chains that falsehood becomes impossible. This passage unpacks the reasoning behind that certainty, using logic and even pre-Islamic poetry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a feel for the argument and the poetic citations.
2
Identify the two modes of transmission
Notice that the author distinguishes between direct observation for contemporaries and tawatur for later generations.
3
Understand the impossibility of collusion
Focus on why the author says a large, diverse group cannot conspire on a lie: different inclinations, opinions, ambitions, and temperaments.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Examine the poetic evidence
Read the two lines of poetry quoted by the author. They reinforce the idea that widespread reports leave no room for doubt.
Poetry was often used as evidence in classical Islamic scholarship.
5
Link to necessary knowledge
The author refers to an earlier proof that tawatur yields necessary knowledge. Understand that this makes the reported miracle as certain as direct sight.
This connects to a broader epistemological framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ثبوت ذلك لأهل زمانهم بالمشاهدة ولمن بعدهم بالخبر المتواتر الذي يوجب العلم الضروري، كما أثبت ذلك بالبلدان النائية والأمم الخالية، ولا مجال للتهمة فيه لا بالفسق ولا بالانتفاع لامتناع إجماع الناس الذين لا يتصور تواطؤهم على الكذب على خبر واحد هو في نفسه كذب، لاستحالة اجتماع دواعي الكذب على أناس مختلفين جبلوا على أهواء مختلفة، وآراء مشتتة، وهمم متفرقة، وطبائع متباينة. ولهذا قال طفيل الغنوي: وجاء من الأخبار ما لا يكذب تأويني هم من الليل منصب تظاهرن حتى لم تكن لي ريبة ولم يك غما أخبروا متعقب وقال سويد بن صبغي: أتتك من الأخبار عين جلية ترادفن حتى ما لقلبك صارف وهذا ظاهر، وقد أقمنا الدلالة في أول الكتاب على كون الخبر المتواتر موجبا للعلم الضروري جاعلاً المخبر عنه كالمنظور إليه للسامع، فلا نشتغل بإعادة ذلك والله الموفق.
Then the establishment of that for the people of their time is by direct observation, and for those after them by the mutawātir report which yields necessary knowledge, just as that is established for distant lands and past nations. And there is no room for accusation in it, neither of immorality nor of self-interest, because it is impossible for all the people — who cannot be imagined to conspire together upon a lie regarding a single report that is in itself a lie — to agree, due to the impossibility of the convergence of motives for lying among diverse people who are naturally disposed to different inclinations, scattered opinions, divergent ambitions, and varying temperaments. For this reason Ṭufayl al-Ghanawī said: 'And there came reports that do not lie / They host me from the night, a place of descent / They are so numerous that I have no doubt / And no grief that they reported is followed by reproach.' And Suwayd ibn Ṣibghī said: 'There came to you from the reports a clear eye / They follow one another until your heart has no diversion.' And this is apparent, and we have established the proof at the beginning of the book that the mutawātir report yields necessary knowledge, making the reported thing for the listener like the thing seen. So we will not occupy ourselves with repeating that. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage explains how miracles are verified for later generations. Contemporaries witness them directly, but later generations receive them through mutawātir reports — reports transmitted by such a large number that collusion on a falsehood is impossible. The author argues that the impossibility of agreement on a lie arises because people have different motives and natures, so it is inconceivable that thousands would conspire on a false report. To support this, he quotes two pre-Islamic Arab poets — Ṭufayl al-Ghanawī and Suwayd ibn Ṣibghī — who express the reliability of widespread reports. The author concludes by referring to his earlier discussion in the book that tawātur yields necessary knowledge, making the reported event as certain as if one had seen it.

Key Terms
خبر متواتر
علم ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tawātur guarantees truth simply because of the large number of reporters.
Correction: The author emphasizes that the key is the diversity of reporters' motives and natures, which makes collusion impossible. Number alone is not sufficient; it is the variety that prevents conspiracy.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for a mutawātir report to be a lie?
A) Because the reporters are all prophets
B) Because the reporters are all scholars
C) Because it is impossible for a large, diverse group to collude on a falsehood
D) Because the report is written in many books

Key Takeaway
Mutawātir reports of miracles yield necessary knowledge because the diversity of reporters makes collusion on a falsehood impossible, making the event as certain as direct observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about miracles of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) like the splitting of the moon, do you treat that event with the same certainty as if you had seen it yourself? If not, what is holding you back from that conviction?

Consider the principle that diversity of reporters prevents collusion. Where in your own life do you accept information uncritically from a single source? How can you apply the standard of tawatur to build a more reliable foundation for your beliefs?
?
The author argues that tawatur makes the reported event 'like the thing seen' for the listener. If you truly internalized that certainty about the Prophet's miracles, what would change about your daily actions, your trust in the message, or your willingness to sacrifice for it?

Why Mutawatir Reports Bring Certainty: Poetic Evidence
Illustrates certainty of mutawatir reports with poetic examples and notes prior proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how poetic examples demonstrate that mutawatir reports produce necessary knowledge, as seen in the works of Tufayl al-Ghanawi and Suwayd ibn Sab'ghi.

Core Idea
Mutawatir reports stack up until doubt is impossible, like seeing it yourself.

The author uses two lines of poetry to show that when a report comes from so many independent sources that no doubt remains, it becomes like direct observation. These powerful examples illustrate why mutawatir reports yield certain knowledge, and the author refers back to an earlier proof for completeness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then its English translation. Let the words of the poets and the author's commentary settle in your mind.
The poetry is quoted as evidence; notice the key phrases about doubt and certainty.
2
Identify the two poetic examples
The passage quotes Tufayl al-Ghanawi and Suwayd ibn Sab'ghi. For each poet, note what they say about news and doubt. How do they describe the effect of multiple reports?
Tufayl uses 'they multiplied until I had no doubt'; Suwayd speaks of an 'evident eye' that leaves no turning away.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author states that this is clear and reminds that he already proved that mutawatir reports give necessary knowledge, making the reported matter like something seen. Why does he not repeat the proof here? What does this tell you about the role of examples?
The examples are meant to illustrate a principle already established; they reinforce the idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا قال طفيل الغنوي وهو يذكر موت رجل من قومه: وجاء من الأخبار ما لا يكذب تأويني هم من الليل منصب تظاهرن حتى لم تكن لي ريبة ولم يك غما أخبروا متعقب ألا نراه قال وجاء من الأخبار ما لا يكذب ثم نبه عن العلة فقال: تظاهرن حتى لم تكن لي ريبة ولم يك عما أخبروا متعقب أتتك وقال سويد بن صبغي من الأخبار عين جلية ترادفن حتى ما لقلبك صارف وهذا ظاهر، وقد أقمنا الدلالة في أول الكتاب على كون الخبر المتواتر موجبا للعلم الضروري جاعلاً المخبر عنه كالمنظور إليه للسامع، فلا نشتغل بإعادة ذلك، والله الموفق.
For this reason Tufayl al-Ghanawi said, while mentioning the death of a man from his people: 'And there came news that does not lie, they shelter me, a care from the night, descending; they multiplied until I had no doubt, and no grief informed of them is followed.' Indeed we see he said 'And there came news that does not lie,' then he alerted to the cause and said: 'they multiplied until I had no doubt, and no grief of what they informed is followed.' 'It came to you.' And Suwayd ibn Sab'ghi said: 'From the news is an evident eye, they follow one another until your heart has no turning away.' And this is clear, and we have established the proof in the beginning of the book that the mutawatir report necessitates necessary knowledge, making the reported matter like the seen object for the listener; so we do not busy ourselves with repeating that. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage uses two poetic examples to affirm the certainty of mutawatir reports. The first poet, Tufayl al-Ghanawi, describes news that does not lie, which multiplies from various sources until no doubt remains. The second poet, Suwayd ibn Sab'ghi, calls such news an 'evident eye' that accumulates until the heart has no alternative. The author then states that this is obvious, and reminds that he has already proven in the beginning of the book that the mutawatir report yields necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri), making the reported matter as if it is directly seen by the listener. Therefore, he does not need to repeat that proof. The passage thus reinforces the epistemological foundation of mutawatir reports in establishing certain knowledge.

Mutawatir reports provide necessary (certain) knowledge because their multiple independent chains eliminate doubt.

Sensory Certainty vs. Rational Inference: Nasafi's Refutation
Nasafi refutes naturalists by asserting sensory certainty defeats rational argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi prioritizes sensory immediate knowledge over rational inference in refuting the naturalists' denial of miracles.

Core Idea
Sensory certainty trumps fallible reason in matters of divine power.

In this passage, Nasafi takes on the naturalists who denied the possibility of miracles (transformation of nature). He argues that sensory evidence provides immediate, infallible knowledge, while rational arguments only yield fallible inferences. When the two conflict, the certain must be preferred. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recognize that the naturalists deny miracles (inqilāb al-ṭabīʿa) based on their own rational arguments. Nasafi counters by appealing to sensory evidence.
2
Identify the two types of knowledge
Nasafi distinguishes between sensory immediate knowledge (ʿilm ḍarūrī) and inferential knowledge (ʿilm istidlālī). Note which one he considers infallible.
3
Follow the argument: conflict resolution
Since both types cannot coexist, the infallible knowledge must be preferred. Nasafi uses this to reject the naturalists' rational argument.
He even says that even if their argument seemed rational, it would still be rejected because it is fallible.
4
Apply the lesson
Reflect on how this principle applies to other theological debates where sensory or transmitted certainty meets rational speculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حجة شبهة اعتمدوا عليها ثابتا بالحس الذي هو أوضح طريق العلم وأعلى سبل المعرفة فكان إنكارهم مردودا ، وجواز انقلاب الطبيعة ثابتا. يحققه أنهم لو اعتمدوا في ذلك على دليل يتراءى أنه معقول ولا يهتدي بالعقل إلى كيفية رده كان ذلك مردودا لما أن الدليل العقلي يوجب العلم الاستدلالي والخطأ على الاستدلالي جائز ممكن، وما أنكروه ثابت بالحس الموجب للعلم الضروري الذي لا يجوز عليه الخطأ والغلط، ولابد من رد أحدهما ورد ما يجوز عليه الخطأ والغلط أولى من رد ما لا يجوز عليه ذلك. يحققه أن معرفة ثبوت ما أنكروه ثابت بالحس فكان بطلان دليلهم بالحس، وكل دليل عرف بطلانه ضرورة كان باطلاً أو كان شبهة لا دليلاً فكيف وهم لم يبنوا إنكارهم على شبهة.
An argument: a specious argument they relied on, while [the possibility] is established by sense (al-ḥiss) which is the clearest path of knowledge and the highest means of cognition, so their denial is rejected, and the permissibility of the transformation of nature (inqilāb al-ṭabīʿa) is established. He verifies that if they relied in that on an argument that appears rational and the intellect cannot find a way to refute it, that [argument] would be rejected because rational argument necessitates inferential knowledge (al-ʿilm al-istidlālī), and error in inferential [knowledge] is possible and permissible, while what they denied is established by sense which necessitates immediate knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī) upon which error and mistake are not possible. One of the two must be rejected, and rejecting that upon which error and mistake are possible is more appropriate than rejecting that upon which that is not possible. He verifies that the knowledge of the establishment of what they denied is established by sense, so their argument is invalidated by sense, and every argument whose invalidity is known necessarily is false or is a specious argument, not a proof. So how [can it be] when they did not base their denial on a specious argument?
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the naturalists' denial of miracles (inqilāb al-ṭabīʿa). Nasafi begins by stating that the possibility of such transformations is established by sensory evidence (al-ḥiss), which is the clearest path to knowledge. He then verifies this by considering a hypothetical: even if the naturalists had a seemingly rational argument that the intellect cannot directly refute, it would still be rejected because rational argument yields only inferential knowledge (al-ʿilm al-istidlālī), which is fallible. In contrast, what they deny is established by sense, which yields immediate knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī) that is infallible. Since both cannot be maintained, we must reject the fallible one. He concludes that their argument is invalidated by sense, and any argument known to be invalid is either false or a mere specious argument (shubha). Thus their denial is baseless.

Key Terms
حس
علم ضروري
علم استدلالي
قلب الطبائع
شبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational arguments always override sensory evidence.
Correction: Nasafi argues that sensory immediate knowledge is infallible, while rational inference is fallible, so when they conflict, the sensory evidence takes priority.

Checkpoint

A) Inferential knowledge (al-ʿilm al-istidlālī)
B) Sensory immediate knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī)
C) Both are equally certain
D) Only rational knowledge

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that sensory immediate knowledge (ʿilm ḍarūrī) is superior to fallible rational inference, thereby refuting the naturalists' denial of miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Four Conditions for a Valid Claim
Nasafi enumerates four necessary conditions for a valid claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four necessary conditions that any valid claim must satisfy according to Nasafi's logical methodology.

Core Idea
A valid claim must be possible, proven, flawless, and consistent.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi sets out the four logical criteria that any claim must meet to be considered valid. These conditions form a universal framework for evaluating assertions, whether in theology, law, or everyday reasoning. As you read, notice how each condition builds on the previous one to ensure a claim is sound.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Nasafi uses a very structured, enumerative style here.
2
Identify each condition
The passage lists four conditions. Find each one and note what it requires. The first condition is about possibility; the second about evidence; the third about the evidence being flawless; the fourth about consistency.
3
Understand the rationale behind each condition
For each condition, ask yourself: why is this necessary for a claim to be valid? The commentary will help you see the logical reasoning.
Pay special attention to the explanation for the third condition and the verification Nasafi provides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نزيد في البيان فتقول : إن كل من ادعى دعوى كانت صحة دعواه متعلقة باستجماع معاني أربعة: أولها كون الدعوى في حيز الإمكان، فإن من ادعى ما هو ممتنع محال في رد قوله في أول وهلة وأعرض عن سماع بينته. والثاني: أن يأتي ببينة مطابقة للدعوى. والثالث: أن تكون دواعي الجرح منتفية عن بينته إذ لو تمكن فيها جرح بوجه من الوجوه لردت إذ لا قبول إلا لما هو صحيح في نفسه. يحققه : أنها ذكرت ليصح بها غيرها وهو الدعوى، ولا وجه إلى تصحيح شيء ما بما ليس في نفسه بصحيح. والرابع سلامة الدعوى والبينة عن مناقضة ترد عليهما أو على أحدهما، إذ المناقض معترف بنقض ما ادعاه وفساده، إذ هو بادعائه صحة أحد المتناقضين شاهد ببطلان الآخر، فصار بادعائه صحة كل واحد منهما في محل شاهدا بفساد الآخر، فإذًا صار معترفا بفساد الآخر، فإذا صار معترفًا بفساد كل واحد من الأمرين ثم لا شك في كون صحة الدعوى متعلقة بهذه المعاني.
Then we add to the explanation and say: Indeed, everyone who makes a claim, the soundness of his claim depends on the gathering of four meanings: First, that the claim be in the realm of possibility, for whoever claims what is impossible and absurd, his statement is rejected at first glance and one turns away from hearing his evidence. Second, that he brings evidence that matches the claim. Third, that reasons for invalidation be absent from his evidence, for if a fault is established in it in any way, it is rejected, since acceptance is only for what is sound in itself. Its verification: it was brought so that something else, namely the claim, could be made sound through it, and there is no way to make something sound through what is not sound in itself. Fourth, the safety of the claim and the evidence from a contradiction that would refute them or one of them, for the contradictory admits the invalidation of what he claimed and its corruption, since by claiming the soundness of one of the two contradictories he bears witness to the falsity of the other, so by claiming the soundness of each of them in one place he becomes a witness to the falsity of the other; thus he becomes an admitter of the falsity of the other, and if he becomes an admitter of the falsity of each of the two matters, then there is no doubt that the soundness of the claim is dependent on these meanings.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage enumerates four necessary conditions for any claim to be valid. The author states these conditions in a logical sequence. First, the claim must be possible (في حيز الإمكان); if it is inherently impossible or absurd, it is rejected outright without needing to hear evidence. Second, the claimant must present evidence (بينة) that exactly matches the claim. Third, the evidence must be free from any reasons for invalidation (دواعي الجرح); if there is any flaw, the evidence is rejected because only what is sound in itself can prove something else. The author clarifies this by explaining that evidence is meant to validate the claim, and it cannot validate something if it is not itself valid. Fourth, both the claim and the evidence must be free from any internal contradiction (مناقضة). If a contradiction exists, the claimant implicitly admits the falsity of one of the conflicting statements, which undermines the entire case. The author concludes that the validity of a claim is necessarily tied to fulfilling these four conditions.

Key Terms
دعوى
إمكان
بينة
جرح
مناقضة
متناقضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These conditions apply only to legal or judicial claims.
Correction: Nasafi intends these conditions universally, including claims of prophethood and theological assertions.

Checkpoint
What is the first condition for a claim to be valid according to Nasafi?
A) The claim must be supported by a miracle.
B) The claim must be possible.
C) The claim must be from a prophet.
D) The claim must be accompanied by witnesses.

Key Takeaway
The soundness of any claim depends on it being possible, supported by matching evidence, free from flaws, and free from internal contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to believe in Allah, what evidence (bayyinah) do you present from your actions and character? Does that evidence truly match your claim, or is there a gap?
?
Can you identify any contradictions in your own religious claims—for example, claiming to trust Allah's plan while worrying excessively about the future? How does this passage call you to correct that?
?

Author states that denial of occurrence rests on five aspects only.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Denial cannot be arbitrary; it always falls into one of five categories.

Before discussing specific arguments, the author establishes a key methodological principle: that all denials of events can be reduced to five possible grounds. This passage presents that framework, which will be elaborated in subsequent lessons. Understanding this prepares you to evaluate objections systematically.

The Five Domains of Religion
Nasafi enumerates the five domains of shari'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the shari'a encompasses five domains: beliefs, acts of worship, transactions, prohibitions, and ethical virtues.

Core Idea
Religion is a comprehensive system covering belief, action, and morality.

Imam Nasafi begins by establishing the universal structure of religion as understood by all religious communities. He identifies five essential domains that the Prophet Muhammad addressed, each of which reason confirms as correct.

Guided Path

The Five Categories of Sharia and the Pillars of Belief
Classification of Sharia into five categories and the five pillars of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five categories that encompass all religious matters according to Islamic teachings, and the five pillars of faith that form the core of beliefs.

Core Idea
All religious matters fall into five categories; belief is the first, with five pillars.

This passage from the Tabṣirat al-Adilla introduces a foundational classification of the entire Sharia into five categories. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, asserts that sound reason testifies to the correctness of what the Prophet Muhammad brought in each category. He then begins to detail the first category—beliefs—which are further divided into the well-known five pillars of faith. Understanding this framework is essential for grasping the structure of Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

2

The five categories are listed first, then the five pillars are given as the division of beliefs.
3
Reflect on the rational testimony

This ties into the broader theme of rational proof in Islamic theology.

The Threefold Division of Islamic Law: Transactions, Deterrents, and Etiquette
Imam Nasafi enumerates the divisions of transactions, deterrents, and etiquette.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic law is classified into transactions, deterrents, and etiquette, and their respective subdivisions.

Core Idea
Islamic law covers every aspect of life: transactions, penalties, and manners.

Imam Nasafi presents a comprehensive classification of Islamic rulings. He divides them into three main categories: transactions (mu'amalat), deterrents (mazajir), and etiquette (adab). Each category is further subdivided to cover the full range of human actions. This lesson will guide you through these categories and their subdivisions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, to grasp the threefold division and their subdivisions.
Notice the numbered lists: five, five, and four.
2
Identify each category's subdivisions
List the five types of transactions, five deterrents, and four aspects of etiquette. Note how each relates to a specific domain of life.
3
Reflect on the comprehensiveness
Consider how these categories cover financial, familial, legal, penal, ethical, and social aspects, showing the holistic nature of Islamic law.
This classification is not exhaustive but covers major areas.
4
Connect to real-world applications
Think of examples from daily life that fall under each subdivision. This will help internalize the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعاملات تنقسم إلى خمسة: وهي المفاوضات المالية كالبيوع والإجارات، والمناكحات كالتزويج والتطليق والمخاصمات كالدعاوي والبينات والأمانات كالودائع والعواري، والتركات كالوصايا والمواريث. والمزاجر تنقسم أيضا إلى خمسة أقسام: وهي مزجرة قتل النفس كالقود والدية، ومزجرة أخذ المال كالقطع أو الصلب، ومزجرة هتك الستر كالجلد أو الرجم، ومزجرة ثلب العرض كالجلد مع التفسيق ورد الشهادات على التأبيد، ومزجرة خلع البيضة كالقتل على الردة. والآداب والشيم تنقسم أربعة أقسام: أحدها: الأخلاق والثاني: الآداب، والثالث: السياسات والرابع: المعاشرات.
And transactions are divided into five: financial negotiations like sales and leases, marriages like marriage and divorce, litigations like claims and evidence, trusts like deposits and loans, and inheritances like bequests and estates. And the deterrents are also divided into five divisions: deterrent for killing the soul, like retaliation and blood money; deterrent for taking wealth, like amputation or crucifixion; deterrent for tearing the veil, like flogging or stoning; deterrent for defaming honor, like flogging with discrediting and lifelong rejection of testimonies; and deterrent for abandoning the religion, like execution for apostasy. And etiquette and customs are divided into four divisions: one: morals; second: etiquette; third: policies; fourth: social dealings.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of Islamic legal rulings into three main categories: transactions (mu'amalat), deterrents (mazajir), and etiquette (adab). The author begins by dividing mu'amalat into five types: financial contracts (e.g., sales and leases), marriage-related matters (marriage and divorce), disputes (claims and evidence), trusts (deposits and loans), and inheritances (bequests and estates). Each category governs specific social and economic interactions. Next, the deterrents (penalties) are also divided into five: those for killing (retaliation or blood money), theft (amputation or crucifixion), illicit sexual relations (flogging or stoning), slander (flogging plus discrediting and lifelong rejection of testimony), and apostasy (execution). These punishments serve as deterrents to protect life, property, honor, and religion. Finally, etiquette and customs (adab wa shiyam) are divided into four: morals (akhlaq), etiquette (adab), policies (siyasat), and social dealings (mu'asharat). This classification shows the comprehensive nature of Islamic law, regulating both external actions and internal character.

Key Terms
المعاملات
المزاجر
الآداب
الأخلاق
السياسات
المعاشرات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These categories are exhaustive and cover every single ruling.
Correction: They are broad divisions that cover major areas of law, but many specific rulings fall under these categories or may be interdisciplinary.

Checkpoint
How many divisions are there for transactions (mu'amalat) according to this passage?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
Islamic law is systematically divided into transactions (5), deterrents (5), and etiquette (4), showing its comprehensive nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the five deterrents listed—punishments for killing, theft, illicit relations, slander, and apostasy—do any of these make you feel a sense of awe before God’s law? Which one challenges your current lifestyle the most, and what concrete step will you take today to live more mindfully of this deterrent?
?
The classification includes 'policies' (siyāsāt) under etiquette. How often do you consider that your personal character and social dealings are part of Islamic law? In which relationship—family, work, or community—do you most struggle to apply the akhlāq (morals) that this passage demands?
?
The five divisions of transactions cover everything from sales to inheritances. Is there a financial or familial matter in your life that you have not grounded in the teachings of Islamic law? What is one specific transaction or trust you will review tonight to ensure it aligns with the comprehensive framework outlined here?

The Wisdom of Purification (Tahara) Before Prayer
The author explains the profound wisdom behind purification requirements for prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the profound wisdom behind the purification requirements for prayer, including how they align with human nature (fitrah) and the role of tayammum as a distinctive emblem of worship.

Core Idea
Purification before prayer reflects both physical cleanliness and a deeper spiritual alignment with our innate disposition (fitrah).

The author of Tabsirat al-Adilla presents a compelling argument for why purification is required for prayer. He compares the servant's preparation for his King to our own preparation for Allah, showing that cleanliness is not arbitrary but rooted in what human intellects naturally approve. This passage also explains how tayammum serves as a distinguishing emblem of worship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the analogy of the servant and the King.
2
Identify the key components
Look for the analogy, the mention of fitrah, and the discussion of tayammum. See how they connect to the overall wisdom.
3
Understand the reasoning
Ask yourself: Why does the author say purification is based on what people's intellects approve? How does tayammum fit into this?
This connects to the learning objective.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding about tayammum being merely a substitute. What does the author say to clarify its significance?
Use the commentary for help if needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم في الطهارة حكمة عظيمة، فإن العبد إذا توجه لخدمة ملكه يجب أن يتخذ زينة ويجدد نظافته، وأخف الزينة ستر العورة، وأيسر النظافة تنقية الأطراف التي تتكشف كثيرًا وتظهر للأبصار أبدًا، ومتى أبصرت نقية من الوسخ نظيفة من الدرن قبلها القلب واستحسنها العقل والله تعالى شرع لنا دينا ذكر أنه فطرته التي فطر الناس عليها فشرع ما استحسنوه في عقولهم وارتضوه فيما بينهم، وعلق وجوب تنظيفها إذا أراد إقامة هذه الطاعة العظيمة بخروج شيء من الأذى والدنس، وأقام عند عوز الماء وعدمه ضرب اليد على الصعيد مقامه، ثم بين الرسول عليه السلام أن المستحب نفض اليدين عن التراب، ليدل بذلك أن أصل العبادة هو المسح لا غير، وأن الصعيد هو علم للتعبد وشعار يمتاز به رخصة العبادة عن غيرها.
Then in purification (al-tahara) there is a great wisdom. For the servant, when he intends to serve his King, must adopt adornment and renew his cleanliness. The least of adornment is covering the private parts, and the easiest of cleanliness is cleaning the extremities which are often exposed and always visible to the eyes. When they are seen pure of filth and clean of dirt, the heart accepts them and the intellect approves them. Allah Almighty legislated for us a religion which He mentioned is His innate disposition (fitrah) upon which He created people. So He legislated what they deemed good in their intellects and approved among themselves. He made the obligation of cleaning them conditional upon intending to perform this great act of obedience when something of harm or impurity comes out. And He established, in case of lack of water or its absence, striking the hands on pure earth (al-sa'id) as its substitute. Then the Messenger (peace be upon him) clarified that it is recommended to shake off the dust from the hands, to indicate that the origin of worship is wiping (al-mash) and nothing else, and that pure earth is a sign of devotion and a distinguishing emblem by which the license of worship is distinguished from other things.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage explains the wisdom of purification before prayer. The author draws an analogy: when a servant approaches a king, he adorns himself and cleanses himself. Similarly, for prayer, the minimum adornment is covering the private parts, and the minimum cleanliness is cleaning the hands, face, and feet (the extremities) which are often exposed. People naturally find cleanliness appealing. Then the author notes that Allah's religion is based on fitrah (natural disposition), so He legislates what people's intellects deem good. Purification is made obligatory when one intends to pray, and in the absence of water, tayammum (dry ablution) is permitted. The Prophet (peace be upon him) recommended shaking off dust after tayammum to show that the essence is wiping, not that the dust is itself impure. Thus, pure earth serves as a distinguishing mark of devotion.

Key Terms
الطهارة (purification)
الوضوء (ablution)
التيمم (dry ablution)
الفطرة (innate disposition)
المسح (wiping)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tayammum is merely a ritual substitute without any deeper significance; it is just a way to fulfill the obligation when water is unavailable.
Correction: The author explains that tayammum is not just a substitute; it serves as a 'sign of devotion and a distinguishing emblem' (shiar) that marks the worship as a special license from Allah. The Prophet's instruction to shake off dust indicates that the core act is wiping, not the earth itself, highlighting that tayammum has its own ritual identity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did Allah make purification conditional upon intending prayer?
A) To make prayer more difficult.
B) Because the mind naturally deems cleanliness good and it is part of fitrah.
C) Because water is always available.
D) To distinguish Muslims from non-Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Purification before prayer embodies a wisdom rooted in human nature (fitrah), with tayammum serving as a distinctive emblem of worship when water is unavailable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform wudu or tayammum, do you see it as a mere physical routine, or do you feel that your heart and intellect are approving of this act as something deeply aligned with your innate nature? What would change if you consciously reminded yourself of this wisdom before your next purification? Commit to one specific thought or intention.
?
The author says that when the extremities are clean, the heart accepts them and the intellect approves. Is there any disconnect between your outward purification and your inner state? Are you cleaning your body while your heart harbors something that displeases Allah? What steps can you take to ensure your purification is both physical and spiritual? Write a short prayer asking Allah to purify your heart as well.
?
Tayammum is described as a 'distinguishing emblem of worship.' If you have ever performed tayammum, did you appreciate it as a special license and a distinct act of devotion, or did you see it as just a substitute? How can you cultivate greater reverence for this allowance from Allah, especially in times when water is unavailable? Make a resolve to thank Allah for this mercy.

The Divine Wisdom of the Sharia and the Decision to Omit Detailed Discussion
Author explains omission of detailed Sharia wisdom and argues for its divine origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author omits detailed discussion of many Sharia topics despite their profound wisdom, and how this omission itself affirms the divine origin of the Sharia.

Core Idea
The Sharia's wisdom is so vast that even a lifetime of contemplation cannot grasp its tenth.

After covering acts of worship, the author announces he will not detail the wisdom behind transactions, marriages, foods, expiations, and crimes. He gives two reasons: to keep the book concise and because readers lack enthusiasm. Yet he immediately turns this into a powerful proof: only divine revelation could produce a system of such profound and comprehensive wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the omitted topics
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the list of topics the author says he will omit: transactions, marriages, foods, drinks, prohibitions, permissions, expiations, crimes, and their wisdom.
The author has already covered acts of worship; now he moves to these areas but decides not to elaborate.
2
Understand the author's twofold reason for omission
The author gives two reasons: first, to avoid lengthening the book; second, because the seekers (readers) lack enthusiasm and have limited aspirations. He acknowledges that the benefit of discussing the wisdom would be great, but he refrains due to these constraints.
His reluctance is not due to lack of knowledge, as he praises the scholars who have expounded on it.
3
Grasp the concluding argument: the depth of wisdom proves divine origin
The author argues that the Sharia is built on pure wisdom and complete benefit, so profound that even a million ascetics contemplating for years cannot grasp a tenth. Therefore, no fair-minded person could attribute it to a human; it must be divine revelation and heavenly law.
This is a powerful argument from the inimitability (i'jaz) of the Sharia's wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذ أتينا على ذكر العبادات فنترك ذكر ما في المعاملات والمناكح والمآكل والمشارب وفي تحريم ما حرم وتحليل ما حلل، وذكر الكفارات، وتقسيم الجرائم التي في بعضها كفارة، وفي بعضها حد وبيان الحكمة من ذلك كله لئلا يطول الكتاب. وإن كنا لو ذكرنا بعض ما أورده علماء الأمة وحكماء أهل الملة وفرسان الكلام وقادة أهل الإسلام لكانت الفائدة متضاعفة والعائدة متوفرة، غير أن فتور رغبات الملتمسين وقصور همهم، حالا بيني وبين الإبانة عن كل ذلك. وأن دينًا بني على الحكمة المحضة والمصلحة التامة على وجه تقصر دون الإحاطة بعشر من أعشار ذلك عقول ألف ألف من المرتاضين الذين أذابوا مطايا أفكارهم سنين كثيرة في التأمل في ذلك، والبحث عنه لتحقيق أن لا يظن عاقل له حظ من الإنصاف وجانب الإلف أو العصبية الجنسية أو الأنفة أو الفرار عن لزوم ربقة التكليف، أن مثله من أوضاع من لم يعرف بتعلم نوع من أنواع العلوم والارتياض بشيء من المعارف بل يتعين أنه هو الوحي الإلهي والشرع السماوي.
And since we have come to the mention of acts of worship, we omit the mention of what is in transactions, marriages, foods and drinks, and regarding the prohibition of what He prohibited and the analysis of what He permitted, and the mention of expiations, and the division of crimes, some of which have expiation and some of which have a prescribed punishment, and the explanation of the wisdom behind all of that, lest the book become long. And if we were to mention some of what the scholars of the community and the sages of the religion and the knights of kalam and the leaders of Islam have presented, the benefit would be multiplied and the return abundant, except that the weakness of the desires of the seekers and the limitation of their aspirations have stood between me and the clarification of all that. And that a religion built upon pure wisdom and complete benefit in a way that the minds of a million ascetics who have melted away the mounts of their thoughts for many years in contemplation of that and research about it fall short of encompassing a tenth of its tenths, in order that a sensible person who has a share of fairness and a side free from bias or tribal fanaticism or pride or escape from the necessity of the harness of obligation should not think that the like of it is from the inventions of someone who did not know any type of sciences or practice of any knowledge, but rather it must be the divine revelation and the heavenly law.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a methodological note and a strong affirmation of the divine origin of the Sharia. The author states that after discussing acts of worship (العبادات), he will omit discussion of transactions (المعاملات), marriages (المناكح), foods (المآكل), drinks (المشارب), prohibitions, permissions, expiations (الكفارات), and crimes (الجرائم) and their wisdom, to avoid lengthening the book. He acknowledges that if he were to mention the wisdom from the scholars and sages, the benefit would be great, but the lack of enthusiasm of the readers prevents him. Then he makes a powerful assertion: this religion is built on pure wisdom (حكمة محضة) and complete benefit (مصلحة تامة) to such an extent that even the minds of a million ascetics who have spent years contemplating it cannot grasp a tenth of it. Therefore, no rational person who is objective and free from bias, tribalism, pride, or evasion of duty could think that such a system comes from someone ignorant of science and knowledge; rather, it must be divine revelation (الوحي الإلهي) and heavenly law (الشرع السماوي).

Key Terms
المعاملات
المناكح
الحكمة المحضة
المصلحة التامة
الوحي الإلهي
الشرع السماوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author omits details because the topics are not important or lack wisdom.
Correction: The author emphasizes that the entire Sharia is built on pure wisdom and complete benefit; he omits only to keep the book short and due to readers' lack of enthusiasm.

Checkpoint
Why does the author omit detailed discussion of transactions, marriages, foods, and other topics?
A) He does not have sufficient knowledge of them.
B) He wants to keep the book short and the readers lack enthusiasm.
C) They are not important for the proof of prophethood.
D) He plans to write a separate book on them.

Key Takeaway
The Sharia is built on pure wisdom and complete benefit, confirming its divine origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on your personal attitude toward the detailed rulings of Islam (e.g., in transactions, marriage, food). Do you ever catch yourself thinking they are outdated or merely cultural? How does the author's argument—that the Sharia's wisdom is so profound that a million ascetics cannot grasp a tenth of it—challenge that thought? What will you change in your attitude starting today?
?
The author implies that rejecting the divine origin of Sharia comes from bias, pride, or escaping obligation. Is there any ruling in your life that you find difficult to accept because it clashes with your desires? In light of this passage, what one concrete action can you take to submit more fully, even without understanding the full wisdom?
?
The author omitted detailed discussion because the seekers' aspirations were limited. How strong is your own aspiration to understand the deeper wisdom behind the Sharia? What is one practical step you will take this week to deepen your appreciation for the divine wisdom in even a single ruling?
