Omitting Transactions and Divine Origin of Law
Author explains omission of transactional details and affirms divine origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author omits detailed discussion of transactions and how this omission reinforces the divine origin of Islamic law.

Core Idea
The author's brevity on transactions points to the vastness of divine wisdom.

In this passage, the author explains his editorial decision to skip detailed coverage of transactional rulings. Far from being a sign of neglect, he argues that the depth and perfection of Islamic law are so immense that even a brief mention would require volumes. This sets the stage for a powerful affirmation of the religion's divine source.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's tone: he is apologetic for omitting details but resolute in his reasoning.
Pay attention to the contrast between worship (ʿibādāt) and transactions (muʿāmalāt).
2
Identify the two reasons for omission
The author gives two reasons for not detailing transactions: to avoid lengthening the book, and because the students' lack of interest and limited capacity prevents expounding all of it. Find these in the text.
3
Analyze the argument for divine origin
The author argues that the religion is built on pure wisdom (ḥikma maḥḍa) and complete benefit (maṣlaḥa tāmma), so profound that no human intellect could encompass it. Therefore, it cannot be a human fabrication but must be divine revelation (waḥy ilāhī) and heavenly law (sharʿ samāwī).
Consider why the author uses hyperbole (e.g., 'a million ascetics') to emphasize the point.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Some might think the omission means the author considers transactions unimportant. The passage guards against that by showing the opposite: the subject is too vast and the audience too limited. Discuss this insight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[الكلام في المعاملات]: وإذ أتينا على ذكر العبادات فنترك ذكر ما في المعاملات والمناكح والمآكل والمشارب وفي تحريم ما حرم وتحليل ما حلل، وذكر الكفارات، وتقسيم الجرائم التي في بعضها كفارة، وفي بعضها حد وبيان الحكمة من ذلك كله لئلا يطول الكتاب. وإن كنا لو ذكرنا بعض ما أورده علماء الأمة وحكماء أهل الملة وفرسان الكلام وقادة أهل الإسلام لكانت الفائدة متضاعفة والعائدة متوفرة، غير أن فتور رغبات الملتمسين وقصور همهم حالا بيني وبين الإبانة عن كل ذلك. وأن دينًا بني على الحكمة المحضة والمصلحة التامة على وجه تقصر دون الإحاطة بعشر من أعشار ذلك عقول ألف ألف من المرتاضين الذين أذابوا مطايا أفكارهم سنين كثيرة في التأمل في ذلك، والبحث عنه لتحقيق أن لا يظن عاقل له حظ من الإنصاف وجانب الإلف أو العصبية الجنسية أو الأنفة أو الفرار عن لزوم ربقة التكليف، أن مثله من أوضاع من لم يعرف بتعلم نوع من أنواع العلوم والارتياض بشيء من المعارف بل يتعين أنه هو الوحي الإلهي والشرع السماوي.
[Discussion of Transactions]: Since we have mentioned worship, we omit mention of what pertains to transactions, marriages, foods, drinks, and the prohibition of what is forbidden and analysis of what is allowed, and mention of expiations, and division of crimes — some of which require expiation, some of which require a fixed penalty — and explanation of the wisdom behind all that, so as not to lengthen the book. And if we were to mention some of what the scholars of the community, the wise of the nation, the knights of kalam, and the leaders of Islam have presented, the benefit would be multiplied and the advantage abundant, except that the weakness of the desires of the seekers and the deficiency of their aims prevented me from expounding all of that. And that a religion built upon pure wisdom and complete benefit, in a manner that the intellects of a million ascetics who have melted the mounts of their thoughts in many years of reflection on that and research into it fall short of encompassing a tenth of its tenths, so that no rational person with a share of fairness and a tendency towards familiarity, or racial fanaticism, or pride, or escape from the obligation of the burden of responsibility would think that such [a religion] could be a fabrication of one who was not known to have learned any type of science or to have practiced any sort of knowledge, but rather it is clearly divine revelation and heavenly law.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is an editorial explanation by the author regarding his decision to omit detailed discussion of transactions and other practical rulings. He begins by stating that after covering worship ('ibadat), he will not go into the same detail for transactions, marriages, foods, drinks, prohibitions, expiations, and crimes. He gives two reasons: first, to avoid lengthening the book; second, because the lack of interest and limited aims of students prevents him. He then makes a powerful argument for the divine origin of the religion: even if he had included all that the great scholars have said, the full benefit would be enormous, but he cannot do justice. He asserts that the religion is built on pure wisdom and complete benefit, so profound that even a million ascetics spending years in reflection could not grasp a tenth of it. Therefore, no fair-minded person could think it is the invention of an unlearned person; it must be divine revelation and heavenly law. The author guards against the misunderstanding that his brevity implies the material is unimportant; instead, it is too vast and the audience too limited.

Key Terms
المعاملات
العبادات
الحكمة المحضة
الوحي الإلهي
الشرع السماوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author omits details because transactions are trivial or not part of true religion.
Correction: The author explicitly states his omission is due to book length and student limitations, and he argues that the religion's wisdom is so immense that it proves divine origin.

Checkpoint

A) Because he does not consider them important
B) Because he does not know them

Key Takeaway
The omission of transactional details underscores the divine origin and limitless wisdom of Islamic law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Do your daily choices reflect this conviction, or do you often act as if the rulings are optional human suggestions? What is one specific change you can make today to align your actions with the belief that this is Allah's law?

The Author's Justification for Elaboration
Explains why the author elaborated despite promising brevity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author departed from his initial promise of brevity and how this reflects a methodological response to heresy.

Core Idea
Elaboration is sometimes a necessary defense against heresy.

In this concluding passage, the author of Tabsirat al-Adilla addresses a potential criticism: he promised brevity but has written at length. He explains that the rise of heresy in his time forced him to be thorough to protect the faithful. This lesson examines his reasoning and the principle behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the author's tone and the key terms.
The author uses the word 'إلحاد' (heresy) to describe the reason.
2
Identify the admission and justification
Find where the author admits to departing from brevity and the reason he gives for doing so. What threat does he mention?
The justification is 'لظهور الإلحاد في زماننا' (the appearance of heresy in our time).
3
Consider the historical context
Reflect on why heresy would be a pressing concern for the author and how it shapes his methodology.
The author likely faced contemporary challenges to prophethood.
4
Reflect on the principle
Think about the broader lesson: when defending core beliefs, thoroughness may override the desire for brevity.
This shows that scholarly works sometimes prioritize clarity and refutation over conciseness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا انتهينا إلى هذا فلنختم الباب، وقد خرجنا في هذا الباب عما شرطنا في أول الكتاب من الإيجاز، وطولنا فيه نوع تطويل، وبالغنا نوع مبالغة، وإن كان الاستقصاء في الباب يقتضي استغراق دفاتر، فعلنا ذلك لظهور الإلحاد في زماننا وطعن كثير من الخلعاء في أمر النبوة، فأردت بذلك حسم مادة شرهم، ودفع معرتهم عن ضعفة المسلمين والله خير مؤيد ومعين.
So when we have reached this, let us conclude the chapter. And we have in this chapter departed from what we stipulated at the beginning of the book of brevity, and we have lengthened it with a kind of lengthening, and we have done a kind of exaggeration. But if exhaustive treatment of the chapter required the consumption of notebooks, we did that because of the appearance of heresy in our time and the attack of many of the dissolute on the matter of prophethood. So I intended by that to cut off the source of their evil, and to repel their harm from the weak of the Muslims. And God is the best supporter and helper.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a concluding remark from the author. He acknowledges that he has departed from his initial promise of brevity, having lengthened and exaggerated the chapter. But he justifies this by noting the emergence of heresy in his time and the attacks on prophethood by many dissolute people. His intention was to eradicate the roots of their evil and protect weak Muslims from their harm. He ends with a supplication to God as the best supporter and helper.

تطويل
مبالغة
إلحاد
خلعاء
حسم مادة شرهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author is being self-critical or apologetic for a mistake.
Correction: The author is not apologizing for an error; he is explaining a deliberate methodological choice driven by the need to combat heresy.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
When heresy threatens core beliefs, scholars may depart from brevity to thoroughly refute falsehood and protect the believers.

?

Refutation of the Objection on Equality of Times
The author refutes an objection about equal possibility of action at different times.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct method of objecting to an argument about equality of times and why the Mu'tazila's approach is flawed.

Core Idea
Equality in one case does not require equality in all cases; distinctions must be shown.

The author has previously argued that action can occur without capacity at different times because both times are equal in lacking capacity. Now, he anticipates an objection: if the times are equal, then why does action occur at one time and not another? The correct objection, he explains, is not to deny equality but to show a distinction between the states. In this passage, he refutes the Mu'tazila's misguided attempt to require absolute equality across all cases and exposes their reliance on superficial appearances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dialectical structure: restatement, objection, response, conclusion.
2
Identify the author's position and the objection
Find where the author restates his position about the possibility of action without capacity at time two and time ten. Then identify what he says the correct objection would be.
The correct objection is to show a distinction, not simply to deny equality.
3
Understand the author's response
The author argues that equality is established in this specific case, so the equality holds in judgment unless a distinction in underlying meaning is shown. He does not need to explain the meaning.
The burden is on the objector to show the distinction, because the claim is specific, not absolute.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the Mu'tazila are ignorant of realities and build arguments on superficial appearances without evidence or meaning. Ask yourself why this critique matters for methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قلنا في جانب الفعل : إن حصوله لما جاز مع عدم القدرة في الوقت الثاني جاز في الوقت العاشر لاستوائهما في العدم، فيكون الاعتراض الصحيح ببيان التفرقة وإبطال التسوية بين الحالة الأولى وبين غيرها من الأحوال في شيء آخر، فإنا نقول : لما ثبتت التسوية بما بينا فيما نحن فيه، ثبتت التسوية في الحكم وفي غيره من الأشياء لما ظهرت التفرقة في الحكم على التفرقة في المعنى، ولا حاجة بنا إلى بيان المعنى الموجب للتفرقة، وإنما يلزمنا ما اعترضوا به أن لو ادعينا التسوية بين أول الأحوال وبين غيرها من الأحوال مطلقا في المواضع أجمع، فأما إذا ادعينا ذلك في موضع مخصوص وأقمنا دلالة التسوية فالاشتغال بإثبات التفرقة في موضع آخر عن سنن الصواب وعدول عن طريقة النظر. وبهذا تبين جهل المعتزلة بالحقائق وبناء الأمر على ظواهر لا دليل عليها ولا معنى تحتها.
And we have said concerning the side of the act: its occurrence, since it is possible with the absence of capacity in the second time, is possible in the tenth time because of their equality in non-existence. So the correct objection is by showing the distinction and invalidating the equality between the first state and other states in something else. For we say: since the equality is established by what we have explained in our present case, the equality is established in the judgment and in other things because the distinction in the judgment appears only upon a distinction in the meaning. And we have no need to explain the meaning necessitating the distinction. Rather, what their objection would oblige us is if we had claimed absolute equality between the first state and other states in all cases. But if we claim that in a specific place and we provide evidence for equality, then occupying ourselves with proving distinction in another place is not the correct path and is a deviation from the method of investigation. And by this the ignorance of the Mu'tazila concerning realities becomes clear, and their building matters upon appearances that have no evidence nor any meaning underneath.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the argument about the possibility of action without capacity. The author first restates his previous argument: if action can occur without capacity at time two, it can also occur without capacity at time ten, because both times are equal in lacking capacity. Then he addresses a potential objection: the correct objection would be to show a distinction between the first state and other states, thus invalidating the claimed equality. The author responds that since equality is established in this specific case (the relation between early and later times), the equality holds in the judgment unless a distinction in the underlying meaning is shown. He does not need to explain the meaning that would cause a distinction; the burden falls on the objector to show a distinction if he claims absolute equality in all cases. Since the claim is only for a specific case, demanding proof of distinction elsewhere is misguided. The author concludes that the Mu'tazila are ignorant of realities and rely on superficial appearances without evidence or meaning.

Key Terms
التفرقة
التسوية
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If equality is established between two times in one respect, then it must apply to all times and all respects.
Correction: Equality can be claimed in a specific case without requiring equality in all cases; the burden on the objector is only to show a distinction in the specific case.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct way to object to his argument about equality of times?
A) To prove that capacity is necessary for action.
B) To show a distinction between the first state and other states.
C) To deny that action can occur without capacity.
D) To argue that times are not equal in non-existence.

Key Takeaway
The correct objection to an argument based on equality in a specific case is to show a distinction in meaning, not to demand equality in all cases.

This passage accuses the Mu'tazila of building on appearances without evidence. Write down one area where you need to stop settling for appearances and demand real evidence.

The author says the correct objection is to show a distinction, not to demand proof in all cases. When someone presents an argument to you, do you tend to attack it by demanding it covers everything, or do you first try to understand its specific scope? Commit to adopting the method described here: engage with the specific case before demanding broader proof.

Maturidi Doctrine on Command and Prohibition as Opposites
The author presents the Maturidi agreement on command and prohibition as opposites and contrasts with Mu'tazili view on the distinctness of speech forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that commanding something is itself a prohibition of its opposite, and why this follows from the oneness of God's speech.

Core Idea
In Maturidi kalam, command and prohibition are two sides of the same coin.

This passage from the Tabsirat al-Adilla presents a key Maturidi doctrine: command and prohibition are not separate acts but are logically intertwined. The early Maturidi scholars agreed that every commanded act's abandonment (its opposite) is prohibited, and every prohibited act's abandonment is commanded. This is grounded in the Maturidi understanding that God's speech is one and indivisible. Let's examine the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning.
Notice the repeated structure of 'every commanded... its abandonment... prohibited'.
2
Understand the Maturidi agreement
Identify the two symmetrical claims: command implies prohibition of opposite, prohibition implies command of opposite. See how the author ties this to the oneness of God's speech.
3
Contrast with Mu'tazili view
Note how the Mu'tazili view of God's speech as composed of distinct forms leads them to separate command and prohibition as distinct speech acts. Reflect on how the nature of divine speech affects the ruling.
The author still asserts the logical connection despite the linguistic difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن أصحابنا مع أوائلهم اتفقوا أن كل مأمور به كان تركه وهو فعل مضادة منهيًا عنه، وكل منهي عنه تركه وهو فعل مضادة مأمورا به، إذا كان لكل واحد منهما ترك مخصوص وضد متعين ، وكذا عندنا في كل ما له أضداد من الجانين جميعًا، وعندهم فيما له أضداد تقسيم يطول ذكره يعرف ذلك في أصول الفقه، أن عندنا كان الأمر بالشيء نهيًا عن ضده، والنهي عن الشيء أمرًا بضده، إذ كلام الله تعالى عندنا واحد وهو بنفسه أمر بما أمر نهي عما نهى، فكان ما هو الأمر بالشيء نهيًا عن ضده. غير وعند المعتزلة كلام الله تعالى هذه العبارات المتركبة من الحروف على هذه الصيغات وللأمر صيغة مخصوصة وكذا للنهي صيغة مخصوصة لا يتصور كون النهي أمرًا ولا كون الأمر نهيًا . ولا شك أن ضد المأمور منهي وضد المنهي مأمور.
Then our companions together with their predecessors agreed that every commanded thing, its abandonment which is the act of its opposite is prohibited, and every prohibited thing, its abandonment which is the act of its opposite is commanded, since for each one there is a specific abandonment and a determinate opposite. And likewise with us in everything that has opposites from both sides. And according to them regarding what has opposites there is a classification whose mention would be lengthy; it is known in usul al-fiqh. That according to us, commanding something is a prohibition of its opposite, and prohibiting something is a command of its opposite, because the speech of Allah, exalted, with us is one, and in its very self it is a command of what He commands and a prohibition of what He prohibits. So what is the command of something is a prohibition of its opposite. Other than that, and according to the Mu'tazila, the speech of Allah, exalted, is these expressions composed of letters in these forms, and for command there is a specific form, and likewise for prohibition a specific form, and it is inconceivable that prohibition be a command or command be a prohibition. And there is no doubt that the opposite of the commanded is prohibited and the opposite of the prohibited is commanded.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents the Maturidi doctrine on the logical relationship between command (amr) and prohibition (nahy). The author states that the Maturidi scholars (ashabuna) and their early authorities agreed on the following: for every commanded act, its abandonment (which is doing the opposite act) is prohibited; and for every prohibited act, its abandonment (which is doing the opposite act) is commanded. This holds when each has a specific opposite. The author then notes that this is the Maturidi view for all things that have opposites on both sides (i.e., both command and prohibition have distinct opposites). He mentions that the Mu'tazila have a more detailed classification on this matter, which is covered in usul al-fiqh. The core Maturidi principle is that commanding something is itself a prohibition of its opposite, and prohibiting something is itself a command of its opposite. This is because, according to the Maturidis, God's speech is one—it is simultaneously a command and a prohibition, and the same speech act constitutes both. In contrast, the Mu'tazila hold that God's speech consists of physical expressions composed of letters and have specific forms; thus a command form cannot be a prohibition form. Nonetheless, the author concludes that there is no doubt that the opposite of the commanded is prohibited and the opposite of the prohibited is commanded, regardless of the linguistic form.

Key Terms
الأمر
النهي
الضد
كلام الله
الصيغ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that command and prohibition are entirely separate and independent acts of divine speech.
Correction: In Maturidi kalam, command and prohibition are logically intertwined because God's speech is one; commanding something is itself a prohibition of its opposite.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi view presented in this passage, what is the relationship between commanding something and its opposite?
A) Commanding something is unrelated to its opposite
B) Commanding something is a prohibition of its opposite
C) Commanding something is the same as commanding its opposite
D) The opposite of a commanded act is also commanded

Key Takeaway
In Maturidi theology, command and prohibition are not independent; each implies the other because God's speech is one and indivisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you struggle to obey a command of Allah, do you realize that your neglect is itself a violation of the prohibition of its opposite? How does this awareness intensify your sense of accountability?
?
If Allah's speech is one and indivisible, then your attitude toward any single command or prohibition is an attitude toward the entirety of revelation. Are there areas where you treat His commands as isolated rules rather than as a unified call? What would change if you saw them as all emanating from one divine word?
?
The Mu'tazili view breaks command and prohibition into separate forms. In your own life, do you compartmentalize obedience – keeping some commands while ignoring others – as if they were unrelated? How might the Maturidi view of unity compel you to a more integrated commitment?

The Shared False Premise of the Jabriyya and Mu'tazila
Nasafi identifies the shared false premise that one action cannot be under two agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the common false premise that led to the Jabriyya and Mu'tazila positions and why it is considered false in Maturidi thought.

Core Idea
Both extremes share one faulty assumption: one act cannot have two agents.

Nasafi traces the root error of both the Jabriyya and Mu'tazila to a shared false premise. By understanding this premise, you will see how both schools, despite their contradiction, commit the same logical mistake. This lesson examines that premise and its consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage ends abruptly; focus on the logical structure presented.
2
Identify the false premise
Find the sentence that states the false premise and note how it is justified using analogy from the witnessed world.
The premise is that a single act cannot be under the power of two agents.
3
Reflect on the divergence
Consider how the Mu'tazila (and by implication the Jabriyya) each took one side of the truth after accepting the false premise.
The Mu'tazila affirmed human agency and denied divine agency; the Jabriyya did the opposite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما تفرع هذان المذهبان الباطلان المتناقضان أعني مذهب الجبرية ومذهب القدرية الحاصلين على طرفي الغلو والتقصير (7) من اتفاق الفريقين على مقدمة كاذبة، وهى أن دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين محال، لما وجدوا ذلك في الشاهد محالاً، والشاهد أصل الغائب، فبعد ذلك نظرت المعتزلة إلى الدلائل الموجبة كون العباد فاعلين قادرين (8) فتمسكوا بها، وجعلوا أفعال (10) العباد
And these two false contradictory schools, namely the school of the Jabriyya and the school of the Qadariyya – who have reached the extremes of exaggeration and deficiency (7) – only branched off from an agreement of the two groups on a false premise: that it is impossible for a single object of power to be under the power of two powerful agents. Because they found that in the witnessed realm impossible, and the witnessed is the basis for the unseen. After that, the Mu'tazila considered the proofs that require that servants are agents and powerful (8), so they held to them, and they made the actions of the servants...
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage explains the root cause of the invalidity of both the Jabriyya and Mu'tazila positions. The author states that both contradictory schools arose from a shared false premise: that one and the same act cannot be within the power of two agents simultaneously. They analogized the unseen (divine power) to the witnessed (human power) and concluded it is impossible. Then the Mu'tazila, seeing proofs that humans are agents, held to that and denied God's role, while the Jabriyya held to God's omnipotence and denied human agency. The text cuts off before stating the correct position, but the implication is that this premise is false and the truth is that both agents can be involved, each in a different way.

قادرين

القدرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Jabriyya and Mu'tazila disagreed on everything; they shared no common ground.
Correction: Nasafi shows they both accept the false premise that one act cannot be under two agents, differing only in which agent they then affirm.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what false premise do the Jabriyya and Mu'tazila share?
A) That humans have no power at all
B) That Allah has no power over actions
C) That one action cannot be under the power of two agents
D) That the witnessed realm is not a basis for the unseen

Key Takeaway
Both the Jabriyya and Mu'tazila err from the same root: assuming an action can only have one agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you examine your own actions, do you lean more toward feeling helpless (Jabri tendency) or self-sufficient (Qadari tendency)? Which one feels more comfortable, and what would it cost you to hold both you and Allah as agents in that moment?
?
The false premise here is an intellectual error, but it leads to spiritual extremes. Is there a belief you hold about divine control or human responsibility that you have never genuinely questioned? Are you willing to test that belief against the Quran and Sunnah today, even if it shakes your comfort?

The Verse's General Form Intends Specificity
The author asserts the verse's specificity and argues against inclusion of divine essence and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a verse that appears general in wording may be intended for a specific meaning, and how this excludes God's essence and attributes from its scope.

Core Idea
A general expression does not always mean general application.

The author begins by asserting that the verse, though in the form of a general statement, was intended specifically. He then argues that including God's essence or attributes would defeat the verse's purpose. This lesson examines that argument and its implications for theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source text once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the author's claim that the verse is specific despite its general wording.
The passage is dense; take your time to understand the flow.
2
Identify the author's claim
The author states that the verse came in the form of generality but was intended for specificity (al-khusus). Note how he uses the phrase 'لم يرد بهذه الآية إن خرجت مخرج العموم إلا الخصوص'.
3
Understand the exclusion argument
The author argues that even if the wording could include God's essence and attributes linguistically, their inclusion would void the verse's purpose (establishing creation of other-than-God). Therefore, inclusion is impossible.
This is a key point: purpose overrides literal generality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العدم إلى الوجود، فعلم بهذا أنه تعالى لم يرد بهذه الآية إن خرجت مخرج العموم إلا الخصوص. جميع ما يحققه أن ذات الله تعالى شيء بلا خلاف بيننا وبينكم، وكذا صفاته عند كثير منكم أشياء ولم تكن ذاته ولا صفاته داخلاً تحت هذه الآية لما في دخول ذلك فيها زوال ما سبقت له الآية من إثبات ما كان دخوله تحت الخطاب يوجب إبطال الفرض الذي سيق له الخطاب ويثبت ضد ذلك يمتنع دخوله تحته، وإن كان بحيث يتناوله اللفظ من حيث اللغة، وإن لم يخص شيء سواه مما يتناوله لفظ الخطاب فكيف وقد خص منه غيره مما هو مساو لذلك الغير في المعنى الموجب خروجه عن قضية الكلام، ولا شك أن إخراج بعض ما يتناوله اللفظ عن وضعه لمعنى يوجب إخراجه موجب إخراج يوجد فيه ذلك المعنى لاقتضاء القول التسوية في الحكم عند ثبوت المساواة في المعنى الموجب لذلك، على أن العام الذي خص منشئ يتوقف فيه عند كثير من أهل الأصول لصيرورته مجملاً، ولا يصح التعلق به لإثبات حكم جار مجرى الفروع يمكن إثباته بما لا وجه إلى إثباتها إلا الدليل القاطع الموجب للعلم الثابت بطريق التيقن، وعند أكثر أهل الأصول وإن لم ينقلب مجملاً إلا أنه يجب الحكم فيما وراء المخصوص من أفراد العموم وأحاده بطريق التيقن، بل يوجب ذلك بطريق غالب الرأي بلا خلاف بيننا وبينكم على أن هذا عندكم حكم العام وإن لم يخص منه شيء، والتعلق بمثل هذا الدليل في الأبواب الاعتقادية جهل بمواضع الحجاج ومواقع الأدلة ومراتبها في أنفسها ودرجات المحال التي يتعلق بها فيها، ويتبين بهذا أن لا متعلق بالآية في محل النزاع وموضع الخلاف.
From non-existence to existence, so it is known by this that He, exalted, did not intend by this verse, although it came in the form of generality, except specificity. All that he verifies is that the essence of Allah is a thing without disagreement between us and you, and likewise His attributes are things according to many of you, but His essence and attributes were not included under this verse, because including them would void what the verse was intended for, of establishing that their inclusion under the address would invalidate the premise for which the address was made and would establish the opposite; thus their inclusion is impossible, even if the wording includes them from a linguistic perspective. And if nothing else were specified from what the wording includes, how can it be that other things have been specified which are equivalent to that other in the meaning that necessitates their exclusion from the purport of the speech? There is no doubt that excluding some of what the wording includes from its usage for a meaning that necessitates its exclusion necessitates the exclusion of whatever contains that meaning, because the statement demands equality in the ruling when equality in the meaning that necessitates it is established. Moreover, the general that has been specified becomes suspended according to many of the scholars of usul because it becomes ambiguous, and it is not valid to rely on it for establishing a ruling that falls under the category of subsidiary matters that can be established by something that has no way to be established except by a decisive proof that necessitates certain knowledge established by the path of certainty. According to most scholars of usul, even if it does not become ambiguous, it is nevertheless necessary to judge regarding what is beyond the specified individuals of the general and its singulars by the path of certainty; rather, it necessitates that by the path of predominant opinion without disagreement between us and you, provided that this is the ruling of the general among you even if nothing has been specified from it. And relying on such a proof in doctrinal matters is ignorance of the places of argumentation, the positions of proofs, their ranks in themselves, and the degrees of premises that are relied upon in them. And by this it becomes clear that there is no grounding in the verse in the place of dispute and point of disagreement.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents an argument refuting the claim that a general verse (such as 'Allah is the Creator of everything') includes God's own essence and attributes. The author begins by stating that the verse, though appearing general, was intended for a specific purpose (al-khusus). He then asserts the agreed point that God's essence is a thing, and attributes are things among many opponents, but such items cannot be included under the verse because inclusion would nullify the very premise the verse was meant to establish (namely, affirming creation of what is other than God). The author explains that even if the wording linguistically could cover them, the context (the verse's purpose) necessitates their exclusion. He then discusses the principle of specification (takhsis) and its implications: if an equivalent item is specified out, the same applies to others with the same meaning. Furthermore, the author notes that a general term that has been specified becomes ambiguous (mujmal) according to many scholars of usul, and such a general cannot be used to establish rulings in theological matters that require certainty. Even among those who do not consider it ambiguous, they require certainty for what remains after specification. The author concludes that relying on such a general argument in doctrinal matters is ignorance of proper argumentative method, and thus the verse provides no basis for the disputed point.

Key Terms
عموم
خصوص
ذات
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if a verse says 'everything' it literally includes everything, even God.
Correction: The author shows that context and purpose restrict the meaning, so general terms are often specific.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are God's essence and attributes not included under the verse?
A) They are not things.
B) Including them would invalidate the verse's purpose.
C) The language cannot express them.
D) They were excluded by a separate verse.

Key Takeaway
The verse's apparent generality is limited by its purpose, which excludes God's essence and attributes.

The Principle of Specification and Doctrinal Certainty
The author explains the principle of specification and argues that general verses cannot provide certain evidence in theology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly discussion on whether a specified general term becomes ambiguous, and why it cannot be used for certain proofs in doctrinal matters.

Core Idea
A specified general cannot establish certainty in theology.

The author continues by discussing the implications of specification. He notes that if an equivalent item was specified out, then all similar items must be excluded. Then he addresses the usul debate: does a specified general become ambiguous? Even if not, it requires certainty in judgment, and using such a proof in doctrinal matters is ignorance. This lesson breaks down that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Focus on the second part of the source, which discusses the equality principle and the status of a specified general.
This part is more technical; refer to the key terms.
2
Understand the equality principle
The author argues that if an equivalent item has been specified out, then all items with the same meaning must also be excluded. This is a logical necessity for consistency.
3
Analyze the usul debate
Many scholars consider a specified general to become ambiguous (mujmal) and thus unreliable for rulings. Even among those who disagree, certainty is required for the remaining items. Therefore, such a general cannot be used in theology, which demands certainty.
The author concludes that reliance on such a proof in doctrinal matters is ignorance of proper methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العدم إلى الوجود، فعلم بهذا أنه تعالى لم يرد بهذه الآية إن خرجت مخرج العموم إلا الخصوص. جميع ما يحققه أن ذات الله تعالى شيء بلا خلاف بيننا وبينكم، وكذا صفاته عند كثير منكم أشياء ولم تكن ذاته ولا صفاته داخلاً تحت هذه الآية لما في دخول ذلك فيها زوال ما سبقت له الآية من إثبات ما كان دخوله تحت الخطاب يوجب إبطال الفرض الذي سيق له الخطاب ويثبت ضد ذلك يمتنع دخوله تحته، وإن كان بحيث يتناوله اللفظ من حيث اللغة، وإن لم يخص شيء سواه مما يتناوله لفظ الخطاب فكيف وقد خص منه غيره مما هو مساو لذلك الغير في المعنى الموجب خروجه عن قضية الكلام، ولا شك أن إخراج بعض ما يتناوله اللفظ عن وضعه لمعنى يوجب إخراجه موجب إخراج يوجد فيه ذلك المعنى لاقتضاء القول التسوية في الحكم عند ثبوت المساواة في المعنى الموجب لذلك، على أن العام الذي خص منشئ يتوقف فيه عند كثير من أهل الأصول لصيرورته مجملاً، ولا يصح التعلق به لإثبات حكم جار مجرى الفروع يمكن إثباته بما لا وجه إلى إثباتها إلا الدليل القاطع الموجب للعلم الثابت بطريق التيقن، وعند أكثر أهل الأصول وإن لم ينقلب مجملاً إلا أنه يجب الحكم فيما وراء المخصوص من أفراد العموم وأحاده بطريق التيقن، بل يوجب ذلك بطريق غالب الرأي بلا خلاف بيننا وبينكم على أن هذا عندكم حكم العام وإن لم يخص منه شيء، والتعلق بمثل هذا الدليل في الأبواب الاعتقادية جهل بمواضع الحجاج ومواقع الأدلة ومراتبها في أنفسها ودرجات المحال التي يتعلق بها فيها، ويتبين بهذا أن لا متعلق بالآية في محل النزاع وموضع الخلاف.
From non-existence to existence, so it is known by this that He, exalted, did not intend by this verse, although it came in the form of generality, except specificity. All that he verifies is that the essence of Allah is a thing without disagreement between us and you, and likewise His attributes are things according to many of you, but His essence and attributes were not included under this verse, because including them would void what the verse was intended for, of establishing that their inclusion under the address would invalidate the premise for which the address was made and would establish the opposite; thus their inclusion is impossible, even if the wording includes them from a linguistic perspective. And if nothing else were specified from what the wording includes, how can it be that other things have been specified which are equivalent to that other in the meaning that necessitates their exclusion from the purport of the speech? There is no doubt that excluding some of what the wording includes from its usage for a meaning that necessitates its exclusion necessitates the exclusion of whatever contains that meaning, because the statement demands equality in the ruling when equality in the meaning that necessitates it is established. Moreover, the general that has been specified becomes suspended according to many of the scholars of usul because it becomes ambiguous, and it is not valid to rely on it for establishing a ruling that falls under the category of subsidiary matters that can be established by something that has no way to be established except by a decisive proof that necessitates certain knowledge established by the path of certainty. According to most scholars of usul, even if it does not become ambiguous, it is nevertheless necessary to judge regarding what is beyond the specified individuals of the general and its singulars by the path of certainty; rather, it necessitates that by the path of predominant opinion without disagreement between us and you, provided that this is the ruling of the general among you even if nothing has been specified from it. And relying on such a proof in doctrinal matters is ignorance of the places of argumentation, the positions of proofs, their ranks in themselves, and the degrees of premises that are relied upon in them. And by this it becomes clear that there is no grounding in the verse in the place of dispute and point of disagreement.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents an argument refuting the claim that a general verse (such as 'Allah is the Creator of everything') includes God's own essence and attributes. The author begins by stating that the verse, though appearing general, was intended for a specific purpose (al-khusus). He then asserts the agreed point that God's essence is a thing, and attributes are things among many opponents, but such items cannot be included under the verse because inclusion would nullify the very premise the verse was meant to establish (namely, affirming creation of what is other than God). The author explains that even if the wording linguistically could cover them, the context (the verse's purpose) necessitates their exclusion. He then discusses the principle of specification (takhsis) and its implications: if an equivalent item is specified out, the same applies to others with the same meaning. Furthermore, the author notes that a general term that has been specified becomes ambiguous (mujmal) according to many scholars of usul, and such a general cannot be used to establish rulings in theological matters that require certainty. Even among those who do not consider it ambiguous, they require certainty for what remains after specification. The author concludes that relying on such a general argument in doctrinal matters is ignorance of proper argumentative method, and thus the verse provides no basis for the disputed point.

Key Terms
تخصيص
مجمل
يقين
دليل قاطع
أبواب اعتقادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think a specified general can still be used as a certain proof in theology.
Correction: The author shows that either it becomes ambiguous or requires certainty, and many hold it cannot be used for theological proofs.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of a specified general according to many usul scholars?
A) It remains fully general.
B) It becomes ambiguous.
C) It becomes specific.
D) It is invalidated.

Key Takeaway
A general term that has been specified cannot provide certain evidence in doctrinal matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever settled on a theological belief based on a general verse without checking if it has been specified? Will you now examine your core beliefs to ensure they rest on decisive proof rather than ambiguous generalities?
?
When explaining Islamic teachings to others, do you rely on verses that may have been qualified or specified, thereby potentially misleading? Commit to only using clear, certain proofs in matters of creed.
?
How comfortable are you with the idea that your faith must be built on certainty, not mere probability? Are you willing to abandon any belief that cannot be established by a 'decisive proof' even if it is widely held?

Refuting the Analogy Between Shahid and Gha'ib
Nasafi challenges the opponent's analogy between witnessed and unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy between witnessed human actions and God's actions is invalid and leads to anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
What is blameworthy in humans is not necessarily blameworthy in God.

Nasafi challenges the opponent's method of drawing analogies between the witnessed world (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib). He demands that the opponent specify the exact meaning that makes an action ugly and then check if it applies to God. Using a concrete example, he exposes the absurdity of equating God's actions with human actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the rhetorical questions at the start.
2
Identify the core challenge
Nasafi asks: 'By what meaning is this deemed ugly? Does that meaning apply to God?' This frames the entire refutation.
The opponent must specify the cause (ma'na) of ugliness.
3
Understand the concrete example
Nasafi gives the example of God seeing fornicators and not preventing them, compared to a human master. This illustrates the flaw in the analogy.
Consider the roles of ability, knowledge, and prevention.
4
Analyze the dilemma
Nasafi forces the opponent into a choice: either call God foolish (safih) or admit the human is not foolish—both undermine the analogy.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
5
Conclude with the invalidity
The passage ends by declaring the analogy invalid and exposing the opponent's ignorance. Reflect on how this defends God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تجمعون بين الشاهد والغائب؟ وبأي معنى قبح هذا أمن المعترض لننظر فيه أن ذلك المعنى هل وجد في حق الموجد ليحكم بالتسوية بينهما أو لم يوجد ليمتنع عن التسوية بينهما ؟ وهل هذا منكم إلا التحكم والتشهي ومجرد التشبيه للموجد بالمعترض، وأنى لكم هذا؟ ومن أين تزعمون أن ما قبح في الشاهد يقبح في الغائب؟ أليس أن الله تعالى يرى الزانيين ويعلم بحالهما ويقدر على منعهما ثم لا يمنع عن ذلك جبرًا بل يخلق القدرة على الفعل والشدة في الآلة، ولولا القدرة والشدة لما تصور منهما هذا الفعل؟ في الشاهد من يعلم بزنا عبده بأمته ويقف على ذلك ويمتنع عن منعهما عن ذلك مع القدرة عليه يعد سفيهًا، وإن لم يتول إعطاء القدرة على ذلك وتهيئة أسبابه، أفتزعمون أن الله تعالى سفيه كما أن العبد سفيه؟ أو تزعمون أن العبد ليس بسفيه كما أن الله تعالى ليس بسفيه ؟ فبأي الجوابين أجابوا انسلخوا عن الدين وإن زعموا يوصف بسبب ذلك الصنع بالسفه، والله تعالى لا يوصف به بل هو الحكيم الذي لا يجوز عليه السفه قيل لهم: بطلت تسويتكم إذ بين الشاهد والغائب وظهرت جهالتكم.
Do you draw an analogy between the witnessed (al-shahid) and the unseen (al-gha'ib)? And by what meaning is this deemed ugly? Is it from the opponent so that we look into it: does that meaning exist with respect to the Creator (al-mujid) to rule for equality between them, or does it not exist to prevent equality between them? Is this from you anything but arbitrary judgment and caprice and mere anthropomorphism (tashbih) of the Creator to the opponent? And how could you claim that? From where do you assert that what is ugly in the witnessed is ugly in the unseen? Does not God see the two fornicators, know their condition, and have power to prevent them, yet He does not prevent them by compulsion, but rather creates the ability for the act and the intensity in the instrument? Without ability and intensity, this act would not be possible from them. In the witnessed, one who knows of his slave's fornication with his slave-woman and refrains from preventing them, despite having the power, is considered foolish (safih). Even if he did not give them the ability and prepare its means, do you claim that God is foolish as the slave is foolish? Or do you claim that the slave is not foolish as God is not foolish? By whichever answer they respond, they strip themselves of religion. If they claim that He is described due to that act with foolishness, while God is not described with it, but rather He is the Wise (al-hakim) upon whom foolishness is impossible. It was said to them: Your analogy is invalidated, then, between the witnessed and the unseen, and your ignorance has become apparent.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponent's use of analogy between the witnessed (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib). Nasafi begins with rhetorical questions challenging the validity of the analogy: 'Do you draw an analogy between the witnessed and the unseen?' He demands that the opponent specify the exact meaning (ma'na) that makes an action ugly, and then examine whether that meaning applies to God. He accuses them of arbitrary judgment and mere anthropomorphism. Then he presents a concrete example: God sees fornicators, knows their state, and has power to prevent them, yet He does not prevent them by force but creates ability and strong desire in their limbs. In the witnessed world, a master who allows his slaves to fornicate despite being able to stop them would be considered foolish (safih). But God is not subject to such judgment. Nasafi then presents a dilemma: if the opponent says God is foolish like the human, they blaspheme; if they say the human is not foolish like God, then they admit the analogy fails. Thus, the conclusion is that the opponent's analogy is invalid and their ignorance is exposed.

Key Terms
الشاهد (al-shahid)
الغائب (al-gha'ib)
السفه (al-safah)
التسوية (al-taswiya)
التشبيه (al-tashbih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: What is considered ugly or blameworthy in human actions is necessarily also ugly in God's actions.
Correction: God is not subject to human criteria; His actions are judged by His own wisdom and transcendence. The analogy fails because the context and nature differ fundamentally.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of applying the analogy between shahid and gha'ib to God's actions, according to the passage?
A) It proves God's wisdom
B) It leads to calling God foolish
C) It shows God's justice
D) It is consistent with revelation

Key Takeaway
The analogy between witnessed and unseen is invalid because the criteria for blame in humans do not necessarily apply to God, as shown by the example of the fornicators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even in your heart, questioned God's wisdom when you saw evil or suffering in the world? How does this lesson confront that doubt, and what do you need to say to God right now?
?
The opponent was accused of 'arbitrary judgment and caprice' (التحكم والتشهي). Where in your life do you judge God by human standards, expecting Him to act as you would? Are you willing to submit to His wisdom even when you do not understand?
?
Nasafi says that if the opponent claims God is foolish, they 'strip themselves of religion.' Have your thoughts about God ever subtly slipped into blaming Him for not preventing something? Repent right now and affirm that God is al-Hakim, upon whom foolishness is impossible.

Why Slander in the Witnessed World Doesn't Apply to the Unseen
Nasafi explains why the analogy between shahid and gha'ib fails.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy between human slander and any possible accusation against God fails, due to the difference in the availability of proofs.

Core Idea
Human slander harms because proofs are hidden; God's proofs are clear.

Nasafi continues his argument against analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen by examining the specific case of slander. In the human realm, a slandered person may suffer lasting harm because people may believe the false accusation, and clear proofs of innocence are not always apparent. This vulnerability does not apply to God, whose transcendence is established by overwhelming evidence. Thus, the analogy breaks down.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the vivid description of the slanderer's consequences.
Focus on the phrase 'وإنه يظهر لدى السامعين كذب الشاتم' – this shows the dual outcome.
2
Identify the key contrast
Notice that the passage describes two possibilities: the slanderer's lie is apparent, yet hearers may still believe it. This is the core of the distinction from the divine case.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: why would this slander scenario not apply to God? The commentary will connect it to God's clear proofs.
Think about the concept of 'al-ma'na al-mujib' – the reason necessitating the distinction.
4
Engage with the checkpoint
Answer the quiz question to test your understanding of the lesson's main point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن المعترض في الشاهد للافتراء عليه الحامل غيره على شتمه لم يقم في العقول دلالة تنزهه عما قرن به ونسب إليه من الرذيلة، وإنه يظهر لدى السامعين كذب الشاتم له المفتري عليه ويلحق به عار كذبه وبهته، وعسى السامعون أو أكثرهم أن يصدقوا المفتري فيما افترى فتحط رتبة المشتوم في أعينهم وتنخفض درجته لديهم ويلتحق به عار ربما لا يغسله عنه مرور الأيام وتطاول مدد الزمان بل تبقى وصمة ذلك على الأعقاب على مر الدهور والأحقاب فكان المعترض لذلك متعرضاً لما يكسبه على الدهر ويجلب إليه وإلى ذريته شين الأبد، ولا شك أن من هذا غب فعله وعاقبة صنيعه بلغ في السفه غاية لا يدانيه سفيه فيها وإن بالغ في سفهه وتمادى في طغيانه.
Indeed, the one who is slandered in the witnessed (al-shahid), the one who incites another to curse him, has no proof established in minds to exonerate him from the vice that is combined with him and attributed to him. The falseness of the one who curses him and slanders him becomes apparent to the hearers, and the shame of his lie and slander attaches to him. Yet perhaps the hearers, or most of them, believe the slanderer in his slander, so the rank of the slandered one falls in their eyes and his status declines in their estimation, and shame attaches to him that perhaps the passage of days and lengthening of times may not wash away; rather, the stain of that remains on his descendants through the ages and epochs. So the one who objects thereby exposes himself to what earns him eternal (consequences) and brings to himself and his offspring everlasting disgrace. There is no doubt that one whose action and consequence are like this reaches a degree of foolishness (safah) that no fool can reach, even if he exaggerates in his foolishness and persists in his transgression.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage explains the underlying reason (al-ma'na al-mujib) for the necessary distinction between the witnessed and the unseen. Nasafi describes the situation of a human objector (mu'tarid) who falsely accuses someone: in the witnessed world, the slandered person lacks a clear proof to exonerate himself in the minds of the audience. Although the liar's falsehood becomes evident, many listeners may still believe the slander, causing lasting shame that may even affect his descendants. Such a slanderer reaches the ultimate degree of foolishness (safah). The key point is that in the human realm, reputation can be harmed by slander because proofs of innocence are not always apparent to all. In contrast, God has provided clear and overwhelming proofs of His transcendence, so no such harm can occur. Thus, the analogy between shahid and gha'ib breaks down because the conditions are fundamentally different.

Key Terms
المعترض (al-mu'tarid)
الافتراء (al-iftira')
الشين (al-shayn)
العار (al-'ar)
السفه (al-safah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the harm from slander applies equally to God as to humans.
Correction: In the human realm, slander can cause lasting harm because proofs of innocence are not always clear to all. But God has provided overwhelming and clear proofs of His transcendence, so no accusation can harm Him.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is a key difference between a human witness and God in the context of slander?
A) The human witness can always clear himself of slander
B) God is not subject to any accusation because He has no creation
C) The human witness may suffer lasting shame because people may believe the slanderer, while God has clear proofs of His innocence
D) The human witness never experiences shame

Key Takeaway
The vulnerability to slander in the human realm stems from the absence of universally clear proofs, which is not the case for God, whose transcendence is evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a rumor about someone, do you pause to consider that their side of the story is hidden from you? How will you guard yourself from being a cause of their fall, knowing the severe harm slander causes?

This passage highlights that the stain of slander can persist for generations. Have you ever spoken ill of someone in a way that could taint your family's honor? What repentance is needed today to break that cycle?

Nasafi's Critique of Carrying a Word on Both Meanings
Nasafi dismisses a weak theological compromise regarding Quranic interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi rejects the idea that a homonym can bear both possible meanings at once without further evidence.

Core Idea
A word with two meanings cannot simply cover both at once without evidence.

Nasafi here addresses a proposed solution from some theologians who try to reconcile conflicting interpretations by claiming the word can mean both. He critically evaluates this as methodologically weak, showing the importance of precise linguistic reasoning in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structure: a proposed answer followed by Nasafi's evaluation.
The word 'المشترك' refers to a homonym—a word with multiple meanings.
2
Identify the two meanings and the solution
The passage mentions two possible meanings: the thing acted upon (المعمول) and the action (العمل). Some theologians suggested carrying the word on both together.
3
Understand Nasafi's criticism
Nasafi calls this response weak (وهاء) because it treats a homonym as inclusive of all meanings, which is not a sound method (غير سديد). Ask yourself why this is problematic.
In kalam methodology, a word can only bear multiple meanings simultaneously if there is clear evidence (قرينة) indicating that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعض المتكلمين أجاب أن الآية لما احتملت الأمرين أعني أن هذه اللفظة لما كانت تذكر ويراد بها المعمول، وتذكر ويراد بها العمل حملت عليهما جميعًا، ولكن فيه وهاء لما فيه من جعل المشترك مشتملاً وهو غير سديد.
And some theologians answered that since the verse admitted both meanings, I mean that this word is sometimes used intending the thing acted upon and sometimes used intending the action, so it is carried upon both together, but in it there is weakness because of what is in it of making the homonym inclusive, and it is not sound.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents a proposed answer from some theologians (المتكلمين) who suggest that the word 'ما تعملون' can be interpreted as carrying both meanings simultaneously: the thing acted upon (المعمول) and the action (العمل). They argue that since the word admits both, it should be taken to include both. The author evaluates this response as weak (وهاء) because it treats a homonym (المشترك) as if it can encompass both meanings at once, which is not a sound (غير سديد) method of interpretation. This shows the author's commitment to precise linguistic reasoning.

Key Terms
المتكلمين
المشترك
حمل
وهاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming a word with multiple possible meanings can bear all at once without evidence is unsound.
Correction: A homonym requires contextual evidence (قرينة) to determine which meaning is intended; it cannot simply be assumed to cover both simultaneously.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi consider the answer of some theologians weak?
A) Because it denies that the word has multiple meanings.
B) Because it improperly treats a homonym as inclusive of all meanings simultaneously.
C) Because it rejects the literal meaning of the verse.
D) Because it agrees with the Mu'tazila.

Key Takeaway
A homonym cannot be carried on both meanings at once without contextual evidence; doing so is methodologically weak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever comforted yourself with a broad interpretation of a Quranic verse that you knew deep down was not supported by context? How does Nasafi's warning about weak reasoning challenge you to be honest about your own assumptions?
?
In your study of Islamic theology, are you willing to accept a comfortable but weak interpretation because it avoids difficulty? This passage calls such reasoning 'unsound'. Where in your faith are you guilty of this?
?
When you encounter an ambiguous statement about Allah's attributes, do you default to saying it means both literal and metaphorical? How does this lesson demand you to seek evidence rather than assume inclusivity?

Overview of Remaining Chapters on Kasb, Evil, and Shirk
Nasafi enumerates the remaining three chapters and explains their scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the topics of the remaining chapters of Nasafi's work on acquisition, evil, and polytheism.

Core Idea
Nasafi's remaining chapters cover the nature of acquisition, the goodness of evil's creation, and the essence of polytheism.

As we approach the final sections of the book, Nasafi provides a roadmap of the next three chapters. These chapters address subtle theological issues: whether we need to define 'acquisition,' how evil actions relate to the Creator, and what polytheism truly means. This lesson orients you to these topics before diving into the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the numbered sequence and the author's phrasing.
The passage is a simple list, so focus on the key terms.
2
Identify the three chapter topics
Identify what each of the three chapters (fourth, fifth, sixth) will cover, based on the author's description.
The fourth chapter deals with kasb, the fifth with evil, the sixth with shirk.
3
Examine the methodological conclusion
Read the final sentence where Nasafi explains his approach: he will discuss the views of kalām masters and earlier imams.
This shows his reliance on authoritative sources.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why these topics are important in kalām: they address human action, divine justice, and the definition of shirk.
These are foundational issues in Maturidi theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع: في أن لا حاجة بنا إلى بيان مائية الكسب ثم الاشتغال بذلك على طريق المساهلة. والخامس: في أن إيجاد القبيح ليس بقبيح، وتحصيل ما هو مذموم في نفسه ليس بمذموم كإيجاد السفه والظلم والكفر وغير ذلك، وما يتصل بهذا الفعل من بيان مائية الحكمة والسفه والعدل والجور. والسادس: في بيان مائية الشرك. ونتكلم في كل فصل من هذه الفصول عن بعض ما تكلم به أرباب الكلام وأئمة أهل الإسلام من سلفنا رحمهم الله إن شاء الله تعالى.
And the fourth: concerning that we have no need to explain the quiddity of acquisition (al-kasb) then engage in that in an easy manner. And the fifth: concerning that bringing evil into existence is not evil, and attaining what is blameworthy in itself is not blameworthy, like bringing into existence foolishness, injustice, unbelief, and other than that, and what pertains to this act from explaining the quiddity of wisdom, foolishness, justice, and injustice. And the sixth: concerning explaining the quiddity of polytheism (al-shirk). And we will speak in each chapter of these chapters about some of what was said by the masters of kalām (arbāb al-kalām) and the leaders of the people of Islam from our predecessors, may Allah have mercy on them, if Allah the Exalted wills.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a listing of the remaining chapters of the book. The author, Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, enumerates three chapters: the fourth will discuss that there is no need to explain the quiddity of kasb (acquisition) and then proceed to it in an easy manner; the fifth will discuss that bringing evil into existence is not evil, and attaining what is blameworthy in itself is not blameworthy, giving examples like foolishness, injustice, unbelief; and it will also explain the quiddity of wisdom, foolishness, justice, and injustice. The sixth chapter will explain the quiddity of shirk (polytheism). The author then states that in each of these chapters he will discuss some of what was said by the masters of kalām and the leaders of the Ahl al-Islam from the predecessors, by Allah's will.

Key Terms

قبيح
شرك
مائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because God creates evil actions, God is evil or blameworthy.
Correction: The passage asserts that 'bringing evil into existence is not evil' (إيجاد القبيح ليس بقبيح), indicating that creation of evil is not a moral flaw in the Creator.

Checkpoint
What is the topic of the fourth chapter according to this passage?
A) The quiddity of polytheism
B) That we have no need to explain the quiddity of kasb
C) That bringing evil into existence is evil
D) The explanation of the quiddity of wisdom

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's remaining chapters cover key theological concepts: acquisition (kasb), the moral status of evil's creation, and the nature of shirk.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Have you ever delayed accepting a core Islamic teaching because you felt you needed a full philosophical explanation first (like understanding the quiddity of kasb)? Nasafi states we have no need. How does this challenge your intellectual pride? What step will you take to submit to Allah's word even without complete comprehension?

Methodological Advice for the Mutakallim
The author advises the mutakallim to adhere to the two proofs and refute objections by presenting them to the proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's methodological advice for the mutakallim in dealing with objections about divine power and human action.

Core Idea
Adhere to the two proofs and refer all objections to them.

After establishing the two key proofs regarding divine power of invention and human action, the author now provides practical guidance for the theologian (mutakallim). He emphasizes a disciplined methodology: hold fast to the proofs and counter objections by comparing them to these proofs rather than engaging each one separately. This lesson unpacks that advice.

Guided Path
1

Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's words sink in.
Notice the two proofs mentioned.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the two proofs the author says the mutakallim must adhere to. What are they about?
One is about impossibility of invention power for others, the other about the slave's act.
3
Understand the method of refutation
The author says to repel objections by presenting them to the two proofs. What does this mean? Reflect on why this method is effective.
This avoids getting lost in individual arguments.

He should not be heedless of that but rather stay alert so that disentanglement from the objections they raise becomes easy, and most of what they present as difficulties (ishkāl) becomes attainable through the success (tawfīq) of Allah the Exalted.

Commentary
This passage serves as a methodological conclusion to the discussion. Any objections from opponents should be dealt with by 'presenting them to the two proofs' – that is, measuring them against these established proofs. The mutakallim must remain vigilant (yataqayyaẓ) and not be heedless, so that he can easily disentangle himself from the opponents' specious arguments. Ultimately, success (tawfīq) comes from Allah.

Accepting Proof Beyond Imagination
Imam Nasafi argues that accepting the Creator by proof obligates accepting other proofs beyond imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why intellectual proofs are necessary for knowing God, even though He cannot be imagined.

Core Idea
Proof leads to certainty beyond imagination.

Imam Nasafi tackles a common error: trying to understand God through imagination. He shows that the Creator is beyond imagination, so relying on imagination leads to denial. Instead, we must follow proof (dalil) even when the object is not imaginable. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Get a feel for the author's flow.
2
Contrast imagination and intellect
Notice how the author distinguishes between wahm (imagination) and 'aql (intellect). Imagination deals with sensual things; intellect goes beyond.
The soul and intellect themselves are not imaginable.
3
Understand the consequence of relying on imagination
The author says that if one relies only on imagination, the first thing denied is the Creator, because the Creator is not a substance, body, or accident.
This leads to rejecting rational knowledge of God.
4
Grasp the obligation to follow proof
The conclusion: whoever accepts the Creator by proof must accept other truths proven by proof, even if not imaginable.
This establishes the authority of intellectual proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محيط بثبوت الروح في البدن ووجود العقل فيه، وإن كان لا يتقرر )(۱) في الوهم معنى تحيا به هذه الأعضاء ولا معنى يوقف به على ما غاب عن الحواس من حقائق الأشياء لخروجه عن الحواس، وقد مر أن من رام أن يعرف بالوهم ما سبيل (۲) معرفته العقل فقد حاد عن الطريق، ومن يتبع الوهم ولم ( يفقد ) (3) الدليل فيما يجره إلى معرفته لخروجه عن الوهم، فأول ما يلزمه إنكاره ثبوت الصانع إذ لا تصور في الوهم لما ليس بجوهر ولا جسم ولا عرض ولا قائم بنا ولا بجهة من الجهات منا ولا اتصال له بنا ولا انفصال له عنا، ويلزمه أن يخرج ثبوت الصانع عن العقل لخروجه عن الوهم، ويقول إنه ليس ثبوته بمعقول لما أنه ليس بموهوم، فمن أقر بثبوت الصانع اتباعًا للدليل وإن لم يتقرر ذلك في الوهم يلزمه الإقرار بما بينا ا من الدليل وإن لم يتصور ذلك في الوهم.
Comprehensive [knowledge] is about the establishment of the soul in the body and the existence of the intellect in it, even if there is not established in the imagination (wahm) a meaning by which these limbs live, nor a meaning by which one may be made aware of the realities of things that are absent from the senses because of its being beyond the senses. And it has passed that whoever seeks to know by imagination what the intellect's way of knowing is, has deviated from the path. And whoever follows the imagination and loses the proof (dalil) of what leads to its knowledge because of its being beyond imagination, the first thing incumbent upon him is his denial of the existence of the Creator (al-Sani'), since there is no conceptualization in the imagination for what is not a substance (jawhar), nor a body (jism), nor an accident ('ard), nor subsisting in us, nor in any direction from us, nor having connection with us, nor disconnection from us. And it becomes incumbent on him to exclude the existence of the Creator from the intellect because of its exclusion from imagination, and he says that its existence is not rational (ma'qul) because it is not imaginable (mawhum). So whoever affirms the existence of the Creator following the proof, even if it is not established in the imagination, it is incumbent on him to affirm what we have explained of the proof, even if that is not conceptualized in the imagination.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the proper method for knowing God. It begins by contrasting the roles of imagination (wahm) and intellect (aql). The author states that the reality of the soul and intellect are not graspable by imagination since they are beyond sensual perception. He then warns against relying on imagination to know what only intellect can know. He explains that if one follows imagination and abandons proof, the first consequence is denial of the Creator, because the Creator is not a substance, body, accident, or anything that can be pictured in the imagination. Such a person would then claim that the Creator cannot be known by intellect either, since they equate the rational with the imaginable. The conclusion is that one who accepts the Creator based on proof must also accept other truths proven by proof, even if they are not imaginable. The passage establishes the principle that intellectual proofs are authoritative even when the object is not perceptible to imagination.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that what is not imaginable cannot be rationally known.
Correction: The passage shows that many truths (like the soul) are not imaginable yet known through intellect/proof, and God is known by proof, not imagination.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Accepting the Creator by proof requires accepting other truths proven by proof, even if not imaginable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Mutakallim's Two Proofs and Vigilance
Imam Nasafi advises the mutakallim to adhere to two proofs and defend against doubts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two proofs a mutakallim must hold to (impossibility of invention for other than God, and establishing the act for the servant) and the importance of vigilance in refuting doubts.

Core Idea
The mutakallim's strength lies in holding two proofs and vigilant defense.

Imam Nasafi gives clear methodological advice for the theologian (mutakallim). He emphasizes two essential proofs that form the foundation for addressing the question of human action and divine power. The passage also stresses the need for constant vigilance to refute doubts and difficulties raised by opponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the direct instruction style.
The passage is a single continuous piece of advice.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the two proofs mentioned: the proof of impossibility of invention for other than God, and the proof establishing the act for the servant.
These are the pillars the mutakallim must hold fast to.
3
Understand the method of defense
The mutakallim must repel opponents' doubts by presenting them to these two proofs. This means using the proofs as a criterion to evaluate and refute objections.
Vigilance is key; the mutakallim should not be heedless.
4
Reflect on the role of divine help
The passage ends by attributing success in deliverance from doubts to God. Recognize the balance between human effort and divine assistance.
Tawfiq Allah (success from God) is essential.

اتباعًا لما فالمتكلم في هذه المسألة ينبغي أن يتمسك بما ذكرت من الدليل القائم على استحالة ثبوت قدرة الاختراع لغير الله تعالى، والدليل القائم على ثبوت الفعل للعبد، ويدفع ما يورده الخصوم من الشبه بالعرض على الدليلين، ولا يغفل عن ذلك بل يتيقظ ليسهل التخلص عما يوردون من الشبه وجل ما يوجهون من الإشكال بتوفيق الله تعالى.

And he should not be heedless of that, but rather be vigilant so that deliverance from what they bring of doubts and most of what they direct of difficulties becomes easy, with the success of God Almighty.

The mutakallim must then defend these proofs by presenting the opponents' doubts unto them and refuting them. The passage emphasizes vigilance and attentiveness to ensure that the mutakallim can successfully counter the objections and difficulties raised by opponents, attributing success to God.
