Refuting Mu'tazila's Analogy: Divine Acts vs Human Standards
Refutes Mu'tazila by showing God's acts contradict their analogy from the witnessed world

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila's argument from analogy between the witnessed world and the unseen fails when applied to divine actions.

Core Idea
God's acts break the rules of human ethics.

The Mu'tazila argued that since actions like causing evil are evil in human terms, they must also be evil for God. The author counters by showing that God performs actions that would be considered evil in the witnessed world, yet from God they are not. This lesson examines the specific counterexamples and the conclusion that the analogy is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the argument unfold without interruption.
Notice the strong opening: 'Thus the Mu'tazila were refuted...'
2
Identify the counterexamples
Find the two examples given: the slave committing adultery with his slave-woman, and the disbeliever given power to insult God. How do these challenge the analogy?
Both involve God creating power for actions He disapproves of.
3
Understand the reasoning
The author states that such actions would be 'evil' (qabih) in the witnessed world, but from God they are 'good' (hasan). This shows a fundamental difference between the two realms.
The key is that divine standards differ from human standards.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that the distinction (mufaraqa) between shahid and gha'ib invalidates the Mu'tazila's inference and equating of the two.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنوقضت المعتزلة بما بينا أن الباري يرى عبده يزني بأمته ويخلى بينهما مع القدرة على المنع، ولا يكتفى بذلك بل يخلق قدرة ذلك الفعل والشدة في الآلة، ويعطي الكافر قدرة مع علمه أنه يشتمه بها ويعتدي عليه ومثل هذا في الشاهد قبيح، وقد حسن ذلك منه ووقعت المفارقة بين الشاهد والغائب، فبطل بذلك استشهادهم وتسويتهم بين الشاهد والغائب
Thus the Mu'tazila were refuted by what we have shown: that the Creator sees His slave committing adultery with his slave-woman and leaves them alone despite ability to prevent, and does not suffice with that, but rather creates the power for that act and the intensity in the instrument, and gives the disbeliever power while knowing that he will insult Him with it and transgress against Him. And the likes of this in the witnessed (al-shahid) is evil, yet it has been rendered good from Him, and the distinction between the witnessed and the unseen (al-gha'ib) occurred. Thus their inference and their equating between the witnessed and the unseen are nullified.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's argument from analogy between the witnessed world (al-shahid) and the unseen (al-gha'ib). The author presents counterexamples from divine acts: God sees a slave committing adultery with his slave-woman and does not prevent him, even giving him the power and physical capacity to do so. Similarly, God gives the disbeliever the power to insult and transgress against Him. In human terms, such actions would be evil, but from God they are not. This shows a fundamental difference between the witnessed and the unseen, invalidating the Mu'tazila's use of analogy to argue that creating evil is evil for God. The conclusion is that their inference (istishhad) and equating between the two realms are nullified.

Key Terms
الشاهد
الغائب
المفارقة
الاستشهاد
القبيح
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument rejects all analogy between God and creation, making divine actions entirely arbitrary and unknowable.
Correction: The argument only rejects the specific Mu'tazilite claim that creating evil must be evil for God because it would be evil in human terms. It does not deny analogies about other attributes like knowledge or power.

Checkpoint
What specific counterexample does the author use to refute the Mu'tazila?
A) God giving a thief the ability to steal.
B) God allowing a slave to commit adultery with his slave-woman and giving him power to do so.
C) God creating a pig and declaring it evil.
D) God preventing a believer from sinning.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's argument fails because what is evil in human terms is not necessarily evil for God; thus the analogy between shahid and gha'ib is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see evil in the world, how often do you assume God must act according to your sense of justice? What does this lesson demand you reconsider about your own judgments of God's actions?
?
If God's giving a disbeliever the power to insult Him is considered good (hasan) from Him, how should that transform your attitude towards those who oppose you? Are you willing to trust that God's plan may involve apparent evil for a greater good?
?
This lesson forces you to admit that your moral intuition is not the measure of God. In what specific area of your life are you still using your own standard to evaluate what God should or should not do? What will it cost you to surrender that?

Al-Maturidi's Concession and the Objection on Truth and Falsehood
Explains al-Maturidi's mention of jihaat as concession and refutes the truth/falsehood objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Maturidi mentioned aspects of action as a concession and how the objection about truth and falsehood is answered using the principle that essence (dhāt) is the cause of description, not relation (iḍāfa).

Core Idea
Aspects are a dialectical tool, not a theological necessity.

Here we see al-Maturidi's strategic use of the theory of aspects. He did not need it for his own doctrine but employed it to engage opponents. An objection is raised that this leads to contradictions like one thing being both truth and falsehood. The answer shows that differences arise from relations, not essence, thereby preserving consistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the purpose
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Observe that al-Maturidi mentioned aspects (jihāt) not out of need but as a concession (musāhala) and to refute falsehood comprehensively.
This sets the dialectical context.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection: if one action can be evil from one agent and good from another, then one thing could be both truth and falsehood. Understand why this is seen as a problem.
The objectors assume that moral qualities are absolute, not relative.
3
Follow the three analogies
The answer gives three analogies: obedience to God/disobedience to Satan, progress/delay, act/omission. Notice how each shows that a single thing can have opposing descriptions relative to different relations.
These analogies are drawn from the objectors' own accepted examples.
4
Grasp the final principle
Read the last sentence: what a thing deserves by its essence does not vary with relation. The essence is the cause. So descriptions based on essence are fixed; relational descriptions can differ without contradiction.
This principle resolves the objection and is foundational in Maturidi kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشيخنا أبو منصور الماتريدي ذكر جهات الفعل لا لحاجته إلى ذلك بل على طريق المساهلة، وتصحيحا للمذهب وإبطالاً للباطل من جميع الوجوه. فإن قالوا على من قال من القائلين بجهات الفعل لو جاز أن يكون شيء واحد شرًا من فاعل خيرًا من فاعل، جورًا من فاعل عدلاً من فاعل، لجاز أن يكون شيء صدقًا كذبًا. قيل: لو لزمنا ذلك بإثباتنا ما أثبتنا لزمكم أيضا أن تجعلوا شيئًا واحدًا صدقًا من فاعل كذبًا من فاعل قياسًا على إثباتكم واحدا طاعة الله تعالى معصية لغيره؛ لأن الطاعة له معصية للشيطان، وكذا قياسًا على إثباتكم شيئًا واحدًا تقدمًا إلى مكان تأخرًا عن غيره، وقياسًا على إثباتكم شيئًا هو فعل لشيء ترك لغيره، فإن لم يلزمكم ذلك لم يلزمنا، وحقيقة هذا الكلام أن ما كان يستحقه الشيء من الوصف لذاته لا يختلف باختلاف الإضافة لأن علة استحقاقه ذاته، وذاته لا تختلف باختلاف الإضافة.
And our master Abū Manṣūr al-Māturīdī mentioned the aspects of action (jihāt al-fiʿl) not out of his need for that, but by way of facility (musāhala), and to correct the doctrine and to invalidate falsehood from all aspects. For if they say to those who speak of aspects of action: if it is permissible that a single thing be evil from one agent and good from another, injustice from one agent and justice from another, then it would be permissible that a thing be truth and falsehood. It is said: if that were necessary for us by our affirming what we have affirmed, then it would also be necessary for you that you make a single thing truth from one agent and falsehood from another, by analogy to your affirming a single thing as obedience to God and disobedience to other than Him, because obedience to Him is disobedience to Satan; and likewise by analogy to your affirming a single thing as progress (taqaddum) towards a place and delay (taʾakhkhur) from another; and by analogy to your affirming a thing as an act (fiʿl) for something and an omission (tark) for another. If that does not follow for you, then it does not follow for us. And the reality of this speech is that what a thing deserves of description by virtue of its essence (dhāt) does not differ with difference of relation (iḍāfa), because the cause of its deserving is its essence, and its essence does not differ with difference of relation.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage addresses two connected topics. First, the author notes that Shaykh Abū Manṣūr al-Māturīdī (the eponym of the Maturidi school) mentioned the theory of 'aspects of action' (jihāt al-fiʿl) not because he needed it for his own doctrine, but as a concession (musāhala) to opponents and as a comprehensive refutation of falsehood. Second, the passage presents an objection raised against proponents of jihāt: if one action can be both evil from one agent and good from another, or unjust from one and just from another, then by the same logic one thing could be both truth and falsehood. The answer is given with a counter-objection: the objectors themselves admit that a single thing (e.g., an act) can be obedience to God and disobedience to Satan simultaneously, or progress in one direction and delay in another, or an act for one and omission for another. If they accept these without contradiction, then the same principle applies to truth/falsehood. The key point is that the description a thing deserves by its own essence (dhāt) is invariant; differences arise from relations (iḍāfāt), not from the essence itself. Thus, an action's moral quality (good/evil) can differ by relation without contradiction, just as truth/falsehood could theoretically differ by relation if the essence allowed. The answer shows that the objection fails because it ignores the role of relations.

Key Terms
الماتريدي
مساهلة
جهات الفعل
صدق
كذب
ذات
إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Maturidi fully adopted the theory of aspects of action as part of his theological system.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that he mentioned aspects 'not out of need' but as a concession and to invalidate falsehood. It was a dialectical strategy, not a doctrinal commitment.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the cause of a thing's deserving a particular description?
A) The agent who performs it
B) The relation (iḍāfa) it bears to something else
C) Its own essence (dhāt)
D) The time in which it occurs

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi used jihāt as a concession, and the answer to the truth/falsehood objection hinges on the principle that descriptions based on essence are invariant, while relational descriptions can legitimately differ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own debates about religion, have you ever used arguments you didn't fully believe in just to win a point? How does al-Maturidi's concession challenge you to examine your sincerity?
?
When you judge someone's action as good or evil, do you consider the agent's intention and context, or do you apply absolute labels based on your own perspective? How can you train yourself to see the relational nature of such descriptions?
?
The passage distinguishes between descriptions based on essence and those based on relation. Are there areas in your life where you confuse the two—for example, assuming that because something is true for you, it must be true in essence for everyone? What will you do about it this week?

Two Aspects and Four Meanings of Divine Will
Al-Maturidi presents two methodological approaches and four semantic categories of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Mansur al-Maturidi's method of addressing questions about divine will by distinguishing between absolute affirmation and context-sensitive restriction, and the four distinct meanings of the term 'will' (mashī'a).

Core Idea
Divine will has multiple meanings; confusion arises when they are conflated.

When discussing divine will, theologians must be careful not to conflate different meanings of the term. Abu Mansur al-Maturidi provides a methodological framework: either answer based on the common understanding or, if the questioner's intent is unclear, restrict the answer by distinguishing four distinct meanings of 'will'. This approach avoids anthropomorphism and preserves divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the structure: the two aspects are introduced first, then the four meanings are listed.
Notice how al-Maturidi begins with a conditional statement.
2
Identify the two aspects of answering
The passage says 'لها وجهان'. Find these two aspects in the text. The first is absolute affirmation based on common understanding; the second is restriction when the questioner's intent is unknown or contentious.
3
Examine the four meanings of mashī'a
Al-Maturidi lists four customary meanings of 'will'. List them: tamannī, amr/da'wa, riḍā, nafy al-ghalaba. Understand why each is or is not applicable to God.
The first is negated, the second and third are attributes of blameworthy actions, the fourth is affirmed.
4
Understand the link to creation of actions
Al-Maturidi notes that this issue is essentially the issue of creation of actions (khalaq al-af'āl). Reflect on why affirming that God's will is not overcome relates to God creating all actions.
This connects divine will to divine omnipotence and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال شيخنا أبو منصور : إذا سئلنا عن هذه المسألة فلها وجهان: أحدهما الإطلاق على المفهوم من الإرادة. والثاني: منع الإطلاق إذا لم يفهم مراد السائل أو خشي التعنت، فيه وهو أن يقال: إن للمشيئة معاني فيما يتعارف أحدها للتمني، والثاني الأمر والدعاء إليه، والثالث الرضا به، والرابع نفي الغلبة وخروج الفعل على ما يقدره ويريده. فالأول منفي في كل شيء، لأن التمني إرادة ما علم أنه لا يكون وهو ضعف أو إرادة ما شك في كونه وهو جهل. والثاني والثالث صفتان في كل فعل يذم عليه. والرابع به نقول، وقد أجمعنا نحن ومن خالفنا على معناه وإن اختلفنا في اللفظ. ثم هذه المسألة في الحقيقة عين مسألة خلق الأفعال فإنا إذا أثبتنا بالدليل أن الله تعالى خالق أفعال العباد كفرًا كان أو معصية أو طاعة ، والله تعالى مختار في
Then our teacher Abu Mansur said: If we are asked about this issue, it has two aspects: One is absolute affirmation based on the understood meaning of will. The second is restricting the absolute affirmation if the intent of the questioner is not understood, or if contentiousness is feared. And it is that one should say: This ‘will’ (mashī'a) has customary meanings: one of them is wish (tamannī), the second is command and invitation to it, the third is approval (riḍā) of it, and the fourth is negation of being overcome and the action occurring according to what He decrees and wills. The first is negated in every case, because wish is willing what one knows will not occur, which is weakness, or willing what one doubts will occur, which is ignorance. The second and third are attributes of every blameworthy action. The fourth is what we affirm, and we and those who oppose us agree on its meaning, even if we differ in wording. Then this issue is in reality the very issue of the creation of actions. For if we establish by evidence that God is the creator of the actions of servants, whether disbelief, sin, or obedience, and God is the chooser in...
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage contains the teaching of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi on how to answer questions about divine will. He says there are two aspects: one can answer simply based on the common understanding of 'will', or one can restrict the answer if the questioner's intent is unclear or if contentious argument is feared. He then explains the second aspect by defining the four customary meanings of 'mashī'a': (1) wish (tamannī), desiring what one knows will not happen, indicating weakness or ignorance; (2) command (amr) and invitation; (3) approval (riḍā); (4) negation of being overcome and the action occurring according to God's decree and will. The first meaning is negated from God in every case because it implies weakness or ignorance. The second and third meanings are attributed to every blameworthy action, so they do not apply to God. The fourth meaning is what Maturidis affirm, and both parties agree on this meaning even if they differ in wording. Finally, al-Maturidi notes that this issue is essentially the same as the issue of the creation of actions (khalaq al-af'āl), since both involve God's creative power over all actions. The text breaks off but the connection is clear.

Key Terms

التمني
الأمر
الرضا
نفي الغلبة
خلق الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'will' (mashī'a) for God is often confused with human wish (tamannī) or moral approval.
Correction: Al-Maturidi shows that wish implies weakness/ignorance and is not applicable to God, and approval is only for blameworthy actions. The correct meaning is negation of being overcome.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Mansur, which of the four meanings of mashī'a is affirmed for God?
A) التمني (wish)
B) الأمر (command)
C) الرضا (approval)
D) نفي الغلبة وخروج الفعل على ما يقدره ويريده (negation of being overcome and action occurring according to His decree and will)

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi's method of distinguishing meanings of 'will' preserves God's transcendence while affirming His creative power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that God wishes something will happen, as if He is hoping it might fail? How does this passage call you to purify your understanding of God's will from any trace of weakness?
?
When you sin, do you sometimes feel that God must not truly will that action because He commands against it? How does the fourth meaning of will (nafy al-ghalaba) challenge you to acknowledge His sovereignty even over your failures—and what does that demand of your repentance?
?
If God's will in the sense of effective decree means every moment of your life unfolds exactly as He ordains, what is one area where you have resisted accepting His plan? Will you surrender that to Him today, trusting that His will is not overcome by your circumstances?

The Invalidity of Refutation by Unadmitted Consequences
Nasafi argues that refutation by consequence fails if the opponent does not admit the consequence is invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why refuting an opponent by pointing to undesirable consequences is only valid if the opponent accepts those consequences as actually undesirable.

Core Idea
A refutation is only as strong as the opponent's admission that the consequence is bad.

This passage presents a critical methodological insight for theological debate: simply showing that a belief leads to something you consider bad is not enough. The refutation only works if the opponent also recognizes that consequence as bad. Otherwise, the argument is empty. Nasafi, quoting Ja'far ibn Harb, illustrates this with an example from the debate about prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Focus on the core argument: under what condition does refutation by consequence work?
Notice the rhetorical opening question from Ja'far ibn Harb.
2
Identify the condition for valid refutation
Locate the sentence that states the condition: the opponent must admit the consequence is corrupt. Understand why this is necessary for the refutation to have force.
3
Reflect on the broader implication
Ask yourself: how does this principle apply to theological debates you know? Why does this alert us to a common fallacy?
This connects to the learning objective about the invalidity of unadmitted consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جعفر بن حرب أي تنبيه يحصل لأولئك بهذا القول؟ وأي إلزام حجة بذلك؟ وأكثر ما في الباب أنك تقول - يشنع عليهم بذلك: إن على مقالتكم هذه لا ينفع بعث الأنبياء مبشرين ومنذرين ولا الدعاء إلى الله تعالى، وأن أولئك كانوا منكرين بعث الأنبياء والدعاء على ألسنتهم إلى الله تعالى وتشنيع مقالة بما يفضي إليه المقالة، وأهل المقالة يعتقدون صحته لا يجدي نفعا إنما ينفع ذلك أن لو كانت المقالة تفضي إلى أمر يعترف صاحب المقالة بفساده ويقر ببطلانه فيستدل عليه فيقال: لو كانت مقالتك حقًا لما أفضت إلى هذا الفاسد، إذ الصحيح لا ينتج الفاسد، وإنما الفاسد هو الذي ينتج الفاسد، فأما إذا كان صاحب المقالة لا يقر بفساد ما أفضت إليه مقالته لم يكن للاحتجاج بذلك عليه منفعة ولا فيه حكمة، فدل أن هذا فاسد دفعته إليه الحيرة.
Ja'far ibn Harb: 'What notification occurs for those people through this statement? And what obligation of proof is there in that? The most that can be said is that you say - you attack them by that: "Indeed, according to this thesis of yours, the sending of prophets as bringers of good news and warners is of no benefit, nor is calling to Allah Almighty, and those people were deniers of the sending of prophets and of the call on their tongues to Allah Almighty." And criticizing a statement by what it leads to, while the proponents of the statement believe it to be correct, does not avail any benefit. It only benefits if the statement leads to something the proponent admits is corrupt and acknowledges its falsehood, so that one argues against it by saying: "If your statement were true, it would not lead to this corrupt thing, for the sound does not produce the corrupt; only the corrupt produces the corrupt." But if the proponent does not admit the corruption of what his statement leads to, then there is no benefit in arguing against him by that, nor is there any wisdom in it. Thus it is shown that this [refutation] is corrupt, driven by perplexity.'
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a methodological argument about the invalidity of certain refutations. Nasafi begins by quoting Ja'far ibn Harb's question: what notification or obligation does this refutation provide? He explains that the only way a refutation by consequence works is if the opponent admits the consequence is corrupt. Otherwise, the refutation is useless. He illustrates with the example of accusing the opponent that their thesis nullifies prophethood, but if the opponent does not admit that is a problem, the accusation fails. Therefore, such refutations are themselves corrupt and driven by perplexity. The author follows the logical order: first the question, then the explanation of the condition for a valid refutation, then the conclusion.

Key Terms
تنبيه (notification)
إلزام (obligation/proof)
شنع (to attack/criticize)
فساد (corruption/invalidity)
الحيرة (perplexity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any refutation that shows undesirable consequences of a belief is automatically valid, even if the opponent does not accept those consequences as bad.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that refutation by consequence is only valid if the opponent admits the consequence is corrupt. Otherwise, the argument has no force.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, when is a refutation by pointing to consequences valid?
A) When the consequences are logically impossible.
B) When the opponent admits the consequences are corrupt and false.
C) When the refuter believes the consequences are bad.
D) When the consequences contradict the opponent's other beliefs.

Key Takeaway
A refutation by undesirable consequences is only effective if the opponent concedes the undesirability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever argued against someone's belief by pointing out what you consider an obvious negative consequence, only to find the other person didn't see it as negative? How did that reveal your own assumption that they would agree with you?
?
When you debate matters of faith, are you willing to admit that some of your own positions might lead to consequences you haven't fully considered? Or do you defend them without acknowledging possible contradictions?
?
If the effectiveness of your argument depends on the opponent's admission, how does that affect the way you prepare for discussions about aqeedah? Are you more concerned with winning or with mutual understanding?

Abu Hanifa's Rational Proof and the Refutation of the Worship Verse
Nasafi defends rational proof and refutes the worship verse with three interpretive arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi defends Abu Hanifa's rational method and refutes the apparent meaning of Quran 51:56 using interpretive arguments.

Core Idea
The worship verse does not prove obligation; it can mean servanthood or a command.

Imam Nasafi begins by affirming that the method reported from Abu Hanifa is purely rational proof, which only the obstinate reject. He then addresses a key objection: the verse 'I did not create jinn and mankind except to worship Me' (51:56). This verse is often used to argue that all must worship. Nasafi provides several interpretations to show that the verse does not support the opponent's claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the two main parts: the assertion about rational proof and the refutation of the verse.
2
Understand the first interpretation: 'servants' instead of 'worship'
Many interpreters read the verse as 'except to be servants to Me,' not 'except to worship Me.' This applies to all creatures, including children and the insane, who cannot perform voluntary worship. Thus the verse does not demand actual worship from everyone.
The Arabic word 'عباد' (servants) differs from 'عبادة' (worship).
3
Explore the second interpretation: a command to worship
Another interpretation is that the verse means 'except to command them to worship.' If so, it is a command, not a statement that all actually worship. This removes the opponent's proof that all must worship.
4
Consider the argument of specification (تخصيص)
Even if the verse means actual worship, it is already specified to exclude children and the insane. By the same logic, it can be specified to exclude disbelievers based on other proofs. Thus the verse applies only to those who will believe and worship.
This shows the opponent cannot use the verse as a universal proof.
5
Reflect on the importance for rational method
This refutation shows that scriptural proofs must be interpreted in light of rational principles. Nasafi demonstrates that the verse does not contradict rational proof; rather, it can be understood in multiple ways that all support the rational method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتبين بأن هذه الطريقة المحكية عن أبي حنيفة - رحمه الله - هي الدلالة العقلية التي لا يدفعها إلا معاند ولا يأبى قبولها والانقياد لها إلا مكابر، والله ولي التوفيق. فأما تعلقه بالآيات فغير سديد، فأما قوله تعالى: وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الْجِنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا ليعبدون () فغير دال على ما يزعمون فإن كثيرًا من أهل التأويل قالوا معناه والله أعلم : إلا ليكونوا عبادا لي لا أن يكون المراد أنهم يعبدونه بفعلهم الاختياري. يحققه أنه لو حمل على هذا لأمكن إجراء الآية على العموم فإنهم كلهم صغيرهم وكبيرهم عاقلهم ومجنونهم عباد له، ولو حملت على العبادة لما أمكن إجراؤها على العموم إذ الصغار والمجانين لم يخلقوا للعبادة، وإرجاء الآية على عمومها أولى، وقد قال كثير من العلماء: إن تأويل قوله تعالى: ﴿ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ ﴾ أي إلا لأمرهم بالعبادة فلو حملت الآية على هذا لم يبق للخصم بها متعلق. على أنا وإن سلمنا أن المراد من الآية العبادة إلا أن الآية خص منها الصغار والمجانين فيخص المتنازع منها وهم الكفرة لما ذكرنا من الدلائل، وبقيت الآية محمولة على من علم منهم الإيمان والعبادة على أن بعضا منها لو لم يكن مخصوصا بالإجماع لأمكن تخصيص المتنازع فيه لما أقمنا من الدلائل فكيف وقد خص في الآية بعض الجن والإنس ؟!.
And it is clarified that this method reported from Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) is the rational proof that none rejects except an obstinate one, and none refuses its acceptance and submission except a stubborn one. Allah is the grantor of success. As for his attachment to the verses, it is not sound. As for His saying, 'And I did not create jinn and mankind except to worship Me' (Quran 51:56), it does not indicate what they claim, for many of the people of interpretation said its meaning—and Allah knows best—is 'except to be servants to Me,' not that the intended meaning is that they worship Him by their voluntary action. This is verified by the fact that if it were taken in this sense, it would be possible to apply the verse generally, for all of them—small and great, sane and insane—are His servants. If it were taken as worship, it would not be possible to apply it generally, since children and the insane were not created for worship, and applying the verse to its general meaning is more appropriate. And many scholars have said that the interpretation of His saying 'except to worship Me' is 'except to command them to worship,' so if the verse were taken in this sense, the opponent would have no attachment to it. Moreover, even if we grant that the intended meaning of the verse is worship, the verse is specified to exclude children and the insane, so it should be specified concerning the disputed group, namely the disbelievers, due to the proofs we have mentioned. Thus, the verse remains applied to those of whom faith and worship are known. And if some of them were not specified by consensus, it would be possible to specify the disputed matter due to the proofs we have established—how then when it has been specified in the verse that some of the jinn and mankind are excepted?!
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage first asserts that the method attributed to Abu Hanifa is purely rational proof, which only the obstinate reject. Then it refutes the opponent's proof-text from Quran 51:56. The author provides two interpretations: (1) The verse means 'except to be servants' (عباد), not 'to worship' (عبادة) by voluntary action, since all creatures are servants of Allah by nature. This interpretation allows the verse to apply universally, including children and the insane who cannot perform voluntary worship. (2) Even if it means worship, many scholars interpret it as 'except to command them to worship,' which removes the opponent's argument. As a further argument, even if the verse means actual worship, it is specified to exclude children and the insane, so it can be specified to exclude disbelievers as well, based on other proofs. The verse then applies only to those whom Allah knows will have faith and worship. The author emphasizes that the verse does not force a conclusion that all must obey, and the opponent's reliance on it is unsound.

Key Terms
الدلالة العقلية
العبادة
العباد
التأويل
الخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'I did not create jinn and mankind except to worship Me' proves that every human and jinn is obligated to perform voluntary worship of Allah.
Correction: The verse may mean 'except to be servants' (a state of being) or 'except to command them to worship,' and it is already specified to exclude those incapable of worship, such as children and the insane.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Nasafi, what is one of the interpretations that shows the worship verse does not obligate all to worship?
A) The verse means 'except to be servants to Me,' which applies to all creatures.
B) The verse means 'except to test them,' so it does not imply obligation.
C) The verse was abrogated by later revelations.
D) The verse only applies to the Prophet Muhammad.

Key Takeaway
Imam Nasafi shows that the worship verse does not contradict rational proof by offering interpretations that remove its apparent force as an objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'And I did not create jinn and mankind except to worship Me,' do you live as if your entire existence is for worship, or have you reduced worship to a few rituals? If you truly believed you were created for servitude to Allah, what would change in your daily priorities starting today?
?
The author argues that the verse can be interpreted as 'except to be servants' — meaning all creatures, including disbelievers, are servants of Allah. Does this view challenge your understanding of why Allah created people He knows will disbelieve? How does this affect your view of divine justice and your own responsibility to call others?
?
If you accept that rational proofs for God's existence are valid and that revelation complements reason, are you willing to critically examine your own beliefs? Are there areas where you rely on blind acceptance rather than understanding? What would it cost you to truly seek certainty through reason and revelation together?

The Arabic Form 'Af'ala' Can Mean Naming or Finding
Explanation of the Arabic linguistic usage of 'af'ala' to mean naming or finding someone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Arabic verb form 'af'ala' can mean to call someone a name or to find someone to be something, and why this is relevant for theological debates about divine misguidance.

Core Idea
The Arabic form 'af'ala' can mean naming or finding, not just causing.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi examines a key linguistic point used by the Mu'tazilites to reinterpret verses about God's misguidance. He shows that the Arabic construction 'af'ala' can indeed mean to declare or find someone to be something, but the theological application is contested. Let's read how he supports this with poetic and idiomatic examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the basic meaning. Notice the words 'akfarūnī' and 'asharranī'.
The text quotes the pre-Islamic poet Tarafa.
2
Identify the two examples
Identify the two main examples: 'akfarūnī' (they called me a disbeliever) and 'asharranī' (he called me evil). Then notice the idiomatic phrase 'anḥaltu fulānan wa-ajbantuhu' (I found him thin and cowardly).
These examples illustrate two shades of meaning: calling and finding.
3
Understand the linguistic point and its significance
Reflect on how this shows that 'af'ala' can have the meaning of naming or finding. Consider why this matters for theology: it allows the Mu'tazilites to say that God's 'iḍlāl' means merely calling someone misguided, not creating misguidance.
The passage itself only explains the linguistic point; the theological debate is context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (1): أكفروني أي سموني كافراً. وقال طرفة: وما زال شربي الراح حتى أشرني خليلي وحتى ساءني بعض ذلك أي سماني بشرير، أو ربما يقولون: معناه وجده ضالاً يقال : أنحلت فلانا وأجبنته أي وجدته نحيلاً جبانًا.
His saying (1): 'Akfaruni' meaning they called me a disbeliever. And Tarafa said: 'My drinking of wine did not cease until my friend made me evil (asharranī) and until some of that displeased me' meaning he called me evil. Or perhaps they say: its meaning is he found him misguided. It is said: 'anḥaltu fulānan wa-ajbantuhu' meaning I found him thin and cowardly.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic clarification. It begins by explaining the word 'akfarūnī' (they called me a disbeliever), showing that the Arabic construction 'af'ala' can mean to declare or call someone a certain quality. Then it quotes a line by the pre-Islamic poet Tarafa, where he says 'asharranī' meaning 'he called me evil'. The author notes that some interpret this as 'he found me evil', similar to the phrase 'anḥaltu fulānan wa-ajbantuhu' meaning 'I found him thin and cowardly'. This illustrates that the form 'af'ala' can have the meaning of 'calling someone something' or 'finding someone to be something'. This is important for the theological debate because the Mu'tazilites claim that God's 'iḍlāl' (misguidance) means merely calling someone misguided or finding them misguided, rather than creating misguidance. This linguistic point shows that such a meaning is possible in Arabic, but the author will later argue that it is not applicable to the divine attributes because it cannot be attached to God's will.

Key Terms
أكفروني
أشرني
أفعل
التسمية
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Arabic verb form 'af'ala' always implies causing something.
Correction: The passage shows that 'af'ala' can also mean calling someone a name or finding someone to be something, as in 'akfarūnī' (they called me a disbeliever) and 'anḥaltu' (I found him thin).

Checkpoint
What does the word 'akfarūnī' mean according to the text?
A) They made me a disbeliever
B) They called me a disbeliever
C) They found me a disbeliever
D) They guided me

Key Takeaway
The Arabic form 'af'ala' can mean calling someone a name or finding someone to be something, a key point for understanding Mu'tazilite interpretations of divine misguidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the word 'akfarūnī' in the source, did you instinctively think it means 'they made me a disbeliever'? How does realizing that it actually means 'they called me a disbeliever' change the weight of accusation in that word? Have you ever used a similar verb form to label someone, assuming it reflected reality rather than just your perception?
?
If a single verb form in Arabic can mean either 'to make someone X' or 'to find someone X', how careful must you be when interpreting verses about God's actions? Can you think of a time when you assumed God's verb indicated direct causation, when it might instead indicate His declaration or judgment? How does that affect your trust in the precise wording of the Qur'an?
?
The poet Tarafa said 'asharranī' which could mean either 'he made me evil' or 'he called me evil'. How often do you assume the worst intention in others' words about you, rather than considering that they might be merely describing or labeling you? What would change in your relationships if you adopted the more charitable interpretation?

The Limitlessness of Religious Good
Argues that religious good and its causes have no limit, preserving God's infinite power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why religious good has no limit and how this preserves the infinity of God's power.

Core Idea
Religious good has no end, so its causes must be endless, safeguarding God's power from limitation.

This passage from Imam al-Maturidi addresses a fundamental question: can religious good be limited? The author argues that religious good is inherently limitless because for any good, a better good can be imagined. Consequently, the causes of good are also endless, and claiming otherwise would imply a limit to Allah's power, which is false. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from the principle about benefit to the conclusion about God's power.
The passage begins with an analogy of medicine counteracting heat.
2
Identify the core argument about religious good
Locate the statement: 'As for what is good in religion, it has no limit or end.' Understand why: because any good can be surpassed by a better good.
This is the key claim that drives the rest of the argument.
3
Trace the inference to causes of good
Notice how the author connects the limitlessness of good to the limitlessness of its causes. Then see how limiting causes would imply limiting God's power.
Pay attention to the phrase 'end of His power' and 'affirming incapability'.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: Why is it false to say the causes of good have an end? How does this protect God's attribute of power?
The passage ends with 'that is false' – a clear doctrinal stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى دفعه فكانت الزيادة ضارة لا نافعة، فأما إلى القدر الذي يقاوم الحرارة فكل جزء منه يدفع شيئًا من أجزاء الحرارة فيكون دافعا للبعض فيكون نافعًا فهذا معنى قوله رحمه الله أن ضم النفع إلى النفع لا يضر إنما يضر ضم ما ليس ينفع إلى النفع فأما ما هو صلاح في الدين فليس له حد ولا نهاية إذ ما من صلاح إلا ويتوهم ما هو أصلح منه فإذا لم يكن للصلاح في نفسه نهاية لا يتصور حصول ضد الصلاح بالمجاوزة عن القدر المقدر له بل كلما ضم صلاح إلى صلاح ازداد الصلاح وإذا لم يكن للصلاح نهاية لم يكن لأسباب الصلاح نهاية فمن أثبت لأسباب الصلاح الداخلة تحت قدرة الباري جل وعلا نهاية فقد قال بنهاية مقدوره وهو إثبات العجز وهو باطل وليس هذا من الشيخ أبي منصور بتسليم أن الدواء نافع بل النافع هو الله تعالى إلا أنه جعل سببًا للنفع غير أنه من دأبه أنه كثيرًا ما يسلم تسليم جدل ما لا يقول به ثم يبين مع فساد استدلال الخصوم ليكون ذلك أبين لضلالتهم وجهالتهم وأغيظ لهم وكيف نسلم ذلك وقد ثبت أن الضار والنافع في الحقيقة هو الله تعالى وقد ذكر ذلك رحمه الله في مواضع من كتبه وقد روى عن أبي بكر الصديق عنه أنه قال الطبيب أمرضني وروى عن عمر الله أنه نهى عن أكل الجبن وقيل له إنه يضر فقال لو كنت أعلم أنه يضرني لعبدته وهذا إخبار منه أن الضار في الحقيقة هو الله تعالى إلا أنه جرى معهم في الظاهر وأراهم بطلان ما يتعلقون به لو كان الأمر على ما يزعمون وهذا طريقه لأهل النظر مسلوكة والله الموفق.
To repel it, the increase would be harmful, not beneficial. As for the amount that counteracts the heat, each part of it repels something of the parts of the heat, so it becomes repelling some, thus beneficial. This is the meaning of his saying, may Allah have mercy on him: that joining benefit to benefit does not harm; harm comes only from joining what is not beneficial to benefit. As for what is good in religion, it has no limit or end, for there is no good except that something better than it can be imagined. So if good in itself has no end, the occurrence of the opposite of good by exceeding the measured amount is inconceivable; rather, every time good is joined to good, the good increases. And if good has no end, then the causes of good have no end. Whoever affirms an end to the causes of good that fall under the power of Allah, the Blessed and Exalted, has spoken of an end to His power, which is affirming incapability, and that is false. This is not from Shaykh Abu Mansur a concession that the medicine is beneficial; rather, the Benefactor is Allah Almighty, except that He made it a cause for benefit. However, it is his habit that he often concedes in dialectical argument what he does not believe, then he shows the corruption of the opponents' reasoning to make their error and ignorance clearer and more vexing to them. How can we concede that when it is established that the truly harmful and beneficial is Allah Almighty? He mentioned this, may Allah have mercy on him, in places in his books. It is narrated from Abu Bakr al-Siddiq that he said: The doctor made me ill. And it is narrated from Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, that he forbade eating cheese and it was said to him: It is harmful. He said: If I knew that it harms me, I would worship it. This is an indication from him that the truly harmful is Allah Almighty, except that he went along with them in appearance and showed them the falseness of what they cling to if the matter were as they claim. This path is followed by the people of investigation. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the nature of benefit and religious good (salāh). It begins by clarifying a statement about joining benefit to benefit: adding benefit to benefit is not harmful; rather, harm comes from adding something not beneficial. For religious good, there is no limit or end because for any good, one can imagine something better. Therefore, if good in itself has no end, then its causes also have no end. Affirming an end to the causes of good that are under God's power would imply a limit to His power, which is affirming incapability (al-'ajz), and that is false. The author then notes that Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi sometimes concedes points in dialectical argument (jadal) that he does not actually believe, in order to expose the opponents' error. The author stresses that true harm and benefit come only from Allah, citing traditions from Abu Bakr and Umar. This is a standard method of argumentation.

Key Terms
صلاح
نفع
قدرة
عجز
سبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that religious good is limited and that adding more good could become harmful.
Correction: The passage clarifies that religious good has no end; adding good always increases good, and harm only comes from adding what is not beneficial.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does religious good have no limit?
A) Because Allah's power is infinite.
B) Because for any good, one can imagine something better.
C) Because harm never results from religious good.
D) Because the causes of good are infinite.

Key Takeaway
Religious good is limitless, so its causes are endless, and affirming a limit to them would imply a limit to Allah's power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If religious good truly has no limit, why do you so often stop at the minimum in your worship? What does your comfort with mediocrity reveal about your conviction that Allah’s power to reward is boundless? This week, commit to adding one small act of goodness you normally skip—and see if it diminishes or multiplies your faith.
?
The passage teaches that joining benefit to benefit does not harm. Where in your life do you hesitate to add a religious good because you fear it will come at the cost of something else? Are you limiting Allah’s power by assuming He cannot make room for both? Today, take one step that proves you trust His capacity to multiply your time and strength.
?
You say you believe Allah’s power has no end. But when you sin, do you truly believe that His mercy—which is a religious good—can wipe it away completely? Or is there a part of you that thinks you have exceeded His ability to forgive? Confront that lie now, and ask Him for a forgiveness that feels unending.

Method of Learning

7 min
The Method of Dialectical Concession
Clarifies Imam Maturidi's habit of dialectical concession and affirms that only Allah is truly harmful and beneficial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Maturidi uses dialectical concession to refute opponents while upholding true doctrine.

Core Idea
Imam Maturidi concedes points in debate to expose error, but only Allah is truly beneficial or harmful.

In this passage, the author clarifies an important methodological point: Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi sometimes concedes premises in dialectical argument (jadal) that he does not actually believe, in order to show the falsity of the opponents' reasoning. This is illustrated with examples from the early caliphs. Let's explore how this method works and why it is used.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on dialectical method
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the claim that Imam Maturidi concedes things he does not believe for argument's sake.
Look for the phrase 'يسلم تسليم جدل' (concedes in dialectical argument).
2
Understand the purpose of concession
The purpose is to make the opponents' error clearer and to vex them, by showing that even if their premises were accepted, their conclusion still fails.
This is a standard method of refutation.
3
Examine the supporting evidence
The passage cites traditions from Abu Bakr al-Siddiq and Umar ibn al-Khattab that emphasize only Allah is truly harmful or beneficial.
Note how these quotes support the true doctrine.
4
Reflect on the lesson for argumentation
Consider why such a method is valid in theological debate. How does it protect the truth while engaging with falsehood?
This shows the sophistication of classical kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى دفعه فكانت الزيادة ضارة لا نافعة، فأما إلى القدر الذي يقاوم الحرارة فكل جزء منه يدفع شيئًا من أجزاء الحرارة فيكون دافعا للبعض فيكون نافعًا فهذا معنى قوله رحمه الله أن ضم النفع إلى النفع لا يضر إنما يضر ضم ما ليس ينفع إلى النفع فأما ما هو صلاح في الدين فليس له حد ولا نهاية إذ ما من صلاح إلا ويتوهم ما هو أصلح منه فإذا لم يكن للصلاح في نفسه نهاية لا يتصور حصول ضد الصلاح بالمجاوزة عن القدر المقدر له بل كلما ضم صلاح إلى صلاح ازداد الصلاح وإذا لم يكن للصلاح نهاية لم يكن لأسباب الصلاح نهاية فمن أثبت لأسباب الصلاح الداخلة تحت قدرة الباري جل وعلا نهاية فقد قال بنهاية مقدوره وهو إثبات العجز وهو باطل وليس هذا من الشيخ أبي منصور بتسليم أن الدواء نافع بل النافع هو الله تعالى إلا أنه جعل سببًا للنفع غير أنه من دأبه أنه كثيرًا ما يسلم تسليم جدل ما لا يقول به ثم يبين مع فساد استدلال الخصوم ليكون ذلك أبين لضلالتهم وجهالتهم وأغيظ لهم وكيف نسلم ذلك وقد ثبت أن الضار والنافع في الحقيقة هو الله تعالى وقد ذكر ذلك رحمه الله في مواضع من كتبه وقد روى عن أبي بكر الصديق عنه أنه قال الطبيب أمرضني وروى عن عمر الله أنه نهى عن أكل الجبن وقيل له إنه يضر فقال لو كنت أعلم أنه يضرني لعبدته وهذا إخبار منه أن الضار في الحقيقة هو الله تعالى إلا أنه جرى معهم في الظاهر وأراهم بطلان ما يتعلقون به لو كان الأمر على ما يزعمون وهذا طريقه لأهل النظر مسلوكة والله الموفق.

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the nature of benefit and religious good (salāh). It begins by clarifying a statement about joining benefit to benefit: adding benefit to benefit is not harmful; rather, harm comes from adding something not beneficial. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Imam Maturidi actually believed medicine itself is beneficial independently.
Correction: The passage clarifies that he concedes this only for argument's sake; the true belief is that only Allah is the source of benefit.

Checkpoint
Why does the author cite the traditions from Abu Bakr and Umar?
A) To prove that medicine is harmful.
B) To show that they used the same dialectical method.
C) To support the claim that only Allah is truly harmful and beneficial.
D) To argue that cheese should not be eaten.

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi's dialectical concessions are strategic, not doctrinal; the core belief is that only Allah is the true source of benefit and harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in debates or discussions about your faith, do you ever concede a point just to avoid conflict, even while knowing deep down that it is false? How can you learn from Imam Maturidi's method of conceding strategically only to reveal the truth more powerfully? Will you commit to studying his dialectical approach and practicing it with wisdom and courage?
?
Reflect on a time when you feared a medical diagnosis, job loss, or relationship breakdown. Did your heart truly believe that only Allah can harm or benefit you, or did you feel that the immediate cause had ultimate power over you? What would change in your daily life if you lived with the conviction of Abu Bakr and Umar, seeing all causes as mere instruments of Allah?
?
The source tells us that Umar said he would worship cheese if it truly harmed him. Is there anything in your life—wealth, status, health, relationships—that you subconsciously 'worship' by giving it ultimate control over your happiness or security? What concrete steps can you take today to redirect your reliance entirely to Allah?

Method of Learning

Refuting the Mu'tazila: The Shāhid-Ghā'ib Analogy
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazila doubt by challenging analogy from shāhid to ghā'ib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila argument from analogy fails unless equivalence (taswiya) between the witnessed and unseen realms is proven.

Core Idea
Analogy from creation to Creator requires proof of equivalence, not assumption.

The Mu'tazila often argue that what we observe in human behavior must apply to God. They claim that a wise human commander would never command obedience without providing the means to obey, so God must do the same. In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi begins his refutation by targeting the very foundation of their analogy: the jump from the witnessed world (al-shāhid) to the unseen divine realm (al-ghā'ib) without justification. This is a classic methodological issue in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila claim
Carefully read the description of the Mu'tazila doubt: they assert that a wise being who commands obedience and loves it cannot prevent the commanded from having the means to obey.
This is an argument from human analogy.
2
Identify the author's first objection
Find where the author declares the statement false and begins listing aspects. The first aspect is the use of shāhid without taswiya.
Taswiya means establishing that the two realms are comparable.
3
Understand the challenge
The author says: even if the principle holds in the witnessed realm, why must it hold in the unseen? What reason is there to equate them? This is a direct challenge to the Mu'tazila methodology.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection is important. If analogies from creation to Creator are accepted without proof, many false conclusions about God could follow.
This reflects the Maturidi commitment to avoiding anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة أخرى للمعتزلة والرد عليها

فأما ما اعتمدوا عليه من الشبه فنقول قولهم إنا وجدنا الحكيم إذا كان أمرًا بطاعة محبًا لها مريدًا فلن يجوز أن يمنع المأمور ما يصل به إلى طاعته إلى آخر ما حكينا. كلام باطل من وجوه. أحدها: أنه استدل على هذا بالشاهد من غير إثبات التسوية فيقال له: هب أن الأمر في الشاهد كان على ما زعمت، ولكن لم ينبغي أن يكون الأمر كذلك في الغائب؟ وبأي معنى تجمع بينهما ؟ فإن زعمت أن كل ما ثبت في الشاهد كان
Another doubt (shubha) of the Mu'tazila and the refutation of it.

As for what they relied upon from the doubts, we say: Their statement, 'We found that the wise person, when he commands an act of obedience and loves it and wills it, it is not permissible for him to prevent the commanded one from that by which he can reach his obedience' up to the rest of what we narrated. This statement is false from several aspects. One of them: That he deduces this from the witnessed (al-shāhid) without establishing equivalence (al-taswiya). So it is said to him: Suppose the matter in the witnessed is as you claim, but why should the matter be likewise in the unseen (al-ghā'ib)? And in what sense do you equate between them? For if you claim that everything established in the witnessed is...
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage introduces another doubt (shubha) from the Mu'tazila and begins its refutation. The Mu'tazila argue that a wise person, when he commands an act of obedience and genuinely loves and wills it, cannot prevent the commanded person from obtaining the means to perform that obedience. The author declares this statement false on multiple grounds, and begins to list them. The first ground is that the Mu'tazila are using an analogy from the witnessed realm (al-shāhid, the observable world) to the unseen realm (al-ghā'ib, the divine realm) without establishing that the two realms are equivalent (al-taswiya). The author challenges the opponent: even if the principle holds in the witnessed realm, why must it hold in the unseen? The refutation is incomplete in this passage.

Key Terms
الشبهة
الشاهد
الغائب
التسوية
الحكيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila think that because wise humans provide means to obey, God must do the same.
Correction: This analogy assumes equivalence between human and divine wisdom, which is not proven. God's wisdom is not bound by human limitations.

Checkpoint
What is the first flaw the author points out in the Mu'tazila doubt?
A) The doubt is based on a false premise.
B) The doubt uses an analogy from the witnessed to the unseen without proving equivalence.
C) The doubt contradicts the Quran.
D) The doubt ignores divine omnipotence.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argument fails because it assumes an unproven equivalence between human and divine wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's actions, do you ever unconsciously assume that His wisdom must work like human wisdom? Where in your life have you imposed human logic on God? Will you commit today to seeking His revelation before assuming how He should act?
?
The Mu'tazila were confident in their analogy, yet they were wrong. Are there beliefs or expectations you hold about God that you have never tested against the principle that He is not like His creation? Name one such expectation and resolve to examine it through scripture.
?
This passage says God is not bound by what seems fitting to human minds. Does your worship reflect a God who is truly free and sovereign, or have you been serving a god made in your own image? What must change in your heart to submit to the God who is beyond your analogies?

The Error of Turning Tawaqquf into a Creed
Refutes the error of making tawaqquf a permanent doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why turning suspension of judgment into a permanent doctrine violates consensus and correct methodology.

Core Idea
Suspension of judgment is a temporary tool, not a creed to defend.

This passage from Imam Nasafi's 'Tabsirat al-Adilla' addresses a critical methodological error: treating tawaqquf (suspension of judgment) as a standing theological position. The author explains the proper approach to differing opinions within the community, emphasizing that tawaqquf is only justified when unable to distinguish truth from falsehood, and even then it must lead to seeking knowledge from qualified scholars. Turning it into a permanent doctrine is a deviation from reason and consensus.

Guided Path
1
Understand consensus and opposition
The author first defines what it means to oppose consensus. He gives examples: if the community agrees on three opinions, a fourth opinion becomes opposition. Similarly, they agreed there is no intermediate station between faith and disbelief, so affirming one is opposition.
Note how the author uses consensus as a boundary for acceptable positions.
2
Learn the proper methodology
When the community disagrees, one must investigate the opinions and evaluate them against evidence. If one cannot distinguish truth from falsehood, temporary suspension is required, coupled with seeking guidance from a scholar.
The process is: investigate → evaluate with evidence → if unable, suspend and ask a scholar.
3
Identify the error of permanent tawaqquf
The author criticizes those who take this temporary suspension—born from conflicting evidence or incapacity—and turn it into a permanent doctrine to be defended. This, he says, is a deviation from reason and fundamental principles.
The error is that tawaqquf is treated as a theological school instead of a procedural step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحقيقه ما هي المخالفة؟ فإن الأمة قبل ظهور نحلتهم إذا كانوا على ثلاثة أقوال كانوا مجتمعين على أن ما وراءها قول باطل، فهم إذا أحدثوا قولاً رابعا فقد خالفوا الإجماع، وكذا أجمعوا أن لا منزلة بين الإيمان والكفر، فمن أثبت المنزلة فقد خالف الأمة، والأخذ بالإجماع بما هو مخالف للإجماع من وجهين جهل فاحش.
ثم الأمة إذا اختلفت في شيء على أقاويل لم يجز الخروج عن أقاويلهم لما مر أن ذلك منهم إجماع أن ما عدا هذه الأقاويل باطل، فبعد ذلك فالواجب البحث عن الأقاويل وعرضها على الدلائل ليتبين الصحيح من الفاسد والحق من الباطل، وعند العجز عن التمييز بين الحق منها والباطل يجب التوقف والرجوع إلى من أكرم بالعلم والخضوع له والجثو بين يديه ليبين له ذلك، فأما جعل التوقف الذي هو مقتضى تعارض الأدلة ونتيجة العجز عن ترجيح بعض الأقاويل على بعض موجبات الحيرة - مذهبًا يتمسك به وعقيدة يدان بها ويناضل عنها ويناظر مخالفوها فيحيد عما توجبه العقول وتقتضيه الأصول.
And its verification: what is the opposition? For the community before the emergence of their sect, if they were upon three opinions, they were in agreement that what lies beyond them is a false statement. So if they introduce a fourth opinion, they have opposed the consensus. Similarly, they agreed that there is no intermediate station between faith and disbelief; so whoever affirms the intermediate station has opposed the community. And adhering to consensus by what is contrary to consensus in two respects is gross ignorance.

Then, if the community disagrees about something in several opinions, it is not permissible to go outside their opinions, because, as has passed, that from them is a consensus that everything other than these opinions is false. So after that, it is obligatory to investigate the opinions and present them to the evidences so that the correct may be distinguished from the corrupt and the truth from falsehood. And when unable to distinguish between the truth and falsehood among them, it is obligatory to suspend judgment and refer to one who is honored with knowledge, and to submit to him and kneel before him so that he may clarify it for him. But to make suspension of judgment — which is the requirement of conflicting evidences and the result of inability to prefer some opinions over others, and a cause of confusion — into a doctrine to be adhered to and a creed to be professed, and to defend it and debate its opponents, is to deviate from what intellects necessitate and principles require.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This is a methodological passage from the author's refutation of a certain sect (likely the Mu'tazila) regarding the intermediate station (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). The author first explains the concept of consensus (ijma'). He notes that before the emergence of the sect being criticized, the community (umma) was agreed on three opinions concerning a particular issue, meaning that any opinion outside those three is false. Thus, introducing a fourth opinion constitutes a violation of consensus. Similarly, the community agreed that there is no middle station between faith (iman) and disbelief (kufr); whoever asserts such a station has contradicted consensus. Building on this, the author describes the proper methodology when the community disagrees: one may not exit the set of positions held by the community, as that would break the implicit consensus that only those positions are valid. The next step is to examine those opinions against scriptural evidences (dala'il) to distinguish truth from falsehood. If one is unable to do so, suspension of judgment (tawaqquf) is obligatory, combined with seeking guidance from a qualified scholar. The author then sharply criticizes those who turn this temporary suspension—born from conflicting evidences and incapacity—into a permanent school of thought and a creed to be defended and debated. This, he says, is a deviation from what reason and fundamental principles require.

Key Terms
إجماع
توقف
دلائل
منزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Suspension of judgment (tawaqquf) is itself a valid theological school or doctrine to be permanently adhered to.
Correction: The author clarifies that tawaqquf is only a temporary measure when unable to distinguish truth from falsehood, and it should lead to seeking guidance from a scholar, not become a permanent creed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should a person do when unable to distinguish truth from falsehood among differing opinions?
A) Adopt a position of permanent suspension and defend it as a doctrine.
B) Suspend judgment temporarily and refer to a qualified scholar.
C) Follow the majority opinion without investigation.
D) Reject all opinions and rely solely on personal reason.

Key Takeaway
Turning suspension of judgment into a permanent doctrine violates consensus and the correct method of seeking knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a position of 'I don't know' not because you truly sought clarity, but because it felt safer than committing to a truth that demands action? Will you today seek out a scholar who can guide you, even if it means humbling yourself before them?
?
When you encounter conflicting opinions on a matter of faith, do you honestly investigate the evidences, or do you default to suspension as a permanent comfort zone? What is one issue where you have refused to move from suspension to conviction—and what will you do about it this week?
?
The passage warns against making a temporary tool into a permanent creed. Are there any theological or practical positions you hold that began as uncertainty but have hardened into a shield against accountability? Ask yourself: Am I defending my ignorance as a 'position' instead of seeking the truth?

Interpreting Promise and Threat Verses: Principle and Sectarian Views
Sanusi states the principle of non-literal interpretation and records Mu'tazila, Khawarij, and Murji'a views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why verses of promise and threat require interpretation and the two opposing sectarian approaches to them.

Core Idea
Taking all promise and threat verses literally would create contradiction; interpretation is necessary.

Sanusi introduces a key methodological principle: verses of promise (waʿd) and threat (waʿīd) are expressed in general forms, but applying them all universally would lead to contradiction in Allah’s words. This necessitates interpretation. He then reports the conflicting views of two major theological groups: the Muʿtazila/Khawārij prioritize the threat verses, while the Murjiʾa prioritize the promise verses. This passage sets the stage for the Maturidi position, which we will explore later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall flow.
The author uses a very concise style. Pay attention to the key terms: الوعد, الوعيد, العموم, التناقض.
2
Understand the principle of non-literal interpretation
The author states that verses of promise and threat are in general form, but taking them all universally is impossible because it would create contradiction and conflict in Allah’s verses and proofs. This shows that some interpretation is necessary.
This principle is the foundation for the later discussion of sectarian views.
3
Identify the two opposing sectarian views
The Mu'tazila and Khawarij argue that threat verses are more deserving of universal application because they are more effective as deterrents. The Murji'a argue that promise verses are more deserving because they align with Allah’s attributes of mercy, pardon, and forgiveness.
Notice that each group picks the set of verses that supports their theological emphasis.
4
Consider the author’s neutrality
At this point, Sanusi is only reporting the views. He does not endorse either. This is important because he will later present the Maturidi position as a balanced alternative.
Avoid assuming the author agrees with one side based on this passage alone.
5
Reflect on why this matters
This lesson shows how different theological commitments lead to different interpretive priorities. It also highlights the need for a consistent method when dealing with seemingly contradictory scriptural passages.
This connects to broader debates about God’s justice, mercy, and free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوعد خارجة على صيغة العموم وكذا آيات الوعيد، ولا وجه إلى إجراء الكل على العموم لما فيه من إثبات التناقض في آيات الله تعالى والتعارض في أدلته، وذلك خارج عن الحكمة. ثم بعد ذلك اضطربت الأقاويل فزعمت المعتزلة والخوارج أن آيات الوعيد أحق بالعموم لما هو أبلغ في الزجر، وزعمت المرجئة أن آيات الوعد أحق بالعموم لأنه أحق بالذي عرف من صفات الله تعالى من الرحمة والعفو والغفران.
The promise is [uttered] in a general form, and similarly the verses of threat, and there is no justification for applying all of them universally because that would entail establishing contradiction in the verses of Allah and contradiction in His proofs, and that is outside of wisdom. Then after that, the opinions differed: the Mu'tazila and the Khawarij claimed that the verses of threat are more deserving of being general because that is more effective in deterrence, and the Murji'a claimed that the verses of promise are more deserving of being general because that is more in line with what is known of the attributes of Allah from mercy, pardon, and forgiveness.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage presents a methodological principle and a summary of sectarian disagreement. It begins by stating that the verses of promise (waʿd) and threat (waʿīd) are expressed in general terms (ʿalā ṣīghati l-ʿumūm). However, it is not permissible to take all of them universally (ʿalā l-ʿumūm) because that would produce contradiction (tanāquḍ) and conflict (taʿāruḍ) in Allah’s verses and proofs, which is incompatible with divine wisdom (ḥikma). This principle sets the stage for why interpretation (taʾwīl) is needed. Then the author notes that after this, opinions became confused (iḍṭarabat al-aqāwīl). The Muʿtazila and Khawārij held that the verses of threat are more deserving of being taken generally because that is more powerful in deterrence (azjar). The Murjiʾa, on the other hand, held that the verses of promise are more deserving of generality because they align better with what is known of Allah’s attributes of mercy (raḥma), pardon (ʿafw), and forgiveness (ghufrān). The passage thus lays out the methodological necessity of interpretation and the two opposing sectarian tendencies, without yet giving the Maturidi position.

Key Terms
الوعد
الوعيد
العموم
التناقض
التعارض
الحكمة
المعتزلة
الخوارج
المرجئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the author endorses one of these sectarian views or that these views are the Maturidi position.
Correction: The author is merely reporting the views of these groups. The Maturidi position is presented later as a balanced alternative that does not prioritise one set of verses over the other absolutely.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that it is not possible to take all verses of promise and threat universally?
A) Because they are not meant to be taken literally.
B) Because it would lead to contradiction in Allah's verses and proofs.
C) Because the Mu'tazila and Khawarij disagree.
D) Because the verses are not in the form of generality.

Key Takeaway
Because universally applying all promise and threat verses would contradict divine wisdom, interpretation is necessary; the Mu'tazila/Khawarij prioritise threat verses, while the Murji'a prioritise promise verses, reflecting their theological emphases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses of punishment in the Qur’an, is your immediate instinct to warn others or to examine your own soul? Which sect’s tendency does that reflect, and what does it reveal about your image of Allah? Commit to balancing both by reading a verse of mercy after every verse of threat this week.
?
The Mu’tazila and Khawarij emphasized threat for deterrence; the Murji’a emphasized mercy. Which of these tendencies is more dominant in your heart, and why? How might an overemphasis on one side distort your relationship with Allah today? Write down one specific way you can actively correct that imbalance.
?
If you were to apply every promise and threat literally, you would face contradiction. Where in your own life have you ignored one side of Allah’s message because it felt contradictory? Will you now humbly seek understanding through interpretation, as this lesson demands, instead of dismissing what unsettles you?

The Conditional Argument on Later Specification
Nasafi presents a conditional argument about general forms and later specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditional argument that if a general form indicates absolute universality, then later specification would constitute abrogation rather than clarification, and why this applies to all general forms.

Core Idea
A truly universal form cannot be specified later without abrogation.

This passage presents a logical problem: if a general command is taken as absolutely universal, any later specification would contradict its original meaning. Nasafi uses this to challenge those who treat threat verses as universally inclusive. Let's examine his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Read the opening conditional statement carefully. It assumes that a general form stripped of specification evidence indicates universality and inclusion of every individual.
This is a hypothetical premise.
2
Identify the consequence
If the premise holds, then later specification would be abrogation, not clarification, because the specified item would not have been included originally.
This is the core logical implication.
3
Consider the scope
Nasafi then asks whether this ruling is specific to threat verses or applies to all general forms. Find his answer in the text.
The answer is that it applies to every general form.
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this argument is important for debates about the generality of Qur'anic verses, especially verses of threat and promise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو كانت الصيغة المتعرية عن دليل الخصوص دليل إرادة العموم والاستيعاب وتناولها كل فرد، كما لو ذكر باسمه الخاص والتخصيص المتأخر يكون نسخا لا بيانًا إن قدر المخصوص ما كان داخلاً في العام، أمختص هذا بصيغة آيات الوعيد، أم هو حكم آيات الوعد وكل صيغة عامة ؟ فلا بد من القول إنه حكم كل صيغة عامة.
If the form stripped of evidence of specification is evidence of intending generality and inclusiveness and its inclusion of every individual, as if it mentioned by its specific name, and later specification would be abrogation not clarification, if it is assumed that the specified was not included in the general, does this apply specifically to the form of verses of threat, or is it the rule of verses of promise and every general form? Then it must be said that it is the rule of every general form.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is a logical conditional argument about the nature of general forms (صيغة العموم) in the Qur'an. It begins with a premise: if a general form that has no evidence of specification (دليل الخصوص) is considered evidence of intending universality and inclusion of every individual, then it would be like mentioning someone by their specific name. In that case, any later specification (تخصيص) would amount to abrogation (نسخ), not clarification (بيان), because the specified item would not have been included in the general statement in the first place. The author then poses a question: Does this ruling apply only to the forms of threat verses (آيات الوعيد), or does it also apply to promise verses (آيات الوعد) and every general form? The reader is compelled to answer that it applies to every general form. This argument sets up a problem for the Mu'tazila, who claimed that threat verses are general and inclusive, but then they later specify them by saying that the punishment applies only to those who die unrepentant. According to this argument, if the general form truly includes everyone, later specification is abrogation, which is problematic.

Key Terms
الصيغة المتعرية
دليل الخصوص
العموم
التخصيص المتأخر
النسخ
البيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Later specification of a general verse is always a clarification (بيان) of the original intent.
Correction: If the general form is truly universal and includes all individuals, later specification would change the original meaning, making it abrogation, not clarification.

Checkpoint
According to the conditional argument, if a general form is interpreted to include every individual, what would later specification be?
A) Clarification (بيان)
B) Abrogation (نسخ)
C) Confirmation (تأكيد)
D) Interpretation (تأويل)

Key Takeaway
A general form that truly includes every individual cannot be later specified without abrogation, and this rule applies to all general forms, not just threat verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a general threat in the Quran (e.g., 'whoever does wrong will be punished'), do you instinctively assume it might be specified to exclude you? How does Nasafi's argument challenge your tendency to escape the general implications of divine threats? Are you willing to let God's words stand as generally applicable to you, without seeking loopholes?
?
If later specification of a general divine statement is actually abrogation, then what does that imply about your interpretation of seemingly contradictory verses? Are you sometimes too quick to 'specify' verses that confront your lifestyle, effectively treating them as abrogated? Will you now treat each general form as binding until clear evidence of specification comes?
?
Consider a promise of reward in the Quran: 'whoever does good will receive paradise.' Do you treat that as absolute and inclusive of all your good deeds, or do you privately doubt that God's promise applies to you? Nasafi says the rule of generality applies to all forms—including promises. How does that affect your trust in God's promises today? Will you act on that trust?
