Rational Restriction on Verses of Promise and Threat
Defense of rational restriction against Ash'ari denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi defense of using rational evidence to restrict absolute verses of promise and threat, and the Ash'ari objection to that method.

Core Idea
Reason restricts the apparent generality of some Quranic verses, but not all theologians agree.

In this passage, the author presents the argument that rational evidence (dalli al-'aql) restricts the absolute wording of verses about eternal punishment and reward. He then reports the Ash'ari rebuttal and adds further rational considerations. This debate highlights how reason and revelation interact in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Note the structure: an opponent's argument, then the author's response.
The passage is a single continuous argument with an objection and reply.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Find the claim that rational evidence restricts all verses of promise and threat. What examples does the opponent give?
The opponent lists four examples: a believer, a repentant, a disbeliever in Paradise, a sinner.
3
Note the Ash'ari counter-claim
The author says the Ash'aris deny that reason deems these things impossible. Understand their position.
The Ash'aris argue that reason does not prove impossibility of eternal punishment for believers or eternal reward for disbelievers.
4
Understand the additional arguments
The author adds further reasons: nullification of faith for a sin is rationally rejected, and God's attributes of mercy prevent generalization.
These reinforce the restriction on verses of threat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال: دليل القيد قائم وهو دلالة العقل عند نزول صيغة الوعد والوعيد لما أن تخليد من آمن في النار مما يأباه العقل، وكذا تخليد من تاب عن المعصية، وكذا تخليد من ختم له بالكفر في الجنة مما يأباه العقل وكذا تخليد من ارتكب معصية إذ بها يصير عدوا لله تعالى وتخليد العدو في الجنة محال، والدليل العقلي قائم عند نزول كل آية من آيات الوعد والوعيد فتقيدت الآيات كلها بدليل العقل . قلنا : والأشعرية ينازعونك في هذا أشد النزاع ويزعمون أنه ليس في العقل دليل استحالة تخليد المؤمنين المطيعين الذين لا معصية لهم في النار ولا تخليد الكفرة والعصاة في الجنة - ثم لو قيل لكم إن تخليد من آمن - وعمل الصالحات ولم يكفر بل ختم له بالتصديق – في النار، مما يأباه العقل، وكذا إحباط الإيمان الذي هو نهاية في الخير وأعمال كثيرة من العبادات كل واحد منها ينبغي أن يجزي بعشرة بإخبار الله تعالى بارتكاب ما ليس بنهاية (۱) في ، ولا يجزى إلا بواحدة بإخبار الله (1) تعالى مع اقتران خوف العقاب ورجاء الرحمة والثقة بكرمه وهي في نفسه شيء دفع إليه بغلبة شهوة أو قهر غضب أو شدة حمية فهذا أيضا مما يأباه العقل فصار قيدًا في آيات الوعيد المقترنة بالتخليد، وكذا قيام دليل كون الله تعالى موصوفا بالرحمة والرأفة والعفو والمغفرة يمنع من تعميم آيات الوعيد أيضا، والله الموفق.
For he said: The evidence of restriction is established, which is the evidence of reason at the revelation of the wording of promise and threat, because eternal punishment of one who believed in the Fire is something that reason rejects, likewise eternal punishment of one who repented from disobedience, likewise eternal punishment of one who ended with disbelief in Paradise is something reason rejects, likewise eternal punishment of one who committed a sin because thereby he becomes an enemy of God, and eternal punishment of an enemy in Paradise is impossible, and the rational evidence is established at the revelation of every verse of promise and threat, so all verses are restricted by rational evidence. We say: The Ash'aris vehemently dispute with you in this and claim that there is no rational evidence for the impossibility of eternal punishment of believing obedient ones who have no sin in the Fire nor eternal punishment of disbelievers and sinners in Paradise. Then if it were said to you that eternal punishment of one who believed and did righteous deeds and did not disbelieve but ended with affirmation—in the Fire, is something reason rejects, likewise nullification of faith which is utmost in goodness and many acts of worship each of which should be rewarded tenfold by God's information, for committing something that is not utmost [in evil]? and is rewarded only with one by God's information, accompanied by fear of punishment and hope of mercy and trust in His generosity, and it is in itself something driven to by overcoming of desire or overpowering of anger or intense zeal, this also is something reason rejects, so it becomes a restriction on the verses of threat connected with eternal punishment. Likewise the establishment of evidence that God is described with mercy, compassion, pardon, and forgiveness prevents the generalization of the verses of threat as well. And God is the Guide.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer exchange. The opponent argues that rational evidence (dalli al-aql) restricts all verses of promise (wa'd) and threat (wa'id) because reason deems it impossible for a believer to be eternally punished, for a repentant sinner to be eternally punished, and for a disbeliever to be in Paradise eternally. Therefore, every verse that appears absolute is actually qualified by reason. The author responds: The Ash'aris strongly deny this, claiming that reason does not deem such things impossible. Then the author adds a second argument: If one says that reason rejects eternal punishment for a believer who did good deeds and ended with affirmation, it also rejects nullifying faith and good deeds for a single sin that may be driven by desire or anger. This too would restrict the threat verses. Finally, the evidence that God is merciful also prevents generalizing the threat verses. The passage ends with 'And God is the Guide.'

Key Terms
دليل القيد (dalli al-qayd)
الوعد (wa'd)
الوعيد (wa'id)
التخليد (takhleed)
الأشعرية (Ash'ariyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Belief that rational evidence always overrides any scriptural text.
Correction: The passage shows a nuanced debate: the opponent uses reason to restrict verses, but the Ash'aris deny such rational evidence exists. Thus, the role of reason is contested, not absolute.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'aris as mentioned in the passage, what do they claim about rational evidence for eternal punishment?
A) Reason proves eternal punishment is impossible.
B) Reason does not prove impossibility of eternal punishment for believers.
C) Reason restricts all verses.
D) Reason is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The debate over rational restriction demonstrates that theological methodology is not uniform, and Maturidis defend a role for reason in interpreting scriptural promises and threats.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this passage, the opponent argues that reason recoils at the idea of a believer suffering eternal punishment. When you look at your own life and sins, do you secretly assume you are 'safe' from God's punishment because you believe? What would it mean if rational evidence did not guarantee that safety?
?
The Ash'ari position leaves open the possibility that an obedient believer could be eternally punished. Does that possibility shake your confidence in God's mercy, or does it make you take the verses of threat more seriously? How would you respond if you had to defend your hope in God's forgiveness without relying on rational restriction?
?
If reason truly cannot prove that a repentant sinner will not be eternally punished, then what is the basis of your hope when you repent? Is your repentance genuine if it depends on a rational guarantee rather than on God's mercy alone?

Adam's Promise: Absolute Wording and Restriction
Proof from Adam's promise that absolute wording can be restricted without being false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of Adam's promise refutes the claim that absolute wording always indicates unrestricted generality.

Core Idea
Absolute wording can be restricted by context without making the speaker a liar.

The Maturidi theologian uses a striking example from the Qur'an: God's promise to Adam that he would not be naked in Paradise. When Adam sinned, he became naked. This seems to contradict the promise, but the theologian argues that the promise was understood to be conditional on obedience. This lesson explores how this example refutes the opponent's doctrine that unqualified wording necessarily indicates generality and absoluteness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic source and its translation once to grasp the argument's flow. Don't analyze yet – just absorb the story and the rhetorical question.
Notice the key terms: al-wa'd al-mutlaq (absolute promise) and al-sigha al-mutajarrida (unqualified wording).
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage presents two answers to whether the absolute promise included the time of Adam's sin. Identify each answer and what it implies about God's truthfulness and the nature of the promise.
3
Understand the refutation
See how both possible answers lead to a conclusion that refutes the opponent's position. One leads to impossibility (lying attributed to God), the other forces admission that the promise was restricted, undermining their doctrine.
4
Connect to methodology
Reflect on how this example shows that absolute wording can be implicitly restricted by context or condition, without destroying the speaker's veracity. This is a key methodological point in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم: أليس أن الله تعالى قال لآدم عليه السلام حين أسكنه الجنة (إنَّ لك ألا تجوعَ فِيهَا وَلَا تَعْرَى )) وَأَنَّكَ لَا تَظْمَؤُا فِيهَا وَلَا تَضْحَى ) ثم لما وجدت منه الزلة عرى على ما قال تعالى: وَطَفِقَا يَعْصِفَانِ عَلَيْهِمَا مِن وَرَقِ النَّةِ أكان ذلك الوعد المطلق تناول حال وجود الزلة أم لا؟ فإن قال : تناول فقد ظهر كذب الله تعالى حيث وعد له أن لا يعرى وقد عرى وإن قال لم يتناول حال الزلة، فقد زعم أن الوعد المطلق العاري عن القيد كان المراد منه القيد بحالة، وفيه إبطال مذهبهم وفساد القول بدلالة الصيغة المتعرية عن دليل الخصوص والقيد على إرادة العموم والإطلاق.
Then we say to them: Did not God Almighty say to Adam, peace be upon him, when He settled him in Paradise: 'Indeed, you will not be hungry therein nor be naked, and you will not be thirsty therein nor suffer from heat.' Then when the misstep occurred from him, he became naked as God said: 'And they began to cover themselves with the leaves of Paradise.' Did that absolute promise include the time of the occurrence of the misstep or not? If he says: It included, then lying appears from God Almighty since He promised him that he would not be naked and he became naked. And if he says: It did not include the time of the misstep, then he has claimed that the absolute promise devoid of restriction was intended to be restricted by a condition, and in this is the invalidation of their doctrine and the corruption of the claim that the unqualified wording (al-sigha al-mutajarrida) without evidence of specification or restriction indicates the intention of generality and absoluteness.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy. The author addresses the opponent: God made an absolute promise to Adam that he would not be naked in Paradise. Yet when Adam sinned, he became naked. This shows that the absolute promise was restricted by the condition of obedience. If the opponent claims the promise included the time of sin, then God would have lied—impossible. If they claim it did not include that time, then they admit that the absolute wording was intended with a restriction. This refutes their doctrine that an unqualified wording indicates generality and absoluteness without any restriction.

Key Terms
الوعد المطلق (al-wa'd al-mutlaq)
القيد (al-qayd)
الخصوص (al-khusus)
الصيغة المتعرية (al-sigha al-mutajarrida)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's promise was broken or that absolute wording can never be restricted.
Correction: The example shows that the promise was always understood as conditional on obedience, so restriction does not make the promise false; rather, it clarifies the intended scope.

Checkpoint
What does the example of Adam's promise demonstrate about absolute wording?
A) Absolute wording always means unrestricted.
B) Absolute wording can be restricted without making God a liar.
C) Adam was never promised anything.
D) The promise was false.

Key Takeaway
The example of Adam's promise demonstrates that absolute wording can be implicitly restricted, refuting the claim that unqualified wording always indicates unrestricted generality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a promise or an absolute statement, do you consider the hidden conditions that might apply? How does Adam's story challenge you to be more careful with your own words?
?
Have you ever treated a Qur'anic promise as unconditional, only to feel disappointed or confused? What does this lesson teach you about looking for implicit restrictions in divine speech?
?

Forgiveness Verse as Proof of Restricted Generality
Proof from verse and defense of Maturidi school.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'Indeed, Allah forgives all sins' is used as a logical proof that general terms can be restricted, and how the Maturidi school defends against the accusation of insisting on literal interpretation of all general terms.

Core Idea
General terms in revelation are not always literal; they can be restricted by context and consensus.

In this passage, the author presents a compelling logical argument from the Qur'anic verse about forgiveness to demonstrate that even apparent general terms have restrictions. He then defends the Maturidi school against the false claim that they insist on literal interpretation of all general terms, showing that they allow for intended meanings beyond the literal in certain contexts.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the dilemma
Read the quoted verse and notice how the author poses a binary question: does God forgive every sin or not? This sets up the logical trap.
The verse is from Surah Az-Zumar (39:53).
2
Follow the logical options
Trace the two possible answers: 'yes' contradicts consensus, 'no' implies God would be lying, which is impossible. See how each option forces the opponent to accept restriction.
3
Understand the conclusion
The only way out for the opponent is to admit that the general term 'all sins' is restricted. This proves that general terms can be specified.
This is a key point in usul al-fiqh.
4
Read the defense against the misconception
Read how the author refutes the idea that Maturidis take every general term literally without exception. Note the reference to their books allowing intended meanings beyond the literal when the speaker is not accused of lying.
The passage ends abruptly; the defense is cut off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا يقال: أليس أن الله تعالى قال : قُلْ يَنعِبَادِيَ الَّذِينَ أَسْرَفُوا عَلَى أَنفُسِهِمْ لَا تَقْنَطُوا مِن رَّحْمَةِ اللَّهِ إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَغْفِرُ الذُّنُوبَ جَمِيعًا أيغفر كل ذنب أم لا؟ ولم يقترن به قيد ولا تخصيص ذنب فإن قالوا: نعم. أبطلوا مذهبهم وخرجوا أيضا عن الإجماع. وإن قالوا: لا . قيل هل يصير الله تعالى كاذبًا بذلك؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. كفروا. وإن قالوا: لا أبطلوا (مذهبهم) ولولا مخافة الإطالة لبينت بعض الدلائل في مسألة العموم وكشفت عما يموهون على الضعفة بإيراد بعض مسائل أصحابنا ويصورون عندهم أن من مذهب أصحابنا القول بوجوب اعتقاد شمول حكم العام فيما يتناوله بلفظه، والشهادة على الله تعالى أنه أراد به الكل والحجر عليه عن إرادة خصوص فيه أو قيد، غير أن ما شرطنا في أول الكتاب من الاجتناب عن الإطالة منعنا عن الاشتغال بذلك، وكيف يدعي أحد ذلك على أصحابنا وقد يوجد في كتبهم ما يتعذر إحصاؤه من المسائل أن من تكلم بكلام وقال: عنيت به غير الظاهر الذي وضع له في حقيقة اللغة أنه يصدق إذا كان غير متهم في ذلك بأن شدد بما أخبر على نفسه والتزم به الضرر وإنما لا يصدقه القاضي فما ينتفع به لو
And likewise it is said: Did not God Almighty say: 'Say, O My servants who have transgressed against themselves, do not despair of the mercy of Allah. Indeed, Allah forgives all sins.' Does He forgive every sin or not? And no restriction or specification of sins has accompanied it. If they say: Yes, they invalidate their doctrine and also depart from consensus. And if they say: No, it is said: Does God Almighty thereby become a liar? If they say: Yes, they disbelieve. And if they say: No, they invalidate their doctrine. And were it not for fear of prolongation, I would have explained some evidences regarding the issue of generality ('umum) and uncovered what they obfuscate to the weak by presenting some issues of our companions and they portray among them that the doctrine of our companions is the obligation to believe in the inclusiveness of the ruling of a general term in what its wording encompasses, and to testify about God Almighty that He intended by it everything, and to restrict Him from intending any specification or restriction therein, except that what we stipulated at the beginning of the book—avoiding prolongation—prevented us from engaging in that. And how can anyone claim that about our companions when there are found in their books innumerable issues that if one speaks a speech and says: I meant by it other than the apparent meaning for which it was placed in the reality of language, that he is believed if he is not accused of that, because he has bound himself and accepted harm by what he informed about himself, though the judge does not believe him, so what benefit is there in it...
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage contains two parts. First, the author presents another proof using the verse 'Indeed, Allah forgives all sins' (39:53). The opponent is asked: Does God forgive every sin? If they say yes, they contradict consensus (since some sins require repentance, etc., and the verse was revealed concerning specific people). If they say no, then they must answer whether God becomes a liar—impossible. Thus they are forced to accept that the general term was restricted. Second, the author declines to elaborate further due to his promise to avoid prolixity, and defends the Maturidi school against the accusation that they hold that every general term must be taken literally without possibility of specification. He states that their books contain many examples where a speaker's intended meaning contrary to the literal wording is accepted if he is not accused of lying, but the judge's legal ruling does not accept that. The passage ends abruptly, likely continuing the defense.

Correction: The passage shows that the Maturidi school allows for specification (takhsis) and that general terms can be restricted. They hold that the intended meaning may differ from the literal, and the speaker's intention is considered if not accused of lying.

Refuting Denial of Generality: The Adam and Forgiveness Dilemmas
Two dilemmas refuting denial of generality in divine promises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi scholar refutes the denial of generality in absolute divine promises through two dilemmas that lead to absurdities.

Core Idea
Absolute promises imply generality; denying it leads to impossibility or blasphemy.

The author begins by presenting two examples from the Qur'an where an absolute promise or statement is made. He shows that if we deny that these absolute expressions include all instances, we are forced into contradictions: either God's promise becomes false, or the expression must be secretly qualified. This refutes the position that absolute expression does not indicate generality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first dilemma from Adam
Read the story of Adam covering himself. The absolute promise 'you will not be naked' is tested. Does it include the moment of sin? Both options lead to problems.
The author uses this to show that denying generality leads to 'invalidation of their doctrine'.
2
Analyze the second dilemma from the forgiveness verse
Read the verse 'God forgives all sins.' The question is whether it includes every sin. Again both answers contradict the opponents' view or lead to blasphemy.
This shows that the absolute must be taken as general unless evidence specifies.
3
Connect to the main argument
Both dilemmas demonstrate that absolute expressions stripped of qualifiers indicate generality. The opponents' claim that they do not is untenable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الزلة عرى على ما قال تعالى: وَطَفِقَا يَعْصِفَانِ عَلَيْهِمَا مِن وَرَقِ الْجَنَّةِ أكان ذلك الوعد المطلق تناول حال وجود الزلة أم لا؟ فإن قال: تناول فقد ظهر كذب الله تعالى حيث وعد له أن لا يعرى وقد عرى. وإن قال لم يتناول حال الزلة، فقد زعم أن الوعد المطلق العاري عن القيد كان المراد منه القيد بحالة، وفيه إبطال مذهبهم وفساد القول بدلالة الصيغة المتعرية عن دليل الخصوص والقيد على إرادة العموم والإطلاق. وكذا يقال أليس أن الله تعالى قال: قُلْ يَا عِبَادِيَ الَّذِينَ أَسْرَفُوا عَلَى أَنفُسِهِمْ لَا تَقْنَطُوا مِن رَّحْمَةِ اللَّهِ إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَغْفِرُ الذُّنُوبَ جَمِيعًا أيغفر كل ذنب أم لا؟ ولم يقترن به قيد ولا تخصيص ذنب. فإن قالوا: نعم. أبطلوا مذهبهم وخرجوا أيضا عن الإجماع. وإن قالوا: لا. قيل هل يصير الله تعالى كاذبًا بذلك؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. كفروا. وإن قالوا: لا أبطلوا مذهبهم. ولولا مخافة الإطالة لبينت بعض الدلائل في مسألة العموم وكشفت عما يموهون على الضعفة بإيراد بعض مسائل أصحابنا ويصورون عندهم أن من مذهب أصحابنا القول بوجوب اعتقاد شمول حكم العام فيما يتناوله بلفظه، والشهادة على الله تعالى أنه أراد به الكل والحجر عليه عن إرادة خصوص فيه أو قيد، غير أن ما شرطنا في أول الكتاب من الاجتناب عن الإطالة منعنا عن الاشتغال بذلك، وكيف يدعي أحد ذلك على أصحابنا وقد يوجد في كتبهم ما يتعذر إحصاؤه من المسائل أن من تكلم بكلام وقال: عنيت به غير الظاهر الذي وضع له في حقيقة اللغة أنه يصدق إذا كان غير متهم في ذلك بأن شدد بما أخبر على نفسه والتزم به الضرر وإنما لا يصدقه القاضي فما ينتفع به لو فيما يدعيه من إرادته خلاف الظاهر، فكان هذا مذهبًا منهم أن من تكلم بلفظ ثم عنى به ما يحتمله اللفظ يصدق، وإن كان فيه عدول عن ظاهرة إذا لم يكن متهما في ذلك، وفيه دليل أنهم ما جعلوا ظاهر اللفظ عند تعريه عن دليل يدل على أن المراد به خلاف الظاهر دليلاً على إرادة الظاهر، إذ لو كان كذلك لما قبل منه قوله أني عنيت خلاف الظاهر وإن لم يكن متهمًا في ذلك، كمن ادعى ما لا يحتمله اللفظ لا يصدق وإن لم يكن متهمًا في ذلك. ثم الله تعالى غير متهم فيما يقيم من دلالة الخصوص ينبغي أن يكون ذلك دليل إرادة الخصوص من وقت نزوله، والله أعلم.
The sin of covering (themselves) according to what He, exalted, said: 'And they began to cover themselves with leaves of Paradise.' Did that absolute promise include the state of the sin or not? If he said: it included, then the lie of God, exalted, has become apparent, where He promised him that he would not be naked and he became naked. And if he said: it did not include the state of sin, then he claimed that the absolute promise free of qualification was intended to be qualified by a state. In that is the invalidation of their doctrine and the corruption of the claim that the unqualified expression, free from evidence of specificity and qualification, indicates the intention of generality and absoluteness. Similarly, it is said: Is it not that God, exalted, said: 'Say: O My servants who have transgressed against themselves, do not despair of the mercy of God. Indeed, God forgives all sins.' Does He forgive every sin or not? And no qualification nor specification of sin is attached to it. If they say: Yes, they invalidate their doctrine and also depart from consensus. And if they say: No, it is said: Does God, exalted, become a liar by that? If they say: Yes, they disbelieve. And if they say: No, they invalidate their doctrine. Were it not for the fear of lengthiness, I would have explained some evidences concerning the issue of generality and uncovered what they obscure to the weak by presenting some issues of our companions and they depict to them that it is from the doctrine of our companions that one must believe in the inclusion of the ruling of the general in what it encompasses by its wording, and bearing witness upon God that He intended by it the whole, and constraining Him from intending any particularity or qualification in it. However, what we stipulated at the beginning of the book of avoiding lengthiness prevented us from engaging in that. And how can anyone claim that against our companions while there is found in their books what is impossible to count of issues: that whoever speaks speech and says: I meant by it other than the apparent meaning which it is placed for in the true language, he is believed if he is not suspected in that, because he affirms firmly what he informs about himself and accepts the harm. But the judge does not believe him, so what benefit would he have from what he claims of intending contrary to the apparent meaning? Thus this was a doctrine from them: that whoever speaks a word then means by it what the word can bear is believed, even if it is a departure from its apparent meaning, provided he is not suspected in that. And in it is evidence that they did not make the apparent meaning of the word when it is stripped of evidence indicating that what is meant is contrary to the apparent, evidence for intending the apparent. For if it were so, his saying 'I meant contrary to the apparent' would not be accepted from him even if he is not suspected in that, like one who claims what the word cannot bear is not believed even if he is not suspected in that. Then God, exalted, is not suspected in what He establishes as evidence of specificity. It should be that that evidence indicates the intention of specificity from the time of its revelation. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who deny that absolute expressions in the Qur'an imply generality, followed by an exposition of the Maturidi school's position on interpreting general terms. The author first presents a dilemma using the story of Adam: after eating from the tree, they covered themselves with leaves. The divine promise that they would not be naked—if taken as absolute—either included the moment of sin (making God's promise false) or did not (making the promise actually qualified). Both options are unacceptable, showing that the opponents' denial of generality leads to absurdity. A second dilemma is presented from the verse on forgiveness: God says He forgives all sins absolutely. If the opponents say He forgives every sin, they contradict their own principle; if they say He does not, then God would be lying. Thus these two examples demonstrate the necessity of taking absolute statements as general unless there is evidence to the contrary. The author then refrains from giving more proofs but clarifies the school's position: a speaker who intends a non-apparent meaning is believed if not suspected of lying, because he accepts the consequences. The judge does not accept this for legal benefit, showing that the apparent meaning is not decisive. Applying this to God, since He is not suspected, any evidence of specificity He provides indicates His intention from the start.

Key Terms
الوعد المطلق
العموم
الخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that absolute expressions in the Qur'an do not imply generality unless there is external evidence.
Correction: This passage argues that the very denial leads to absurdity, so the default is generality.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion from the argument about the promise of covering for Adam?
A) The promise was absolute and included the sin.
B) The promise was qualified by the absence of sin.
C) The argument shows that absolute promises must be taken as general unless there is evidence to the contrary.
D) The argument is only about Adam and does not apply to other verses.

Key Takeaway
Absolute divine promises and statements, when free from qualifiers, imply generality; denying this leads to logical contradictions or blasphemy.

?

?
Have you ever added unspoken conditions to God’s promises, assuming they don’t apply to your situation? What would change if you took them as absolute and general?
?
If you deny that God’s absolute words mean what they say, are you not placing limits on His power and truth? Will you trust His promises without qualification, even when your feelings or circumstances suggest otherwise?

The Maturidi Stance: Allowing Non-Apparent Meanings in General Terms
Explains Maturidi stance on intended meaning vs apparent meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi principle that a speaker's intended meaning, even if contrary to the apparent, is accepted if the speaker is not suspect of lying, and how this applies to God's speech.

Core Idea
A speaker's intended non-apparent meaning is accepted if he is not suspect; God is not suspect, so He may intend specificity.

After refuting the denial of generality, the author clarifies the Maturidi school's actual position. They do not insist that general terms always mean their apparent sense; rather, if the speaker states he meant something else that the wording can bear, he is believed as long as he is not suspected of lying. This applies even to God, who is never suspect, so any evidence of specificity He provides indicates His intention.

Guided Path
1
Read the clarification of the school's position
Read the passage from 'Were it not for the fear of lengthiness...' to the end. Notice how the author defends the school against the claim that they insist on the apparent meaning.
The school allows a speaker to intend a non-apparent meaning if he is not suspect.
2
Understand the condition of being 'not suspect'
A speaker is believed when he says he meant something else, because he accepts the harm of contradicting the apparent, which indicates truthfulness. The judge does not accept this, but the principle holds in theological matters.
This shows the school does not treat the apparent meaning as decisive.
3
Apply to God's speech
God is not suspect, so any evidence of specificity He provides shows He intended that from the start. This preserves the generality default while allowing for exceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الزلة عرى على ما قال تعالى: وَطَفِقَا يَعْصِفَانِ عَلَيْهِمَا مِن وَرَقِ الْجَنَّةِ أكان ذلك الوعد المطلق تناول حال وجود الزلة أم لا؟ فإن قال: تناول فقد ظهر كذب الله تعالى حيث وعد له أن لا يعرى وقد عرى. وإن قال لم يتناول حال الزلة، فقد زعم أن الوعد المطلق العاري عن القيد كان المراد منه القيد بحالة، وفيه إبطال مذهبهم وفساد القول بدلالة الصيغة المتعرية عن دليل الخصوص والقيد على إرادة العموم والإطلاق. وكذا يقال أليس أن الله تعالى قال: قُلْ يَا عِبَادِيَ الَّذِينَ أَسْرَفُوا عَلَى أَنفُسِهِمْ لَا تَقْنَطُوا مِن رَّحْمَةِ اللَّهِ إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَغْفِرُ الذُّنُوبَ جَمِيعًا أيغفر كل ذنب أم لا؟ ولم يقترن به قيد ولا تخصيص ذنب. فإن قالوا: نعم. أبطلوا مذهبهم وخرجوا أيضا عن الإجماع. وإن قالوا: لا. قيل هل يصير الله تعالى كاذبًا بذلك؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. كفروا. وإن قالوا: لا أبطلوا مذهبهم. ولولا مخافة الإطالة لبينت بعض الدلائل في مسألة العموم وكشفت عما يموهون على الضعفة بإيراد بعض مسائل أصحابنا ويصورون عندهم أن من مذهب أصحابنا القول بوجوب اعتقاد شمول حكم العام فيما يتناوله بلفظه، والشهادة على الله تعالى أنه أراد به الكل والحجر عليه عن إرادة خصوص فيه أو قيد، غير أن ما شرطنا في أول الكتاب من الاجتناب عن الإطالة منعنا عن الاشتغال بذلك، وكيف يدعي أحد ذلك على أصحابنا وقد يوجد في كتبهم ما يتعذر إحصاؤه من المسائل أن من تكلم بكلام وقال: عنيت به غير الظاهر الذي وضع له في حقيقة اللغة أنه يصدق إذا كان غير متهم في ذلك بأن شدد بما أخبر على نفسه والتزم به الضرر وإنما لا يصدقه القاضي فما ينتفع به لو فيما يدعيه من إرادته خلاف الظاهر، فكان هذا مذهبًا منهم أن من تكلم بلفظ ثم عنى به ما يحتمله اللفظ يصدق، وإن كان فيه عدول عن ظاهرة إذا لم يكن متهما في ذلك، وفيه دليل أنهم ما جعلوا ظاهر اللفظ عند تعريه عن دليل يدل على أن المراد به خلاف الظاهر دليلاً على إرادة الظاهر، إذ لو كان كذلك لما قبل منه قوله أني عنيت خلاف الظاهر وإن لم يكن متهمًا في ذلك، كمن ادعى ما لا يحتمله اللفظ لا يصدق وإن لم يكن متهمًا في ذلك. ثم الله تعالى غير متهم فيما يقيم من دلالة الخصوص ينبغي أن يكون ذلك دليل إرادة الخصوص من وقت نزوله، والله أعلم.
The sin of covering (themselves) according to what He, exalted, said: 'And they began to cover themselves with leaves of Paradise.' Did that absolute promise include the state of the sin or not? If he said: it included, then the lie of God, exalted, has become apparent, where He promised him that he would not be naked and he became naked. And if he said: it did not include the state of sin, then he claimed that the absolute promise free of qualification was intended to be qualified by a state. In that is the invalidation of their doctrine and the corruption of the claim that the unqualified expression, free from evidence of specificity and qualification, indicates the intention of generality and absoluteness. Similarly, it is said: Is it not that God, exalted, said: 'Say: O My servants who have transgressed against themselves, do not despair of the mercy of God. Indeed, God forgives all sins.' Does He forgive every sin or not? And no qualification nor specification of sin is attached to it. If they say: Yes, they invalidate their doctrine and also depart from consensus. And if they say: No, it is said: Does God, exalted, become a liar by that? If they say: Yes, they disbelieve. And if they say: No, they invalidate their doctrine. Were it not for the fear of lengthiness, I would have explained some evidences concerning the issue of generality and uncovered what they obscure to the weak by presenting some issues of our companions and they depict to them that it is from the doctrine of our companions that one must believe in the inclusion of the ruling of the general in what it encompasses by its wording, and bearing witness upon God that He intended by it the whole, and constraining Him from intending any particularity or qualification in it. However, what we stipulated at the beginning of the book of avoiding lengthiness prevented us from engaging in that. And how can anyone claim that against our companions while there is found in their books what is impossible to count of issues: that whoever speaks speech and says: I meant by it other than the apparent meaning which it is placed for in the true language, he is believed if he is not suspected in that, because he affirms firmly what he informs about himself and accepts the harm. But the judge does not believe him, so what benefit would he have from what he claims of intending contrary to the apparent meaning? Thus this was a doctrine from them: that whoever speaks a word then means by it what the word can bear is believed, even if it is a departure from its apparent meaning, provided he is not suspected in that. And in it is evidence that they did not make the apparent meaning of the word when it is stripped of evidence indicating that what is meant is contrary to the apparent, evidence for intending the apparent. For if it were so, his saying 'I meant contrary to the apparent' would not be accepted from him even if he is not suspected in that, like one who claims what the word cannot bear is not believed even if he is not suspected in that. Then God, exalted, is not suspected in what He establishes as evidence of specificity. It should be that that evidence indicates the intention of specificity from the time of its revelation. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who deny that absolute expressions in the Qur'an imply generality, followed by an exposition of the Maturidi school's position on interpreting general terms. The author first presents a dilemma using the story of Adam: after eating from the tree, they covered themselves with leaves. The divine promise that they would not be naked—if taken as absolute—either included the moment of sin (making God's promise false) or did not (making the promise actually qualified). Both options are unacceptable, showing that the opponents' denial of generality leads to absurdity. A second dilemma is presented from the verse on forgiveness: God says He forgives all sins absolutely. If the opponents say He forgives every sin, they contradict their own principle; if they say He does not, then God would be lying. Thus these two examples demonstrate the necessity of taking absolute statements as general unless there is evidence to the contrary. The author then refrains from giving more proofs but clarifies the school's position: a speaker who intends a non-apparent meaning is believed if not suspected of lying, because he accepts the consequences. The judge does not accept this for legal benefit, showing that the apparent meaning is not decisive. Applying this to God, since He is not suspected, any evidence of specificity He provides indicates His intention from the start.

Key Terms
الظاهر
التهمة
الخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Maturidi school insists that general terms always mean their apparent, comprehensive sense, and that God cannot intend anything else.
Correction: The school allows a speaker's intended non-apparent meaning if he is not suspect; God is not suspect, so He can intend specificity.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi school, when is a speaker's claim of intending a non-apparent meaning accepted?
A) Whenever the speaker is a scholar.
B) When the speaker is not suspected of lying and the meaning is possible.
C) Only if the speaker provides evidence.
D) Never, the apparent meaning always holds.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi school does not rigidly adhere to the apparent meaning; a speaker's intended non-apparent meaning is accepted if plausible and the speaker is not suspect, and this applies to God's speech as well.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse from the Qur'an that seems general, do you ever assume you know exactly what God intends for you personally? How does this lesson challenge you to consider that God may have a more specific purpose that requires deeper study? Will you commit to seeking tafsir before making firm conclusions?
?
The author says a speaker is believed if he is not suspected. Think about your own words: when you say something but mean something else, are you trustworthy? Do you accept the consequences of your words? This lesson calls you to be honest because God knows your true intent. How will you change your speech to reflect integrity?
?
If God is not suspected, then any specification He provides is intentional from the beginning. Do you sometimes doubt that certain verses apply to your situation? How can this lesson increase your trust that God's guidance is precise? What step will you take to align your life with the specific commands you find?

Introduction to the Disagreement on Iman
Author introduces the disagreement over iman and explains his brief treatment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi only presents a summary of the disagreements on iman and how his approach follows the Salaf.

Core Idea
The vastness of disagreement on iman requires a concise, methodical summary.

Al-Nasafi opens his discussion of iman by acknowledging the extensive differences among scholars regarding its definition. He cannot cover all opinions, so he chooses a practical path: a brief summary following the method of the Salaf. This sets the stage for his concise treatment.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage aloud if possible, then read the English translation. Notice the author's humble tone as he admits the impossibility of covering all opinions.
The text is a methodological preface, not yet a substantive discussion.
2
Understand the author's reasoning
Al-Nasafi gives two reasons for his brevity: the immense number of views and his desire to follow the Salaf. Ask yourself why following the Salaf is important in this context.
The Salaf are the pious predecessors of the first three generations.
3
Reflect on the methodological choice
Consider the balance between thoroughness and practicality. Al-Nasafi chooses a summary to keep the book focused. How does this approach serve the reader?
This reveals the author's pedagogical intent.

This shows his intention to be concise yet comprehensive enough not to neglect the topic.

When Narrators Omit: Integrity vs. Accuracy
First reconciliation: narrator omission due to not hearing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Maturidi reconciles a hadith discrepancy by attributing the omission of the word 'shara'i' to a narrator's failure to hear, and why this is preferred over accusing the narrator of dishonesty.

Core Idea
Trustworthy narrators can be mistaken but not dishonest.

When two hadith narrations seem to say different things, scholars like al-Maturidi do not immediately assume one is false. Instead, they examine possible innocent explanations. In this passage, he addresses a discrepancy where one version of a hadith mentions iman and the other mentions Islam. The first reconciliation method is based on a simple human error: a narrator might not have heard the full question.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the first reconciliation method. Notice that al-Maturidi cites Umar's narration and then proposes that the narrator simply missed the word 'shara'i'.
The word 'shara'i' means 'laws' or 'prescriptions'.
2
Understand the principle of adala
Al-Maturidi argues that attributing omission to not hearing is better than accusing the narrator of adding words, because integrity (adala) prevents intentional addition but not failure to hear. Reflect on why this principle is important in hadith criticism.
This principle preserves the trustworthiness of narrators while allowing for human error.
3
Connect to the bigger problem
Consider how this method fits into the overall goal: showing that the hadiths are not contradictory. Write one sentence explaining why this resolution is plausible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشيخ الإمام أبو منصور الماتريدي ذكر أن عمر الله ذكر أن رسول الله سئل عن الإيمان ثم روى عن شرائع الإسلام فأجاب بالذي ذكر، فيكون هذا الخبر تفسيرًا للأول فيحمل ما أنه سئل عن الإسلام على أن بعض الرواة لم يسمع لفظة الشرائع في السؤال، يؤيد هذا ما روينا أنها كانت مذكورة وحمل أمر بعض الرواة على أنه لم يسمع أولى من حمل البعض أنه تعمد الزيادة، لما أن عدالتهم تنفي تعمد الزيادة ولا تنفي عدم السماع، أو يحمل على أن هذا الراوي ترك تلك الزيادة لعلمه أن أحدًا لا يشتبه عليه أن المراد بالسؤال الثاني هو الشرائع دون الإسلام إذ لا يتصور مؤمن ليس بمسلم ولا مسلم ليس بمؤمن أو يحمل على أنه أضمر لفظة الشرائع، وأقام المضاف إليه مقام المضاف على ما هو دأب العرب عند ارتفاع خوف اللبس وَلِ الْقَرْيَةَ أي: أهل القرية إلا أن الأهل أضمر لدلالة الحال لما أن السؤال طلب البيان وذلك غير متصور من القرية التي هي جماد، فكذا هاهنا لما علم أن ما ذكر من الجواب شرائع الإسلام لا نفس الإسلام لما أن بوجودها بدون ما ذكر في جواب الإيمان لا يكون مسلمًا، وبانعدامها مع وجود ما ذكر من جواب الإيمان لا ينعدم الإسلام، ولا يخرج الإنسان من أن يكون مسلما أضمر لفظة الشرائع وأقيم لفظة الإسلام مقامها أو يقال: إنه إنما ذكر الإسلام وأورد به الشرائع بطريق
And the Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi mentioned that Umar Allah mentioned that the Messenger of Allah was asked about iman (faith), then he narrated about the laws (shara'i') of Islam and answered with what was mentioned. So this report becomes an interpretation of the first. So it is taken that he was asked about Islam because some narrators did not hear the word 'shara'i'' in the question. This is supported by what we narrated: that it (the word) was mentioned. And attributing the matter of some narrators to not hearing is preferable to attributing it to intentionally adding, because their integrity (adala) precludes intentional addition but does not preclude not hearing. Or it is taken that this narrator omitted that addition because he knew that no one would be confused that what was meant by the second question was the laws (shara'i') not Islam itself, since it is inconceivable that there be a believer (mu'min) who is not a Muslim (muslim) or a Muslim who is not a believer. Or it is taken that he implied (admar) the word 'shara'i', and substituted the genitive construct (mudaf ilayh) for the construct (mudaf), as is the practice of the Arabs when fear of confusion is removed—like 'and ask the town' (wa-li l-qarya) meaning the people of the town, except that the word 'people' is implied due to context because the question is a request for explanation, and that is inconceivable from the town which is inanimate. So likewise here, since it is known that what was mentioned in the answer is the laws of Islam (shara'i' al-islam) not Islam itself, because with their existence without what was mentioned in the answer about iman one is not a Muslim, and with their absence along with the existence of what was mentioned in the answer about iman Islam is not absent, nor does a person leave being a Muslim. So the word 'shara'i' is implied and the word 'Islam' is put in its place. Or it is said: he only mentioned Islam and intended by it the laws (shara'i') by way of
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part reconciliation of hadith narrations where the Prophet was asked about iman in one version and about the laws (shara'i') of Islam in another. The author, following al-Maturidi, provides several interpretations to resolve the apparent discrepancy. First, he suggests that the narrator in the version mentioning 'Islam' simply did not hear the word 'shara'i' in the question, and this is more plausible than accusing a narrator of intentionally adding words, because narrators are trustworthy (adala) and would not fabricate. Second, the narrator may have omitted the word 'shara'i' because the context made it clear, since a believer must also be a Muslim and vice versa—so asking about Islam naturally implies asking about its laws. Third, a rhetorical device of Arabic (idad) is invoked: the word 'shara'i' is implied (admar) and the word 'Islam' stands in its place, similar to the Quranic phrase 'and ask the town' meaning 'ask the people of the town', where 'people' is omitted because the context disambiguates. Fourth, the author argues that the answer given (prayer, zakat, etc.) is clearly about the laws of Islam, not Islam itself, because one can have faith (iman) without performing these acts and still be a believer (though imperfect), but one cannot be a Muslim without them. Conversely, performing these acts without faith does not make one a Muslim. Thus, the answer must be about the laws. The author concludes by considering that the Prophet may have simply used 'Islam' to refer to its laws, a common metonymy.

Key Terms
الرواة
العدالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All hadith discrepancies imply that some narrators are liars.
Correction: Narrators can make honest mistakes, and scholars prefer to attribute errors to mishearing or forgetfulness rather than dishonesty, given their known integrity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it preferable to say that a narrator simply did not hear the word 'shara'i' rather than that he intentionally added it?
A) The narrator's integrity (adala) precludes intentional addition but not failure to hear.
B) The narrator was known to be forgetful.
C) The word 'shara'i' was never mentioned in any narration.
D) The narrator was from a different generation.

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi first reconciles the hadith by suggesting a narrator misheard the question, preserving the narrator's integrity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear conflicting accounts from two trusted individuals, do you instinctively suspect one of them is lying? How can al-Maturidi's principle of assuming mishearing over intentional deception change your approach?
?
Have you ever confidently reported something only to later realize you misheard or misremembered? How does that experience affect your trust in your own testimony—and your judgment of others who might make similar errors?
?
This lesson teaches that even righteous narrators can make honest mistakes. Is there a person you have dismissed based on a single error? What would it take for you to reconsider their sincerity and give them the benefit of the doubt?

Context and Implicit Meaning in Hadith
Second reconciliation: narrator omitted because context makes it clear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Maturidi uses the conceptual overlap between iman and Islam to explain why a narrator could omit the word 'shara'i' without causing confusion.

Core Idea
A believer is always a Muslim, so asking about Islam implies its laws.

Another way to reconcile the hadith is to realize that the context already makes the intended meaning clear. Al-Maturidi argues that since every believer is necessarily a Muslim and vice versa, the narrator might have omitted the word 'shara'i' because he knew no one would misunderstand.

Guided Path
1
Read the second reconciliation
Read the Arabic and translation of the 'aw yuhmalu' section. Notice that this option does not blame any narrator error but instead relies on the listener's understanding.
The word 'shara'i' is not mentioned in this version, but the context provides it.
2
Understand the overlap of iman and Islam
Al-Maturidi states that it is impossible to have a believer (mu'min) who is not a Muslim (muslim) and vice versa. Why is this the case? Reflect on the relationship between the two concepts in Islamic theology.
In Maturidi thought, iman and Islam are closely linked; one cannot exist without the other.
3
Evaluate the strength of this argument
Consider whether this reconciliation fully resolves the discrepancy. Does it require that the listener already knows the answer involves the laws? Write a brief thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi mentioned that Umar Allah mentioned that the Messenger of Allah was asked about iman (faith), then he narrated about the laws (shara'i') of Islam and answered with what was mentioned. So this report becomes an interpretation of the first. So it is taken that he was asked about Islam because some narrators did not hear the word 'shara'i'' in the question. This is supported by what we narrated: that it (the word) was mentioned. And attributing the matter of some narrators to not hearing is preferable to attributing it to intentionally adding, because their integrity (adala) precludes intentional addition but does not preclude not hearing. Or it is taken that this narrator omitted that addition because he knew that no one would be confused that what was meant by the second question was the laws (shara'i') not Islam itself, since it is inconceivable that there be a believer (mu'min) who is not a Muslim (muslim) or a Muslim who is not a believer. Or it is taken that he implied (admar) the word 'shara'i', and substituted the genitive construct (mudaf ilayh) for the construct (mudaf), as is the practice of the Arabs when fear of confusion is removed—like 'and ask the town' (wa-li l-qarya) meaning the people of the town, except that the word 'people' is implied due to context because the question is a request for explanation, and that is inconceivable from the town which is inanimate. So likewise here, since it is known that what was mentioned in the answer is the laws of Islam (shara'i' al-islam) not Islam itself, because with their existence without what was mentioned in the answer about iman one is not a Muslim, and with their absence along with the existence of what was mentioned in the answer about iman Islam is not absent, nor does a person leave being a Muslim. So the word 'shara'i' is implied and the word 'Islam' is put in its place. Or it is said: he only mentioned Islam and intended by it the laws (shara'i') by way of
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part reconciliation of hadith narrations where the Prophet was asked about iman in one version and about the laws (shara'i') of Islam in another. The author, following al-Maturidi, provides several interpretations to resolve the apparent discrepancy. First, he suggests that the narrator in the version mentioning 'Islam' simply did not hear the word 'shara'i' in the question, and this is more plausible than accusing a narrator of intentionally adding words, because narrators are trustworthy (adala) and would not fabricate. Second, the narrator may have omitted the word 'shara'i' because the context made it clear, since a believer must also be a Muslim and vice versa—so asking about Islam naturally implies asking about its laws. Third, a rhetorical device of Arabic (idad) is invoked: the word 'shara'i' is implied (admar) and the word 'Islam' stands in its place, similar to the Quranic phrase 'and ask the town' meaning 'ask the people of the town', where 'people' is omitted because the context disambiguates. Fourth, the author argues that the answer given (prayer, zakat, etc.) is clearly about the laws of Islam, not Islam itself, because one can have faith (iman) without performing these acts and still be a believer (though imperfect), but one cannot be a Muslim without them. Conversely, performing these acts without faith does not make one a Muslim. Thus, the answer must be about the laws. The author concludes by considering that the Prophet may have simply used 'Islam' to refer to its laws, a common metonymy.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الإسلام
الشرائع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Iman and Islam are entirely separate concepts with no overlap.
Correction: In traditional Sunni theology, they are closely tied: a true believer is necessarily a Muslim, and outward Islam requires inward faith.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why could the narrator omit the word 'shara'i'?
A) He forgot it.
B) He knew that no one would confuse the intended meaning, since iman and Islam are inseparable.
C) He was intentionally misleading.
D) The word was not in the original hadith.

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi's second reconciliation relies on the listener's prior understanding that iman and Islam are coextensive, making the omission of 'shara'i' harmless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you communicate about your faith, do you assume that others already understand the inseparable link between iman and Islam? Where in your life have you omitted important details because you assumed they were obvious, and has that ever led to misunderstanding? What will you do this week to check your assumptions when explaining Islamic concepts?
?
Al-Maturidi says it is 'inconceivable' for a believer not to be a Muslim. Yet in your daily life, do you ever separate your inner faith from your outward practice? In which area of your life are you acting as if iman can exist without the laws of Islam, and what one step will you take today to reunite them?
?
The narrator omitted 'shara'i' because he trusted that the listener's prior knowledge would fill the gap. Are you willing to extend that trust to others, or do you frequently assume the worst when someone delivers an incomplete message? Think of one person you have misunderstood recently. How can you approach them now with the assumption of their good intention and contextual understanding?

Arabic Rhetoric: Implied Words in Hadith Reconciliation
Third and fourth reconciliations: idmar and metonymy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Maturidi invokes the Arabic rhetorical device of idmar (implication) and the Quranic analogy to reconcile the hadith narration.

Core Idea
Arabs routinely imply words when context clarifies meaning, just as in Quranic usage.

The final reconciliation method draws on a well-known feature of Arabic eloquence: the omission of a word that the context makes obvious. Al-Maturidi compares this to the Quranic verse 'and ask the town' where 'people' is implied. He then provides a theological argument showing that the answer given must refer to the laws of Islam, not Islam itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the third and fourth reconciliations
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that this method uses the concept of 'idmar' (implication) and supports it with a Quranic analogy.
The Quranic phrase 'wa-li l-qarya' is from Surah Yusuf, meaning 'and ask the town' but implying 'the people of the town'.
2
Understand idmar and the Quranic example
Al-Maturidi explains that Arabs often place the genitive construct (mudaf ilayh) in place of the construct (mudaf) when confusion is impossible. The Quranic example shows that 'the town' stands for 'the people of the town' because the context makes it clear that a town cannot be asked.
Similarly, in the hadith, 'Islam' stands for 'the laws of Islam' because the context shows that the answer is about practices, not the essence.
3
Analyze the theological argument
Al-Maturidi then gives a theological reason: the answer (prayer, zakat, etc.) cannot be about Islam itself because a person can have iman without these acts and still be a believer, but without these acts one is not a Muslim. Therefore, the answer must be about the laws (shara'i').
This reinforces that the omission is not arbitrary but based on logical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشيخ الإمام أبو منصور الماتريدي ذكر أن عمر الله ذكر أن رسول الله سئل عن الإيمان ثم روى عن شرائع الإسلام فأجاب بالذي ذكر، فيكون هذا الخبر تفسيرًا للأول فيحمل ما أنه سئل عن الإسلام على أن بعض الرواة لم يسمع لفظة الشرائع في السؤال، يؤيد هذا ما روينا أنها كانت مذكورة وحمل أمر بعض الرواة على أنه لم يسمع أولى من حمل البعض أنه تعمد الزيادة، لما أن عدالتهم تنفي تعمد الزيادة ولا تنفي عدم السماع، أو يحمل على أن هذا الراوي ترك تلك الزيادة لعلمه أن أحدًا لا يشتبه عليه أن المراد بالسؤال الثاني هو الشرائع دون الإسلام إذ لا يتصور مؤمن ليس بمسلم ولا مسلم ليس بمؤمن أو يحمل على أنه أضمر لفظة الشرائع، وأقام المضاف إليه مقام المضاف على ما هو دأب العرب عند ارتفاع خوف اللبس وَلِ الْقَرْيَةَ أي: أهل القرية إلا أن الأهل أضمر لدلالة الحال لما أن السؤال طلب البيان وذلك غير متصور من القرية التي هي جماد، فكذا هاهنا لما علم أن ما ذكر من الجواب شرائع الإسلام لا نفس الإسلام لما أن بوجودها بدون ما ذكر في جواب الإيمان لا يكون مسلمًا، وبانعدامها مع وجود ما ذكر من جواب الإيمان لا ينعدم الإسلام، ولا يخرج الإنسان من أن يكون مسلما أضمر لفظة الشرائع وأقيم لفظة الإسلام مقامها أو يقال: إنه إنما ذكر الإسلام وأورد به الشرائع بطريق
And the Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi mentioned that Umar Allah mentioned that the Messenger of Allah was asked about iman (faith), then he narrated about the laws (shara'i') of Islam and answered with what was mentioned. So this report becomes an interpretation of the first. So it is taken that he was asked about Islam because some narrators did not hear the word 'shara'i'' in the question. This is supported by what we narrated: that it (the word) was mentioned. Imamate Is Established by Ijtihad
Author states imamate by ijtihad and rejects nass claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that imamate is determined through ijtihad, not a divine text, and that the Shia claims of a nass for Ali or al-Abbas are false.

Core Idea
Imamate is a matter of human reasoning, not divine appointment.

The author begins by clearly stating the Sunni position: imamate is established through ijtihad, the same method used to derive rulings for novel legal issues. He then immediately dismisses the opposing views of the Rafida and Rawandiyya as false. This lesson sets the foundation for the rest of his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author asserts the method (ijtihad) before rejecting alternative claims.
The word 'ثم' (then) indicates this is a continuation of a larger discussion.
2
Identify the positive claim
Find where the author states that imamate is by ijtihad. He compares it to how rulings are derived for ordinary legal incidents (hawadith shar'iyya).
3
Identify the refutation
Notice the author declares the claims of the Rafida (Shia) and Rawandiyya as false (batil). He does not provide proof yet—that comes later.
Rafida refers to those who reject the first two caliphs; Rawandiyya were a group claiming nass for al-Abbas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم طريق ثبوت الإمامة لمن يصلح أهلاً لها على سبيل الاجتهاد واستعمال الرأي على ما يستعمل في الحوادث الشرعية التي بالناس إلى معرفة أحكامها حاجة وقد عدموا فيها النص إذ لا نص ها هنا، وقول الروافض بوجود النص من النبي عليه السلام على علي رضي الله عنه وقول الراوندية بوجود النص على العباس رضي الله عنه باطل. رد القول بالنص في الإمامة والدليل على بطلان النص أن أمر الخلافة أمر عام يقع بكل الناس إلى معرفته حاجة ماسة، وما هذا سبيله كان النص فيه لو كان ثابتا يشتهر اشتهارا لا يبقى معه على أحد من الناس خفاء، ولا اضطر الناس إلى معرفته كالنص على القبلة وعلى أعداد ركعات الصلاة ومقاديرها وأوقاتها ومقادير النصيب والواجبات في باب الزكاة، وغير ذلك من الأمور العامة، ولما لم يوجد في ذلك خبر هذا سبيله، دل أن لا نص فيه البتة، يدل عليه أن النص لو كان ثابتا لما أعرضت الصحابة مع جلالة أقدارهم في الدين وشدة ورعهم وتمسكهم بالدين وتحرزهم عن مخالفة الشريعة عن قبوله والعمل به وتفويض الأمر إلى المنصوص عليه، بل بادروا إليه وولوه الأمر ولم ينازعه أحد في ذلك إذ لم يكن تقلد من تقلد للأمر منهم رغبة في الجاه والمنزلة والترأس ولا حرصا على جمع الحطام والاستمتاع باللذات البدنية والشهوات الطبيعية بل كان تقلدهم لذلك للقيام بحقوق الدين واستيفاء حقوق الله تعالى وتقوية الملة والسعى في إعلاء كلمة الله تعالى وقهر أعدائه وتشييد بنيان الإسلام ألا ترى الصديق رضي الله عنه كيف كانت معاملته الخلق ورفضه الشهوات والاكتفاء باليسير من القوت والخشن من الثياب والاجتزاء بقليل
Then the method of establishing the imamate for one who is qualified for it is by way of ijtihād and the exercise of opinion, as is done in the case of legal incidents (ḥawādith shar‘iyya) whose rulings people need to know, and they have no text concerning them, for there is no text here. The saying of the Rāfiḍa that there is a text from the Prophet, peace be upon him, concerning ‘Alī, may God be pleased with him, and the saying of the Rāwandiyya that there is a text concerning al-‘Abbās, may God be pleased with him, are false. Refutation of the claim of text (naṣṣ) in the imamate. The proof of the falsehood of the naṣṣ is that the matter of the caliphate is a general matter whose knowledge is needed by all people, and something of this sort, if a naṣṣ existed concerning it, would become so famous that no one would remain ignorant of it, and people would not be confused about knowing it, like the naṣṣ on the direction of prayer (qibla), on the numbers of rak‘as of prayer, their measures, their times, the measures of shares and obligations in the chapter of zakāt, and other general matters. Since no report of this sort has been found concerning it, this indicates that there is absolutely no naṣṣ about it. This is supported by the fact that if the naṣṣ had existed, the Companions—despite the greatness of their rank in religion, the intensity of their piety, their adherence to religion, and their cautiousness against violating the Sharī‘a—would not have turned away from accepting it and acting upon it, and from entrusting the matter to the one designated by the naṣṣ. Rather, they would have hastened to him and entrusted him with the matter, and no one would have contested it. For those who assumed the office among them did not do so out of desire for status, position, or leadership, nor out of greed for accumulating worldly goods or enjoying bodily pleasures and natural desires. Rather, they assumed it to fulfill the rights of religion, to exact the rights of God Almighty, to strengthen the community, to strive for the exaltation of God’s word, to subdue His enemies, and to reinforce the structure of Islam. Do you not see al-Ṣiddīq, may God be pleased with him, how he treated the people, how he rejected desires, was satisfied with little food and coarse clothing, and contented himself with a small amount...
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage contains the author's positive assertion that the imamate is established by ijtihād (independent reasoning) and his proof against the claim of a nass (explicit text). The author begins by stating that the correct way to establish the imamate for a qualified person is through ijtihād, analogous to how rulings are deduced for novel legal questions. He then declares the claims of the Rāfiḍa (Shī‘a) and Rāwandiyya regarding a nass on ‘Alī or al-‘Abbās are false. Next, he provides a proof (dalīl) for the falsehood of any nass: the caliphate is a universal matter of great importance, and if such a nass existed, it would have become as widely known as the fundamental rituals of Islam (qibla, number of rak‘as, zakāt obligations). Since no such widely transmitted report exists, it proves definitively that no nass existed. He then reinforces this with a second argument (yadullu ‘alayhi anna): if there had been a nass, the Companions, known for their piety and scrupulous adherence to the Sharī‘a, would never have ignored it; they would have immediately accepted the designated person. Instead, they chose the caliph through consultation, and their motives were purely religious—not worldly ambition, as demonstrated by the example of Abū Bakr al-Ṣiddīq's simple lifestyle and lack of personal desire. The argument uses the unanimity of the Companions' practice as evidence that no contrary nass existed.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihād)
النص (al-naṣṣ)
الروافض (al-rawāfiḍ)
الراوندية (al-rāwandiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think imamate must be explicitly appointed by a divine text (nass).
Correction: The author argues that imamate is established through ijtihad, just like other legal rulings, and no nass exists for it.

Checkpoint
How does the author say the imamate is established?
A) By explicit nass from the Prophet
B) By ijtihad and opinion
C) By hereditary succession
D) By popular vote

Key Takeaway
Imamate is established through ijtihad, not through a divine text; claims of a nass are false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an important decision for which there is no clear guidance from revelation, do you trust the process of ijtihad (reasoned judgment) or do you secretly wish for a divinely appointed answer? Why?
?
The author calls the Shia claim 'bāṭil' (false). Is there a belief you hold that you have never truly examined with the same rigor? Dare you test it today against the evidence?
?
If you accepted that imamate is a matter of human reasoning, how would that change your attitude toward leadership and authority in your own community? Are you willing to take responsibility for choosing leaders through consultation and ijtihad?

The Proof from Widespread Transmission
Author provides proof from absence of widespread transmission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that if a nass for imamate existed, it would be as famous as the basic rituals of Islam, and its absence proves no nass.

Core Idea
If a nass existed, it would be universally known like the qibla—but it isn't.

The author now provides his first substantive proof. He reasons that the caliphate concerns all Muslims, so any divine appointment would have been transmitted as widely as the direction of prayer or the number of rak'as. Since no such report exists, there is no nass. This is a powerful argument from common sense and historical fact.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the analogy
The author compares the imamate to other universally known matters: qibla, rak'as, times of prayer, zakāt obligations. Understand why he chooses these examples.
These are matters that every Muslim knows and that are transmitted by the entire community (tawātur).
2
Identify the logical structure
The author uses a conditional: 'if there were a nass, it would be famous.' Then he checks the historical record: no famous report. Therefore, no nass. This is a valid deductive argument.
3
See how he applies it
He moves from the general principle to the specific case of the caliphate. The caliphate is a general matter (amr 'āmm) affecting everyone, so the same rule applies.
4
Confirm the conclusion
The author concludes definitively: 'دل أن لا نص فيه البتة'—this indicates absolutely no nass.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم طريق ثبوت الإمامة لمن يصلح أهلاً لها على سبيل الاجتهاد واستعمال الرأي على ما يستعمل في الحوادث الشرعية التي بالناس إلى معرفة أحكامها حاجة وقد عدموا فيها النص إذ لا نص ها هنا، وقول الروافض بوجود النص من النبي عليه السلام على علي رضي الله عنه وقول الراوندية بوجود النص على العباس رضي الله عنه باطل. رد القول بالنص في الإمامة والدليل على بطلان النص أن أمر الخلافة أمر عام يقع بكل الناس إلى معرفته حاجة ماسة، وما هذا سبيله كان النص فيه لو كان ثابتا يشتهر اشتهارا لا يبقى معه على أحد من الناس خفاء، ولا اضطر الناس إلى معرفته كالنص على القبلة وعلى أعداد ركعات الصلاة ومقاديرها وأوقاتها ومقادير النصيب والواجبات في باب الزكاة، وغير ذلك من الأمور العامة، ولما لم يوجد في ذلك خبر هذا سبيله، دل أن لا نص فيه البتة، يدل عليه أن النص لو كان ثابتا لما أعرضت الصحابة مع جلالة أقدارهم في الدين وشدة ورعهم وتمسكهم بالدين وتحرزهم عن مخالفة الشريعة عن قبوله والعمل به وتفويض الأمر إلى المنصوص عليه، بل بادروا إليه وولوه الأمر ولم ينازعه أحد في ذلك إذ لم يكن تقلد من تقلد للأمر منهم رغبة في الجاه والمنزلة والترأس ولا حرصا على جمع الحطام والاستمتاع باللذات البدنية والشهوات الطبيعية بل كان تقلدهم لذلك للقيام بحقوق الدين واستيفاء حقوق الله تعالى وتقوية الملة والسعى في إعلاء كلمة الله تعالى وقهر أعدائه وتشييد بنيان الإسلام ألا ترى الصديق رضي الله عنه كيف كانت معاملته الخلق ورفضه الشهوات والاكتفاء باليسير من القوت والخشن من الثياب والاجتزاء بقليل
Then the method of establishing the imamate for one who is qualified for it is by way of ijtihād and the exercise of opinion, as is done in the case of legal incidents (ḥawādith shar‘iyya) whose rulings people need to know, and they have no text concerning them, for there is no text here. The saying of the Rāfiḍa that there is a text from the Prophet, peace be upon him, concerning ‘Alī, may God be pleased with him, and the saying of the Rāwandiyya that there is a text concerning al-‘Abbās, may God be pleased with him, are false. Refutation of the claim of text (naṣṣ) in the imamate. The proof of the falsehood of the naṣṣ is that the matter of the caliphate is a general matter whose knowledge is needed by all people, and something of this sort, if a naṣṣ existed concerning it, would become so famous that no one would remain ignorant of it, and people would not be confused about knowing it, like the naṣṣ on the direction of prayer (qibla), on the numbers of rak‘as of prayer, their measures, their times, the measures of shares and obligations in the chapter of zakāt, and other general matters. Since no report of this sort has been found concerning it, this indicates that there is absolutely no naṣṣ about it. This is supported by the fact that if the naṣṣ had existed, the Companions—despite the greatness of their rank in religion, the intensity of their piety, their adherence to religion, and their cautiousness against violating the Sharī‘a—would not have turned away from accepting it and acting upon it, and from entrusting the matter to the one designated by the naṣṣ. Rather, they would have hastened to him and entrusted him with the matter, and no one would have contested it. For those who assumed the office among them did not do so out of desire for status, position, or leadership, nor out of greed for accumulating worldly goods or enjoying bodily pleasures and natural desires. Rather, they assumed it to fulfill the rights of religion, to exact the rights of God Almighty, to strengthen the community, to strive for the exaltation of God’s word, to subdue His enemies, and to reinforce the structure of Islam. Do you not see al-Ṣiddīq, may God be pleased with him, how he treated the people, how he rejected desires, was satisfied with little food and coarse clothing, and contented himself with a small amount...
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage contains the author's positive assertion that the imamate is established by ijtihād (independent reasoning) and his proof against the claim of a nass (explicit text). The author begins by stating that the correct way to establish the imamate for a qualified person is through ijtihād, analogous to how rulings are deduced for novel legal questions. He then declares the claims of the Rāfiḍa (Shī‘a) and Rāwandiyya regarding a nass on ‘Alī or al-‘Abbās are false. Next, he provides a proof (dalīl) for the falsehood of any nass: the caliphate is a universal matter of great importance, and if such a nass existed, it would have become as widely known as the fundamental rituals of Islam (qibla, number of rak‘as, zakāt obligations). Since no such widely transmitted report exists, it proves definitively that no nass existed. He then reinforces this with a second argument (yadullu ‘alayhi anna): if there had been a nass, the Companions, known for their piety and scrupulous adherence to the Sharī‘a, would never have ignored it; they would have immediately accepted the designated person. Instead, they chose the caliph through consultation, and their motives were purely religious—not worldly ambition, as demonstrated by the example of Abū Bakr al-Ṣiddīq's simple lifestyle and lack of personal desire. The argument uses the unanimity of the Companions' practice as evidence that no contrary nass existed.

Key Terms
النص (al-naṣṣ)
الخلافة (al-khilāfa)
التواتر (al-tawātur)
القبلة (al-qibla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that a nass might exist but be hidden or suppressed.
Correction: The author argues that such a public matter cannot be hidden; if it existed, it would be as obvious as the qibla.

Checkpoint
What does the author compare the imamate to in order to prove no nass exists?
A) The rules of trade
B) The qibla and numbers of rak'as
C) The laws of inheritance
D) The stories of the prophets

Key Takeaway
The absence of a widely known nass for imamate proves that no nass exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learned that matters of universal concern would be widely known if they were divinely decreed. Think of a matter in your own life that you claim is important or commanded by Allah. Has the knowledge of it been spread widely as a sign of its authenticity? Or is it a private interpretation? How do you know that your understanding is not a ‘hidden nass’? What would it take for you to hold it up to the standard of tawātur?
?
The author uses the absence of widespread transmission to reject the claim of a nass for imamate. What if you applied the same principle to your own cherished beliefs? Are there beliefs you hold that you assume are from the Qur'an or Sunnah but which are not universally known or practiced? Could the silence of the community be a sign that you are mistaken?
?
Consider the qibla, prayer rak'as, and zakāt – they are known by all Muslims. Does your practice align with these universally transmitted obligations? Or do you follow paths less traveled, comfortable in the thought that the majority might be wrong? This lesson warns about claiming a nass that is not widely known. How does that challenge your reliance on personal or sectarian readings?

The Proof from the Companions' Behavior
Author argues from Companions' conduct that no nass existed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Companions' unanimous practice of not following a nass proves that no nass existed, and that their motives were purely religious.

Core Idea
The Companions were too pious to ignore a divine command—their silence proves no command existed.

The author now strengthens his argument by appealing to the character of the Companions. If a nass had existed, these paragons of piety would never have abandoned it. Their actual practice—electing Abu Bakr through consultation—shows that no nass existed. He also highlights the selflessness of the caliphs, especially Abu Bakr, to counter any suspicion of worldly motives.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the conditional
The author uses a counterfactual: 'if a nass had existed, the Companions would not have turned away from it.' Then he observes that they did turn away—so no nass.
2
Appreciate the description of the Companions
The author emphasizes their piety (jallālat aqdārihim, shiddat wara'ihim) to show that they would never disobey a clear divine command.
3
Understand the negative motive
The author argues that those who assumed the caliphate did so not out of desire for power, but to uphold religion. This counters the Shia claim that they usurped the caliphate.
4
See the example of Abu Bakr
The author gives a specific example: Abu Bakr's simple lifestyle and lack of worldly desires prove his intentions were pure.

ثم طريق ثبوت الإمامة لمن يصلح أهلاً لها على سبيل الاجتهاد واستعمال الرأي على ما يستعمل في الحوادث الشرعية التي بالناس إلى معرفة أحكامها حاجة وقد عدموا فيها النص إذ لا نص ها هنا، وقول الروافض بوجود النص من النبي عليه السلام على علي رضي الله عنه وقول الراوندية بوجود النص على العباس رضي الله عنه باطل. رد القول بالنص في الإمامة والدليل على بطلان النص أن أمر الخلافة أمر عام يقع بكل الناس إلى معرفته حاجة ماسة، وما هذا سبيله كان النص فيه لو كان ثابتا يشتهر اشتهارا لا يبقى معه على أحد من الناس خفاء، ولا اضطر الناس إلى معرفته كالنص على القبلة وعلى أعداد ركعات الصلاة ومقاديرها وأوقاتها ومقادير النصيب والواجبات في باب الزكاة، وغير ذلك من الأمور العامة، ولما لم يوجد في ذلك خبر هذا سبيله، دل أن لا نص فيه البتة، يدل عليه أن النص لو كان ثابتا لما أعرضت الصحابة مع جلالة أقدارهم في الدين وشدة ورعهم وتمسكهم بالدين وتحرزهم عن مخالفة الشريعة عن قبوله والعمل به وتفويض الأمر إلى المنصوص عليه، بل بادروا إليه وولوه الأمر ولم ينازعه أحد في ذلك إذ لم يكن تقلد من تقلد للأمر منهم رغبة في الجاه والمنزلة والترأس ولا حرصا على جمع الحطام والاستمتاع باللذات البدنية والشهوات الطبيعية بل كان تقلدهم لذلك للقيام بحقوق الدين واستيفاء حقوق الله تعالى وتقوية الملة والسعى في إعلاء كلمة الله تعالى وقهر أعدائه وتشييد بنيان الإسلام ألا ترى الصديق رضي الله عنه كيف كانت معاملته الخلق ورفضه الشهوات والاكتفاء باليسير من القوت والخشن من الثياب والاجتزاء بقليل
Then the method of establishing the imamate for one who is qualified for it is by way of ijtihād and the exercise of opinion, as is done in the case of legal incidents (ḥawādith shar‘iyya) whose rulings people need to know, and they have no text concerning them, for there is no text here.
