How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says the Companions did not seek leadership for 'rind and pleasure'. Examine your own ambitions: when you seek a position of authority, is it truly for the sake of Allah, or is there a hidden desire for status and comfort? What concrete step will you take today to purify your intention in your current role?
?
If you had been among the Companions, would your desire for power have clouded your judgment? The author argues their piety prevented them from ignoring a divine command. Where in your life have you ignored a clear command of Allah because it conflicted with your personal desires? What will you do to change that starting now?
?
The Companions valued collective consultation over personal preference. Think of a recent decision in your family or community where you insisted on your own way. How can you emulate the Companions' example of putting unity and truth above your own opinion? What is one specific situation where you can practice this today?

The Rational Method for Knowing God and Truth
Nasafi presents a rational proof for the necessity of reflection in knowing God and truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi argues that rational reflection (nazar and fikr) is the essential method for knowing God, prophethood, and distinguishing truth from falsehood, and that it is not limited to explicit scriptural texts.

Core Idea
Rational reflection is the universal method for knowing God and truth, not just texts.

Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi opens his discussion by challenging the idea that knowledge of divine laws is confined to explicit scriptural texts. He argues that wisdom itself requires testing and obligation through ways that engage those with understanding. This lesson presents his rational proof starting from the self-evident origination of the self, leading to knowledge of the Originator and then to prophethood and truthfulness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the opening claim
Identify Nasafi's rejection of the idea that knowledge is limited to nass. He sets up a rational framework.
2
Follow the step-by-step rational argument
Trace the argument from self-origination to knowledge of the Originator, then to prophets and testimonies. Notice how each step builds on the previous.
The argument is based on necessity (darura) and reflection (nazar).
3
Understand the role of reflection and supplication
Nasafi concludes that reflection, combined with supplication, is the path to truth. This is the method he defends.
He emphasizes that success comes from Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس طريق بيان الشرائع بمقصور على النص ليقال إنه لو لم ينص فقد ضيع أمر الأمة بل من الحكمة إيجاب الامتحان والتكليف بوجوه يختلف فيها أولو الألباب كما في سائر المواضع التي لا نص فيها، أليس أن كل ذي عقل يعقل بالضرورة حدوث نفسه ثم يعرف بالتدبير والتأمل حدوث كل محسوس لم يشهد ابتداءه، ثم يعقل كون كل حدوث بمحدث أحدثه بفضل تدبر فيه ونظر، ثم يعرف المحدث بعد طول النظر في أمره أنه يوصف بأنه ما هو أجسم أم عرض أم غير جسم ولا عرض؟ ويوصف بالعلم في الأزل أو الحدوث، وكذا القدرة وجميع الصفات ثم معرفة الرسل والأنبياء عليهم السلام، ثم معرفة صدق الأخبار وكذبها، ثم قبول الشهادات وردها. إن ذلك كله أو تمامه كان طرق الحق في ذلك من الباطل النظر والفكر مع التضرع إلى رب العالمين في التوفيق لذلك والهداية إليه فإنه الجواد الكريم، وإذا ثبت ذا لم يكن لأحد منع جواز محنة الصحابة بعد قبضه عليه السلام بمعرفة الأصلح للخلافة، والعقد له بما سبق من أدلة نظره للمسلمين وشفقته عليهم وتعظيمه أمر الله تعالى وقلة مبالاته في ذات الله تعالى بلومة اللائمين وطعن الملحدين واهتمامه لما عليه من أمر المؤمنين، وليست الروافض بأول طائفة من أهل الزيغ جحدت أمرًا قصرت عقولهم عن الوقوف على وجه الحكمة فيه، غير أنهم الجهالة اختصوا بالطعن على الصحابة، وعلى من أرادوا إثبات الحق له وعلى سلف الأمة بل على الله تعالى وعلى رسوله ما قررنا وبالله العصمة والنجاة عن كل ضلالة وبدعة.
And the way of clarifying the laws is not confined to the text (al-nass) so that it could be said that if He had not specified (by text), He would have neglected the affair of the community. Rather, it is from wisdom to require testing and obligation by ways in which people of understanding differ, as in other matters where there is no text. Is it not that every intelligent person necessarily understands the origination (huduth) of his own self, then knows by deliberation and reflection the origination of every sensed thing whose beginning he did not witness, then understands that every originated thing has an originator who originated it by virtue of deliberation and reflection, then knows the originator after long reflection on His affair—that He is described: Is He a body, an accident, or neither body nor accident? And He is described by knowledge in pre-eternity or origination, and likewise power and all attributes. Then [comes] the knowledge of messengers and prophets (peace be upon them), then the knowledge of the truthfulness of reports and their falsehood, then the acceptance of testimonies and their rejection. All of that or its completion—the ways of truth therein from falsehood—are reflection and thought, along with supplication to the Lord of the worlds for success therein and guidance to it, for He is the Generous, the Kind. And if this is established, then no one can prevent the permissibility of testing the Companions after his (peace be upon him) death concerning knowing the most suitable for the caliphate and contracting for him based on what preceded of proofs of his concern for the Muslims, his compassion for them, his veneration of the command of Allah Almighty, his disregard in the matter of Allah Almighty for the blame of the blamers and the slander of the atheists, and his concern for the affairs of the believers. And the Rafida are not the first sect among the people of deviation that denied a matter in which their intellects fell short of grasping the aspect of wisdom. Rather, in their ignorance, they specially attacked the Companions, and those for whom they wanted to establish the truth, and the predecessors of the ummah, nay, even Allah and His Messenger, as we have explained. And with Allah is protection and salvation from all misguidance and innovation.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the rational method in establishing both legal rulings (shara'i') and theological truths. The author, Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, begins by rejecting the idea that understanding God's laws is limited to explicit scriptural texts (nass). He argues that wisdom requires testing and obligation through various ways that differ among people of intellect, just as in other matters where no text exists. He then presents a step-by-step rational proof: every rational person necessarily understands the origination (huduth) of their own self through immediate intuition. Then, through deliberation and reflection, they realize the origination of all sensed things they did not witness from the beginning. Then they understand that every originated thing requires an originator (muhdith). Then, after extended reflection, they come to know the attributes of the Originator—whether He is a body, accident, or neither, and that He is described by knowledge and power, whether eternal or originated. This leads to recognizing the truth of prophets and messengers, then to distinguishing true reports from false ones, and finally to accepting or rejecting testimonies. Nasafi emphasizes that all this is attained through reflection and thought, accompanied by supplication to Allah for guidance. Once this rational foundation is established, he argues, no one can object to the Companions' method of selecting a caliph after the Prophet's death—namely, their effort to identify the most suitable person and contract the caliphate to him based on their concern for the Muslims and fear of Allah. He then addresses the Rafida (a sect that rejected the caliphates of Abu Bakr and 'Umar), stating that they are not the first deviant group to deny a matter beyond their intellectual grasp. The Rafida, in their ignorance, specifically attacked the Companions, the ones they wanted to establish truth for, the early community, and even Allah and His Messenger. The passage concludes with a prayer for divine protection from all misguidance and innovation.

Key Terms
نص (nass)
حدوث (huduth)
محدث (muhdith)
نظر (nazar)
فكر (fikr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of God's laws can only come from explicit scriptural texts.
Correction: Nasafi argues that rational reflection is the primary method, as even understanding nass requires reason, and wisdom demands testing through reason.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first thing every intelligent person necessarily understands?
A) The existence of God
B) The origination (huduth) of his own self
C) The truth of prophethood
D) The distinction between true and false reports

Key Takeaway
Rational reflection on creation leads necessarily to knowledge of the Creator, prophethood, and truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever truly and personally engaged in the reflection that Nasafi describes—starting from your own origination to know your Lord? If not, what is holding you back from beginning this essential journey right now?
?
This passage emphasizes that reflection is accompanied by supplication. When you think about Allah's existence and attributes, do you humbly ask Him for guidance, or do you rely solely on your intellect as if it were sufficient without His help?
?
Nasafi says that reflection leads to distinguishing true reports from false. In an age of misinformation, are you actively using the rational method he outlines to evaluate what you hear about Islam and the world, or do you passively accept what is convenient?

Applying the Rational Method: Caliphate Selection and Refuting the Rafida
Nasafi defends the Companions' caliphate selection and refutes the Rafida.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi applies the rational method to defend the Companions' selection of the caliph and refute the Rafida's objections.

Core Idea
The rational method validates the Companions' caliphate selection and exposes deviant sects.

Having established that rational reflection is the proper method for truth, Nasafi now applies it to a historical case: the Companions' method of choosing Abu Bakr as caliph after the Prophet's death. He argues that their process, based on concern for the ummah and fear of Allah, was rational and valid. He then refutes the Rafida, who rejected this, attributing their denial to intellectual shortcoming.

Guided Path
1
Read how the rational method justifies the Companions' actions
Notice the conditional: if the rational method is established, then no one can prevent the permissibility of testing the Companions. Nasafi lists their virtues.
2
Identify Nasafi's defense and refutation of the Rafida
He states the Rafida are not the first deviant sect to deny what they cannot comprehend. Their attack is on the Companions, scholars, and even Allah.
The refutation is based on their intellectual limitation.
3
Reflect on the broader principle
The rational method is not just for theology but also for historical and political matters, using ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس طريق بيان الشرائع بمقصور على النص ليقال إنه لو لم ينص فقد ضيع أمر الأمة بل من الحكمة إيجاب الامتحان والتكليف بوجوه يختلف فيها أولو الألباب كما في سائر المواضع التي لا نص فيها، أليس أن كل ذي عقل يعقل بالضرورة حدوث نفسه ثم يعرف بالتدبير والتأمل حدوث كل محسوس لم يشهد ابتداءه، ثم يعقل كون كل حدوث بمحدث أحدثه بفضل تدبر فيه ونظر، ثم يعرف المحدث بعد طول النظر في أمره أنه يوصف بأنه ما هو أجسم أم عرض أم غير جسم ولا عرض؟ ويوصف بالعلم في الأزل أو الحدوث، وكذا القدرة وجميع الصفات ثم معرفة الرسل والأنبياء عليهم السلام، ثم معرفة صدق الأخبار وكذبها، ثم قبول الشهادات وردها. إن ذلك كله أو تمامه كان طرق الحق في ذلك من الباطل النظر والفكر مع التضرع إلى رب العالمين في التوفيق لذلك والهداية إليه فإنه الجواد الكريم، وإذا ثبت ذا لم يكن لأحد منع جواز محنة الصحابة بعد قبضه عليه السلام بمعرفة الأصلح للخلافة، والعقد له بما سبق من أدلة نظره للمسلمين وشفقته عليهم وتعظيمه أمر الله تعالى وقلة مبالاته في ذات الله تعالى بلومة اللائمين وطعن الملحدين واهتمامه لما عليه من أمر المؤمنين، وليست الروافض بأول طائفة من أهل الزيغ جحدت أمرًا قصرت عقولهم عن الوقوف على وجه الحكمة فيه، غير أنهم الجهالة اختصوا بالطعن على الصحابة، وعلى من أرادوا إثبات الحق له وعلى سلف الأمة بل على الله تعالى وعلى رسوله ما قررنا وبالله العصمة والنجاة عن كل ضلالة وبدعة.
And the way of clarifying the laws is not confined to the text (al-nass) so that it could be said that if He had not specified (by text), He would have neglected the affair of the community. Rather, it is from wisdom to require testing and obligation by ways in which people of understanding differ, as in other matters where there is no text. Is it not that every intelligent person necessarily understands the origination (huduth) of his own self, then knows by deliberation and reflection the origination of every sensed thing whose beginning he did not witness, then understands that every originated thing has an originator who originated it by virtue of deliberation and reflection, then knows the originator after long reflection on His affair—that He is described: Is He a body, an accident, or neither body nor accident? And He is described by knowledge in pre-eternity or origination, and likewise power and all attributes. Then [comes] the knowledge of messengers and prophets (peace be upon them), then the knowledge of the truthfulness of reports and their falsehood, then the acceptance of testimonies and their rejection. All of that or its completion—the ways of truth therein from falsehood—are reflection and thought, along with supplication to the Lord of the worlds for success therein and guidance to it, for He is the Generous, the Kind. And if this is established, then no one can prevent the permissibility of testing the Companions after his (peace be upon him) death concerning knowing the most suitable for the caliphate and contracting for him based on what preceded of proofs of his concern for the Muslims, his compassion for them, his veneration of the command of Allah Almighty, his disregard in the matter of Allah Almighty for the blame of the blamers and the slander of the atheists, and his concern for the affairs of the believers. And the Rafida are not the first sect among the people of deviation that denied a matter in which their intellects fell short of grasping the aspect of wisdom. Rather, in their ignorance, they specially attacked the Companions, and those for whom they wanted to establish the truth, and the predecessors of the ummah, nay, even Allah and His Messenger, as we have explained. And with Allah is protection and salvation from all misguidance and innovation.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the rational method in establishing both legal rulings (shara'i') and theological truths. The author, Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, begins by rejecting the idea that understanding God's laws is limited to explicit scriptural texts (nass). He argues that wisdom requires testing and obligation through various ways that differ among people of intellect, just as in other matters where no text exists. He then presents a step-by-step rational proof: every rational person necessarily understands the origination (huduth) of their own self through immediate intuition. Then, through deliberation and reflection, they realize the origination of all sensed things they did not witness from the beginning. Then they understand that every originated thing requires an originator (muhdith). Then, after extended reflection, they come to know the attributes of the Originator—whether He is a body, accident, or neither, and that He is described by knowledge and power, whether eternal or originated. This leads to recognizing the truth of prophets and messengers, then to distinguishing true reports from false ones, and finally to accepting or rejecting testimonies. Nasafi emphasizes that all this is attained through reflection and thought, accompanied by supplication to Allah for guidance. Once this rational foundation is established, he argues, no one can object to the Companions' method of selecting a caliph after the Prophet's death—namely, their effort to identify the most suitable person and contract the caliphate to him based on their concern for the Muslims and fear of Allah. He then addresses the Rafida (a sect that rejected the caliphates of Abu Bakr and 'Umar), stating that they are not the first deviant group to deny a matter beyond their intellectual grasp. The Rafida, in their ignorance, specifically attacked the Companions, the ones they wanted to establish truth for, the early community, and even Allah and His Messenger. The passage concludes with a prayer for divine protection from all misguidance and innovation.

Key Terms
إمامة (imama)
روافض (rawafid)
صحابة (sahaba)
محنة (mihna)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Companions' selection of Abu Bakr was based on partiality or lack of divine guidance.
Correction: Nasafi argues that their method was rational, based on their concern for the ummah and veneration of Allah, thus valid and justified.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the reason the Rafida denied the Companions' method?
A) They had a superior rational argument
B) Their intellects fell short of grasping the wisdom
C) They were following explicit texts
D) They were envious of the Companions

Key Takeaway
Rational reflection validates the Companions' ijtihad and refutes sectarian objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a belief that contradicts your understanding, do you humbly examine your own intellect's limitations, or do you quickly attack those who hold the opposing view, like the Rafida attacked the Companions? What would it look like to assume you might be missing wisdom?
?
The Companions sought the most suitable leader based on prophetic qualities. In your own life, how often do you prioritize concern and compassion for others when making decisions, or do you rely on your own preferences? Will you commit to evaluating your choices with the same criteria?
?
Nasafi ends with a prayer for protection from misguidance. What is one area of your faith where you feel you might be at risk of falling into a deviation, and what concrete step will you take today to seek that protection?

Consensus as a Decisive Proof for Certain Knowledge
Nasafi asserts consensus as a decisive proof for certain knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi considers the consensus of the Companions on Abu Bakr's caliphate to yield certain knowledge equal to the Quran and prophetic reports.

Core Idea
Consensus is not just probable—it gives absolute certainty like the Quran.

This brief but powerful passage from Nasafi anchors the doctrine of Abu Bakr's caliphate in a methodological principle: the consensus of the Companions is a definitive proof. Before we can evaluate the historical claim, we need to understand why Nasafi places ijma on the same epistemic level as the Quran and Prophetic reports. This short statement encapsulates the Sunni stance on the authority of early consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Notice the concise and forceful style.
2
Identify the claim and its support
Nasafi states that consensus is a 'decisive proof' (hujja qati'a) that necessitates certain knowledge ('ilm qat'i). He then equates it to three other sources of certainty: the Quran, a directly heard prophetic report, and established signs transmitted without interruption.
This enumeration shows the high epistemic authority given to ijma.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does Nasafi emphasize that consensus yields certainty? What does that imply about the reliability of the Companions' agreement?
This connects to the broader Sunni argument for the caliphate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على صحة خلافته إجماع الصحابة عليها، والإجماع حجة قاطعة موجبة للعلم قطعًا كنص كتاب الله تعالى وخبر الرسول عليه السلام المسموع منه أو الآيات الثابتة عنه بطريق لا يتوهم فيه شبهة الانقطاع.
And the proof for the soundness of his caliphate is the consensus of the Companions upon it, and consensus is a decisive proof that necessarily necessitates certain knowledge like the text of the Book of Allah, the report of the Messenger, peace be upon him, heard from him, or the established signs from him through a path in which no suspicion of discontinuity is imagined.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage is a concise definitional and evidential statement. It asserts that the consensus (ijma) of the Companions on Abu Bakr's caliphate is a decisive proof (hujja qati'a) that gives necessary certain knowledge ('ilm qat'i). The author equates this certainty to that derived from the Quran itself, from the directly heard words of the Prophet, or from the established reports (ayat – probably meaning signs or hadiths) transmitted through an uninterrupted chain. By placing ijma on this high epistemic level, the author argues that the caliphate of Abu Bakr is not a matter of opinion but of definitive certainty.

حجة قاطعة
علم قطعي
نص
خبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think consensus is only a probable proof.
Correction: Nasafi insists consensus gives certain knowledge, equal to the Quran.

Checkpoint

A) It provides probable knowledge.
B) It provides certain knowledge equal to the Quran.
C) It is inferior to the Quran.
D) It is only binding in legal matters.

Key Takeaway
Consensus of the Companions is a decisive proof giving certain knowledge, equal to the Quran and Prophetic reports.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that the Companions' consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate gives certain knowledge. Is there any area of your faith where you resist accepting what the righteous predecessors (salaf) agreed upon? What is holding you back from submitting to that certainty?
?
If consensus is as certain as the Quran, do you treat the positions held unanimously by the early scholars with the same reverence as a clear verse from Allah? How would your daily decisions change if you truly internalized this?
?
The Prophet's reports are certain through uninterrupted chains. Do you seek out authentic knowledge from reliable sources, or do you settle for hearsay and weak narrations? What step will you take today to prioritize certain knowledge in your learning?

Methodological Note on Not Re-proving Consensus
Nasafi declines to re-prove consensus, referring to usul al-fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a theologian or jurist might choose not to reproduce well-known proofs and how this reflects scholarly economy.

Core Idea
Proving consensus is not the aim; referring to standard sources is efficient.

Nasafi assumes his reader knows the proofs for consensus from usul al-fiqh. This passage explains why he does not rehearse them here: they are already famous and accessible. The lesson highlights a methodological principle: scholars avoid redundancy and trust their readers to consult other works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal translation. Observe the author's tone and the structure of his argument.
The passage is a single conditional sentence with a conclusion.
2
Identify the author's rhetorical move
Notice that the author first acknowledges a potential objection (denying consensus as proof) and then states his response: he would prove it using well-known proofs, but he declines to repeat them.
This is a common technique in scholarly works to avoid redundancy.
3
Understand the reason for not giving proofs
The author says 'there is no point in our occupying ourselves with that in this book due to its renown in every book compiled on usul al-fiqh.' This shows he considers the proofs universally known and accessible.
The phrase 'لشهرتها' (due to its renown) is key.
4
Reflect on the methodological principle
Scholars often rely on other disciplines. Here, theology (kalam) uses the established proofs from usul al-fiqh without reproducing them. This interdisciplinary trust saves effort and keeps the focus on the topic at hand.
This is not an evasion but an efficient use of scholarly resources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو أنهم مالوا إلى إنكار كون الإجماع حجة فنثبت عليهم بالدلائل المشهورة التي أوردها أئمة الدين في إثبات كون الإجماع حجة، لا معنى لاشتغالنا في هذا الكتاب بذلك لشهرتها في كل كتاب صنف في أصول الفقه، ومن أراد الوقوف عليها لا يعدمها ولا يتعذر عليه الظفر بها.
So if they incline to denying that consensus is a proof, we would prove it against them by the well-known proofs that the imams of the religion have adduced in establishing that consensus is a proof; there is no point in our occupying ourselves with that in this book due to its renown in every book compiled on the principles of fiqh, and whoever wants to find them will not lack them nor will it be difficult for him to obtain them.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage is a methodological aside. The author anticipates a possible objection that the opponent might deny the very authority of consensus as a proof. He responds by stating that such a denial can be refuted using the well-known proofs found in usul al-fiqh works. However, he explicitly chooses not to rehearse those proofs here because they are widespread and easily accessible. The passage thus serves as a form of literary economy: the author acknowledges the existence of a standard argument but declines to reproduce it, indicating the focus of the book is on applying consensus, not proving its validity.

Key Terms
إجماع
حجة
أصول الفقه
دلائل مشهورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the author is evading the issue or that he does not consider consensus a valid proof.
Correction: The author clearly states that the proofs are well-known and available in usul al-fiqh works; he simply chooses not to repeat them to avoid redundancy.

Checkpoint
Why does the author refuse to provide the proofs for consensus in this book?
A) He does not consider consensus a valid proof.
B) The proofs are well-known and can be found in usul al-fiqh books.
C) He plans to cover them in a later chapter.
D) The opponent's argument is too weak to require refutation.

Key Takeaway
The authority of consensus is so well-established in usul al-fiqh that a work on theology can rely on that scholarship without repeating its proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a foundational Islamic belief that you have not personally verified, do you trust the scholarly consensus or do you feel the need to prove it yourself? How might your approach reflect humility or pride?
?
The author declines to reproduce proofs because they are widely known. Are there areas of your life where you avoid learning what is well-known because you assume you already know? What might you be missing?

Rules for Dealing with Rebels from Ali's Practice
Nasafi lists the rules for handling rebels as derived from Ali's conduct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proper sequence of actions in dealing with rebels as derived from Ali ibn Abi Talib's practice, according to the Maturidi tradition.

Core Idea
The method of dealing with rebels begins with dialogue, not violence.

This passage from Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla presents a comprehensive list of rules for handling rebels (khawarij and ahl al-baghi) based on the conduct of Ali ibn Abi Talib. It emphasizes a measured approach that prioritizes persuasion and restraint over immediate combat. The passage concludes with Abu Hanifa's famous acknowledgment of Ali's authority in this matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the sequence of actions described.
Pay attention to the verbs: istid'a, munazara, nabdh, etc.
2
Identify the sequence of actions
List the actions in order: first call and debate, then refrain from initiating, then proclaim, then refrain from fighting until they start, then if they start, fight but with restrictions.
This sequence shows the preferred approach.
3
Note the specific prohibitions
After fighting, there are prohibitions: not pursuing fugitives, not finishing wounded, not harming women, not seizing property.
These restrictions show restraint even in conflict.
4
Understand the significance
These rules were derived from Ali's practice and became the basis for Sunni jurisprudence on rebellion. Reflect on why Abu Hanifa praised Ali.
Ali's conduct set a precedent for handling internal conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : لولا ما ذكر أحوالهم من الوقوف على ما هو الواجب في معاملة الخوارج، ومن يبتلى بمحاربته من أهل البغي من الابتداء باستدعائهم ومناظرتهم وترك مبادأتهم، والنبذ إليهم قبل نصب الحرب معهم، والامتناع عن محاربتهم إلى أن يبتدعوا، وترك اتباع مدبرهم، وتدفيف جريحهم، والتعرض لنسوتهم، وترك اغتنام أموالهم، وغير ذلك من الأحكام التي أخذها فقهاء الأمة عن معاملة عليه وسيرته فيهم حتى قال أبو حنيفة – قدس الله روحه -: 'لولا علي ما كنا نعرف السيرة في الخوارج'.
Then we say: Were it not for what was mentioned of their conditions of stopping at what is obligatory in dealing with the Khawarij, and those who are afflicted by fighting him from the people of rebellion (ahl al-baghī) – from initiating by calling them and debating them, and refraining from initiating hostilities, and proclaiming to them before setting up war with them, and refraining from fighting them until they begin, and refraining from pursuing their fugitives, and finishing off their wounded, and exposing their women, and refraining from seizing their property, and other such rulings which the jurists of the Ummah took from the dealing of Ali and his conduct with them, until Abu Hanifa – may Allah sanctify his soul – said: 'Were it not for Ali, we would not have known the conduct regarding the Khawarij.'
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage enumerates the rules for dealing with rebels (khawarij and ahl al-baghī) as exemplified by Ali ibn Abi Talib. It states that the proper method includes first summoning them and engaging in debate, refraining from initiating combat, giving them a formal notice before war, avoiding fighting them until they start, not pursuing those who flee, not killing the wounded (ta'dif), not harming women, and not seizing property. These rulings were adopted by the jurists of the Ummah from Ali's actual practice. The passage ends with Abu Hanifa's famous acknowledgment that without Ali, they would not have known the correct conduct regarding the Khawarij. This highlights the authority of Ali's practice in deriving legal rulings for such situations.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni tradition, as exemplified by Ali and codified by early jurists like Abu Hanifa, prescribes a restrained and principled approach to dealing with rebels, beginning with dialogue and limiting violence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter someone you consider deeply wrong—whether in belief or action—does your first instinct mirror Ali's method of initiating dialogue, or do you rush to confrontation? What concrete step can you take this week to choose the path of debate before hostility?
?
The lesson lists specific restraints even in combat: do not pursue the fleeing, do not kill the wounded, do not harm women. In your own conflicts—small or large—do you observe any limits, or do you justify cruelty in the name of being right? How can you incorporate the principle of mercy even when you feel justified in anger?
?
Abu Hanifa admitted that without Ali, the community would not have known the proper conduct. Are you humble enough to recognize your debt to those who came before you in shaping your understanding of justice, or do you assume your own reasoning is sufficient? What would change if you actively sought to learn from the prophetic model embodied by Ali?

The Principle of Silence and Necessity in Discussing Companions
Nasafi weighs refraining vs necessity for hearts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that discussing the Companions' conflicts is sometimes necessary despite the command to remain silent.

Core Idea
Silence is safer, but hearts demand protection.

Nasafi opens with the ideal of refraining from discussing the Companions' internal conflicts, citing the Prophet's command. However, he immediately identifies a problem: hearts are stirred even when tongues are silent. This lesson explores how he resolves the tension between duty and necessity, leading to the conclusion that scholars must speak to safeguard beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the safer approach
Read the first part of the passage where Nasafi states that refraining from mentioning the Companions' conditions would be safer in religion and closer to the Prophet's command.
Notice the hadith 'If my companions are mentioned, withhold.'
2
Understand the heart problem
Nasafi explains that even if tongues are silent, hearts do not refrain from reacting, and that is more severe than tongues.
This is the key justification for breaking silence.
3
Identify the necessity
He acknowledges that the situation called for mentioning their conditions and informing about what occurred.
4
See the scholars' response
The preservers and theologians undertook to mention and explain these events as a safeguard for hearts from false beliefs.
They did this despite the hadith because the need was greater.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكان الكف عن ذكر أحوالهم والإغضاء عما جرى بينهم ودفع أحوالهم جملةً عن القلوب والألسن أسلم في الدين وأوفق بما مَنَّ الله علينا في تأخير انتسابنا عن الوقف الذي فيه خوف الاشتراك فيما لا يحل في الدين، والميل إلى ما الحق في غيره، وأقرب إلى ما دل عليه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بقوله: 'إذا ذكر أصحابي فأمسكوا'. وفي الخوض في أحوالهم مع إمساك اللسان انتشار القلوب لأن القلوب لا تمتنع عن فعلها وذلك أشد من الألسن، إلا أن الحالة التي بيناها دعتنا إلى ذكر أحوالهم والإخبار عما جرى بينهم، فقام حفاظ الأمة بذكرها والمتكلمون بالكشف عن وجوه ذلك، صيانةً للقلوب عن اعتقاد ما لا يحل فيهم مع ما أكرمهم الله تعالى به من صحبة النبي، ونصرة دينه، ونقل شريعته، وتبليغ وحيه، ثم إن فقهاء الأمة أخذوا ذلك عن معاملة علي، ثم يجوز أن يكون ذلك قد عرف النبي عليه السلام غيره لكنهم لم يخرجوا إلى وقت علي، ورأوا بالذي فعله هو كفاية إذ لم يغيروا عليه، وكان فعله ظاهراً بحيث عرفه الكل، وبلغ مبلغا لو ظهر منه منكر ليلزم التغيير، ويجوز أن يكون خصه النبي عليه السلام بتعليم الأحكام بما قد علم أنه يخص بالحاجة إلى ذلك، وفي قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: 'إنا نقاتل على التنزيل وأنت تقاتل على التأويل' دليل أن جميع ما فعل فعل عن علم وعلى موافقة الشريعة، والله أعلم.
Had refraining from mentioning their conditions and overlooking what occurred between them and pushing their conditions entirely from hearts and tongues been safer in religion and more suitable with what Allah has granted us in delaying our affiliation from the pause which contains fear of sharing in what is not permissible in religion, and inclining towards what is right in other than it, and closer to what the Messenger of Allah indicated by his saying: 'When my companions are mentioned, withhold.' And delving into their conditions while withholding the tongue causes hearts to spread because hearts do not refrain from their action, and that is more severe than tongues. Except that the situation which we have explained called us to mention their conditions and inform about what occurred between them. So the preservers of the Ummah undertook their mention and the theologians (al-mutakallimūn) uncovered the aspects of that, as a safeguard for hearts from believing what is not permissible concerning them, along with what Allah the Exalted honored them with of companionship of the Prophet, aiding His religion, transmitting His Sharia, and conveying His revelation. Then indeed the jurists of the Ummah took that from the dealing of Ali. Then it is possible that the Prophet had taught others besides him, but they did not come out until the time of Ali, and they saw what he did as sufficient since they did not object to it, and his action was apparent such that everyone knew it, and it reached a level that if an objectionable [act] had appeared from him, it would have necessitated change. And it is possible that the Prophet specifically taught him the rulings because he knew that he would be specifically in need of that. And in his saying – peace be upon him –: 'We fight according to the revelation, and you fight according to the interpretation' is evidence that all that he did was done from knowledge and in accordance with the Sharia. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage discusses the tension between the religious recommendation to remain silent about conflicts among the Companions (based on the hadith 'When my companions are mentioned, withhold') and the practical necessity of addressing them. It begins by acknowledging that complete silence and ignoring the events would be safer and more in line with the hadith. However, it argues that while tongues can be silent, hearts are still affected by the events, and the internal turmoil is more severe than outward speech. Therefore, the situation necessitated that the preservers of the Ummah (huffāẓ) and the theologians (mutakallimūn) record and explain the events, in order to safeguard hearts from believing falsehoods about the Companions, given their high status. The passage then notes that the jurists derived rulings from Ali's conduct, and explains why Ali's actions are authoritative: the Prophet may have taught others, but those rulings came to light only through Ali; Ali's actions were known to all and no one objected, which implies their correctness; and the Prophet's statement 'We fight according to the revelation, and you fight according to the interpretation' proves that Ali's actions were based on knowledge and in harmony with the Sharia.

Key Terms
إمساك
الخوض في أحوالهم
حفاظ الأمة
متكلمون
صيانة للقلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That discussing the conflicts of the Companions is always forbidden.
Correction: The passage shows it is sometimes necessary to protect correct beliefs and hearts from false notions.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why is it necessary to discuss the conflicts of the Companions despite the hadith 'When my companions are mentioned, withhold'?
A) Because the hadith is weak.
B) Because hearts are affected even if tongues are silent, so clarification is needed to prevent false beliefs.
C) Because the Companions themselves asked for it.
D) Because it is easier to talk than to remain silent.

Key Takeaway
The command to remain silent must be balanced against the need to protect hearts from false beliefs about the Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about conflicts among the Companions, what is the first assumption that arises in your heart? How does this passage challenge you to replace assumption with trust and silence—or to seek correct knowledge?
?
Think of a time you held a silent judgment about a person from the early generations without verifying the facts. How might that hidden thought have affected your love for them? Will you commit to training your heart to withhold until knowledge is obtained?
?
Your heart is constantly forming beliefs about the Companions whether you speak or not. What false notions might you be harboring right now? After reading this passage, what specific action will you take today to correct them?

Method of Learning

The Authority of Ali's Conduct as Precedent
Nasafi provides rationale for relying on Ali's conduct

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three reasons Nasafi gives for relying on Ali's actions as a valid source for judging the Companions' conflicts.

Core Idea
Ali's public conduct, unchallenged and prophetically endorsed, is authoritative.

After establishing the general necessity of discussing the Companions' affairs, Nasafi turns to a specific source: the conduct of Ali. He provides three rationales for why Ali's actions are a valid precedent, culminating in a Prophetic hadith that proves Ali acted with knowledge and in accordance with Sharia.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Ali
Read the second part of the passage where Nasafi discusses how the jurists took from Ali's dealings.
2
Identify the first reason
The Prophet may have taught others, but the rulings only emerged through Ali at his time.
This suggests Ali was the chosen conveyer.
3
Understand the second reason
Ali's actions were public and no one objected; silence implies approval and correctness.
4
Grasp the third reason
The Prophet's hadith 'We fight according to revelation, you according to interpretation' proves Ali acted on knowledge and in harmony with Sharia.
This is the strongest evidence.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
All together, these reasons establish that Ali's conduct is a reliable source for understanding the events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكان الكف عن ذكر أحوالهم والإغضاء عما جرى بينهم ودفع أحوالهم جملةً عن القلوب والألسن أسلم في الدين وأوفق بما مَنَّ الله علينا في تأخير انتسابنا عن الوقف الذي فيه خوف الاشتراك فيما لا يحل في الدين، والميل إلى ما الحق في غيره، وأقرب إلى ما دل عليه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بقوله: 'إذا ذكر أصحابي فأمسكوا'. وفي الخوض في أحوالهم مع إمساك اللسان انتشار القلوب لأن القلوب لا تمتنع عن فعلها وذلك أشد من الألسن، إلا أن الحالة التي بيناها دعتنا إلى ذكر أحوالهم والإخبار عما جرى بينهم، فقام حفاظ الأمة بذكرها والمتكلمون بالكشف عن وجوه ذلك، صيانةً للقلوب عن اعتقاد ما لا يحل فيهم مع ما أكرمهم الله تعالى به من صحبة النبي، ونصرة دينه، ونقل شريعته، وتبليغ وحيه، ثم إن فقهاء الأمة أخذوا ذلك عن معاملة علي، ثم يجوز أن يكون ذلك قد عرف النبي عليه السلام غيره لكنهم لم يخرجوا إلى وقت علي، ورأوا بالذي فعله هو كفاية إذ لم يغيروا عليه، وكان فعله ظاهراً بحيث عرفه الكل، وبلغ مبلغا لو ظهر منه منكر ليلزم التغيير، ويجوز أن يكون خصه النبي عليه السلام بتعليم الأحكام بما قد علم أنه يخص بالحاجة إلى ذلك، وفي قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: 'إنا نقاتل على التنزيل وأنت تقاتل على التأويل' دليل أن جميع ما فعل فعل عن علم وعلى موافقة الشريعة، والله أعلم.
Had refraining from mentioning their conditions and overlooking what occurred between them and pushing their conditions entirely from hearts and tongues been safer in religion and more suitable with what Allah has granted us in delaying our affiliation from the pause which contains fear of sharing in what is not permissible in religion, and inclining towards what is right in other than it, and closer to what the Messenger of Allah indicated by his saying: 'When my companions are mentioned, withhold.' And delving into their conditions while withholding the tongue causes hearts to spread because hearts do not refrain from their action, and that is more severe than tongues. Except that the situation which we have explained called us to mention their conditions and inform about what occurred between them. So the preservers of the Ummah undertook their mention and the theologians (al-mutakallimūn) uncovered the aspects of that, as a safeguard for hearts from believing what is not permissible concerning them, along with what Allah the Exalted honored them with of companionship of the Prophet, aiding His religion, transmitting His Sharia, and conveying His revelation. Then indeed the jurists of the Ummah took that from the dealing of Ali. Then it is possible that the Prophet had taught others besides him, but they did not come out until the time of Ali, and they saw what he did as sufficient since they did not object to it, and his action was apparent such that everyone knew it, and it reached a level that if an objectionable [act] had appeared from him, it would have necessitated change. And it is possible that the Prophet specifically taught him the rulings because he knew that he would be specifically in need of that. And in his saying – peace be upon him –: 'We fight according to the revelation, and you fight according to the interpretation' is evidence that all that he did was done from knowledge and in accordance with the Sharia. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage discusses the tension between the religious recommendation to remain silent about conflicts among the Companions (based on the hadith 'When my companions are mentioned, withhold') and the practical necessity of addressing them. It begins by acknowledging that complete silence and ignoring the events would be safer and more in line with the hadith. However, it argues that while tongues can be silent, hearts are still affected by the events, and the internal turmoil is more severe than outward speech. Therefore, the situation necessitated that the preservers of the Ummah (huffāẓ) and the theologians (mutakallimūn) record and explain the events, in order to safeguard hearts from believing falsehoods about the Companions, given their high status. The passage then notes that the jurists derived rulings from Ali's conduct, and explains why Ali's actions are authoritative: the Prophet may have taught others, but those rulings came to light only through Ali; Ali's actions were known to all and no one objected, which implies their correctness; and the Prophet's statement 'We fight according to the revelation, and you fight according to the interpretation' proves that Ali's actions were based on knowledge and in harmony with the Sharia.

Key Terms
فقهاء الأمة
معاملة علي
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ali acted based on personal opinion without prophetic guidance.
Correction: The passage shows that the Prophet endorsed his interpretation, so his actions are divinely sanctioned.

Checkpoint
What does the Prophet's statement 'We fight according to the revelation, and you fight according to the interpretation' indicate about Ali's actions?
A) Ali was fighting against the Prophet.
B) Ali's actions were based on personal opinion and could be wrong.
C) Ali's actions were done with knowledge and in accordance with the Sharia.
D) The hadith is not authentic.

Key Takeaway
Ali's conduct is authoritative because it was publicly known, unchallenged, and divinely endorsed by the Prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a practice you currently reject was done openly by Ali without any objection from the community, would that change your stance? What would it take for you to accept it—are you willing to set aside your biases and trust the evidence presented in this passage?
?
The Prophet said Ali fights according to interpretation, implying that his actions are based on knowledge and in line with Sharia. Do you examine your own interpretations against the example of the early community, or do you assume your understanding is automatically correct? How will you respond when your personal opinion conflicts with a known practice of the Companions?

Method of Learning
beginner
4 min
The Principle of Specification in the Verse of Divine Election
Explanation of the verse about Adam and Noah and its application to Abu Bakr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of specification (takhṣīṣ) is applied to Quranic verses of divine election, demonstrating that certain preferences are limited to specific contexts without implying absolute superiority.

Core Idea
Quranic verses of election are often specific to their context, not absolute rankings.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabṣirat al-Adilla addresses a key methodological principle: how to interpret verses that seem to give preference to certain prophets. The author uses the example of the verse 'Indeed, Allah chose Adam and Noah... over the worlds' to show that such 'election' is specific to the people of their time, not including Prophet Muhammad. He then applies this reasoning to the virtues of the Companions, particularly Abu Bakr.

Guided Path
1
Read the primary texts
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the author uses a narration and a Quranic verse to build his argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Analyze the narration interpretation
The author interprets a narration about a person eating with the Prophet as specific to that situation. Ask: Why would it be a need-based preference rather than a general rank?
3
Examine the Quranic example
The verse from Surah Al Imran (3:33) is cited. The author argues that 'over the worlds' cannot include Prophet Muhammad, so it means 'over the worlds of their time.' How does this support specification?
This is the core proof.
4
Understand the analogy
The author concludes that the virtues of Abu Bakr are also specific. Reflect on how this principle defends the status of the Prophet while honoring the Companions.
This connects to broader kalam discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يأكل معي، وكذا هو تأويل ما رووا أي بأحب خلقك إليك أن يأكل معي، ولعله كان أحب الخلق أن يأكل مع النبي لمساس حاجته في تلك الحالة، على أنه كان أحب خلقه إليه بعد من تقدمه من الخلفاء الراشدين فكان المراد منه الخصوص عرف ذلك بما تقدم من الدلائل، ومثل هذا جائز قال الله تعالى: إِنَّ اللَّهَ اصْطَفَىٰ آدَمَ وَنُوحًا وَآلَ إِبْرَاهِيمَ وَآلَ عِمْرَانَ عَلَى الْعَالَمِينَ ولم يكن هؤلاء مصطفين على نبينا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم فكانت الآية مخصوصة، وكان معناه على عالمي زمانهم فكذا هذا
He eats with me, and likewise is the interpretation of what they narrated: 'the most beloved of Your creation to You that he eat with me.' And perhaps he was the most beloved of creation to eat with the Prophet due to the need in that situation, while being the most beloved of His creation to Him after those who preceded him among the Rightly Guided Caliphs. So the intended meaning was specificity, as known from previous proofs. And such is permissible. Allah Almighty said: 'Indeed, Allah chose Adam and Noah and the family of Abraham and the family of Imran over the worlds.' And these were not chosen over our Prophet Muhammad, peace be upon him. So the verse was specific, and its meaning was 'over the worlds of their time.' So too is this.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the principle of specification (takhṣīṣ) of Quranic verses. The author explains that a narration about a person being the most beloved to eat with the Prophet is interpreted as specific to that situation, not a general rank. He then cites the verse from Surah Al Imran (3:33) about divine election, noting that it does not include the Prophet Muhammad over others, so it must mean 'over the worlds of their time.' By analogy, the virtues attributed to Abu Bakr are also specific and do not contradict the general precedence of the Prophet. The author follows the order: first, the interpretation of a specific narration; second, the Quranic verse as a parallel; third, the conclusion that such specification is valid and applies here.

Key Terms
تخصيص (specification)
اصطفاء (election)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse 'Indeed, Allah chose Adam and Noah... over the worlds' gives absolute preference to those mentioned over all prophets, including Muhammad.
Correction: The author clarifies that the verse is specific to 'the worlds of their time' and does not include Prophet Muhammad.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about the verse 'Indeed, Allah chose Adam and Noah... over the worlds'?
A) It means they are superior to all prophets including Muhammad.
B) It is specific to the people of their time and does not include Muhammad.
C) It applies only to the family of Imran.
D) It has no application to the discussion of Abu Bakr.

Key Takeaway
The principle of specification allows us to reconcile verses that seem to rank prophets without contradicting the absolute superiority of the Prophet Muhammad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'Allah chose Adam and Noah... over the worlds,' what does this lesson teach you about the danger of taking Quranic verses out of context? Where in your own understanding of Islam might you be making similar errors by ignoring the principle of specification?
?
The author uses specification to protect the unique status of Prophet Muhammad while still acknowledging the virtues of others. Is there a person or group in your life whose virtues you have been reluctant to acknowledge because you feared it would diminish someone else's status? How does this principle free you from that false competition?
?
If you truly believed that every verse and hadith must be understood within its specific context, what would change about the way you argue about religious matters? Specifically, what is one verse or hadith you have used in a generalized way that you now need to revisit?
